# 黄帝八十一难经纂图句解

**"Huangdi's *Eighty-One Difficult Questions* with Illustrated Commentary and Verse Explanations**

**Title:** *Huangdi’s Eighty-One Difficult Questions* (Illustrated Commentary and Verse Explanations).
**Authored by:** Li Si of the Song Dynasty.
**Seven volumes.**
**Source of the base text:** *Zhengtong Daozang* (正统道藏), **Daoist Canon Section: Taixuan (太玄部).**

**Collated editions:**
- *Nanjing Jizhu* (难经集注) – Shadow-printed edition by the People’s Health Publishing House (hereafter referred to as *Jizhu*).
- *Nanjing Benyi* (难经本义) – Edition from *Gujin Yitong Zhengmai Quan Shu* (古今医统正脉全书) (hereafter referred to as *Benyi*).

**Volume One of the *Yellow Emperor’s Eighty-One Difficulties* Compiled and Annotated by Lu Guo and Qin Yue Ren, with Commentary by Li Xi and Fan Zi from Linchuan**

**Chapter 1: The *Pulse Ox’s* Diagnostic Observations**
**Difficulty 1:** The first verse expounds upon the difficulty and inquires: All twelve meridians possess the **Pulse Ox** (*Tunxi*). The term *jing* (經) means pathway, constancy—the constant pathways through which **meridian-qi** flows. The three **Yin** and three **Yang** meridians of the hand, and the three **Yin** and three **Yang** meridians of the foot:

- The **Heart**, the hand **Lesser Yin** meridian;
- The **Small Intestine**, the hand **Greater Yang** meridian;
- The **Liver**, the foot **Jue Yin** meridian;
- The **Gallbladder**, the foot **Lesser Yang** meridian;
- The **Kidneys**, the foot **Lesser Yin** meridian;
- The **Bladder**, the foot **Greater Yang** meridian;
- The **Lungs**, the hand **Greater Yin** meridian;
- The **Large Intestine**, the hand **Yangming** meridian;
- The **Spleen**, the foot **Greater Yin** meridian;
- The **Stomach**, the foot **Yangming** meridian;
- The **Pericardium**, the hand **Jue Yin** meridian;
- The **Triple Burner**, the hand **Lesser Yang** meridian.

Among the twelve meridians above, all possess the **Pulse Ox** (*Tunxi*) with manifest movement. Yet only the **Cun Kou** (寸口) is taken to determine the laws of life, death, auspice, and inauspice for the five **Zang** (藏) and six **Fu** (府). What does this mean?

At a distance of one *cun* (寸) from the **Yu Ji** (魚際) bone, it is named **Cun Kou** (寸口). The **Liver**, **Heart**, **Spleen**, **Lungs**, and **Kidneys** are the five **Zang** (藏); the **Gallbladder**, **Stomach**, **Large Intestine**, **Small Intestine**, **Bladder**, and **Triple Burner** are the six **Fu** (府). Yet only the **Cun Kou** (寸口) of one meridian is used to judge whether the central **Zang** (藏) and **Fu** (府) are in life or death, auspice or inauspice. How is this explained?

The **Cun Kou** (寸口) is the grand confluence of the meridians, where the **hand Greater Yin** meridian pulses. This refers specifically to the **Cun Kou** (寸口) of the right hand, the **hand Greater Yin** meridian, which converges at **Da Yuan** (大渊). **Da Yuan** (大渊) is the name of an acupoint, a critical junction where the twelve meridians meet. It is the place where the **hand Greater Yin** meridian’s **Pulse Ox** (*Tunxi*) manifests.

When a person exhales, the **Pulse Ox** (*Tunxi*) travels three *cun* (寸). Exhalation corresponds to **Yang** emergence; between one exhalation, the **Greater Yang**, **Lesser Yang**, and **Yangming** meridians advance, totaling three *cun*. When a person inhales, the **Pulse Ox** (*Tunxi*) travels three *cun*. Inhalation follows **Yin** entry; between one inhalation, the **Greater Yin**, **Lesser Yin**, and **Jue Yin** meridians advance, totaling three *cun*. With exhalation and inhalation settled, the **Pulse Ox** (*Tunxi*) travels six *cun*. The rise and fall of **Heaven** and **Earth**’s **Yin** and **Yang**, like the waxing and waning of the sun and moon, are thus determined. The **Pulse Ox** (*Tunxi*) travels twenty-five *cun* during exhalation and twenty-five *cun* during inhalation, completing fifty degrees in one cycle around the body. The **Cun Kou** (寸口) is the starting and ending point of the five **Zang** (藏) and six **Fu** (府), hence the law is taken from the **Cun Kou** (寸口).

The **Cun Kou** (寸口) begins at the right hand’s **Lung** meridian. From the **Lungs** and **Large Intestine**, it proceeds to the **Stomach** and **Spleen**; from the **Spleen**, it moves to the **Heart** and **Small Intestine**; from the **Small Intestine**, it advances to the **Bladder** and **Kidneys**; from the **Kidneys**, it travels to the **Pericardium** and **Triple Burner**; and from the **Triple Burner**, it returns to the **Gallbladder** and **Liver**, where the cycle ends—and then begins anew at the **Lungs**. The **Nutritive Qi** (*Honor-Wei*) and **Defensive Qi** (*Wei*) circulate twenty-five degrees along **Yang** and twenty-five degrees along **Yin**, completing one cycle. **Nutritive Qi** (*Honor-Wei*) belongs to **blood** and **Yin**, circulating within the meridians; **Defensive Qi** (*Wei*) belongs to **qi** and **Yang**, circulating outside the meridians. The **qi** and **blood** are awake while traversing the body and rest within the **Zang** (藏), ceaselessly cycling day and night without pause. In two hundred seventy breaths, the **Pulse Ox** (*Tunxi*) travels sixteen *zhang* two *chi* (丈二尺), corresponding to two *ke* (刻) of the **water clock** (*Lou Shu*). In thirteen thousand five hundred breaths, the **Pulse Ox** (*Tunxi*) travels eight hundred ten *zhang* (丈), corresponding to one hundred *ke* (刻) of the **water clock** (*Lou Shu*). Twenty-five degrees of **Yang** and twenty-five degrees of **Yin** make up the measure of the **water clock** (*Lou Shu*). One cycle corresponds to one full day and night. Thus, fifty cycles converge again at the **hand Greater Yin** meridian.

Thus, the diagnostic method of **pulse examination** relies on the **Cun Kou** (寸口), by which one discerns life and death, fortune and misfortune.

**Second Difficulty:** The second inquiry elaborates and asks: What are the **Cun** and **Chi** (尺寸) of the pulse?

The pulse has a **Chi** (尺) section and a **Cun** (寸) section—how does this distinction arise?

Indeed, the **Cun** and **Chi** are the pulse’s great confluence. The **Cun** belongs to the **Yang** region, marking the convergence of **Yang**’s essence; the **Chi** belongs to the **Yin** region, marking the convergence of **Yin**’s essence. This is where the **Pulse Ox** (*Tunxi*) gathers most profoundly.

From the **Guan** (关) to the **Chi** is the **Chi** region, governed by **Yin**; from the **Guan** to the **Yu Ji** (鱼际) is the **Cun Kou** region, governed by **Yang**. The **Guan** is the pass between **Yin** and **Yang**—before the **Guan** is **Yang**, after the **Guan** is **Yin**.

From the **Guan** to the **Chi Ze** (尺泽) acupoint, spanning one **Chi**, lies within the **Chi** region, governed by **Yin**. Though the **Chi** region’s pulse appears only one **Cun** long, it is called **Chi** because it is the root.

From the **Guan** to the **Yu Ji** bone is the **Cun** region, governed by **Yang**. The **Cun Kou** measures one **Cun**, yet the **Pulse Ox** manifests nine **Fen** (分).

Thus, **Chi** is divided into **Cun**, and **Cun** is divided into **Fen**. By cross-referencing **Chi** and **Cun**, one discerns the **Cun Kou** and **Chi** regions. Hence, **Yin** occupies one **Cun** within the **Chi** region, while **Yang** occupies nine **Fen** within the **Cun** region.

**Yin** is paired (even), Earth’s number is ten, so **Yin** occupies one **Cun** within the **Chi** region. **Yang** is odd, Heaven’s number is nine, so **Yang** occupies nine **Fen** within the **Cun** region.

Thus, the **Chi** and **Cun** mark the beginning and end—one **Cun** and nine **Fen**—hence the term **Chi Cun** (尺寸).

**Yang qi** originates in the **Chi** and moves to the **Cun**; **Yin qi** originates in the **Cun** and moves to the **Chi**. Therefore, the law states that **Yang qi** first arises at **Lì Chun** (立春), ascending to the **Mang Zhong** (芒种) node—its count is nine, with the Three **Yangs** reigning ahead, corresponding to the nine **Fen** within the **Cun** as floating.

At the **Xia Zhi** (夏至) node, its **qi** descends, reaching **Lì Dong** (立冬) to conclude—its count is ten, with the Three **Yins** reigning behind, corresponding to one **Cun** within the **Chi** as deep.

This is the **Yin** within **Yang**’s beginning and end.

**Yin qi** again arises from **Lì Qiū** (立秋), descending to the **Dong Zhi** (冬至) node—its count is ten. After **Dong Zhi**, it follows the **Shao Yang** upward, reaching the **Lì Xia** (立夏) node—its count is nine.

This is the **Yang** within **Yin**’s beginning and end.

The **Chi** and **Cun** each mark their own beginning and end—one **Cun** and nine **Fen**—hence the term **Chi Cun**.

**Third Difficulty:** [Same as before.]

The pulse has **excess** (太過). The pulse beats in **excess** at their designated positions. There is **deficiency**. There is **deficiency** at their designated positions. There is **Yin-Yang counteraction**. There is **Yin-qi counteracting the Yang pulse**, and there is **Yang pulse counteracting the Yin position**.

There is **covering** (覆). The **covering pulse** travels from the *Guan* (關) to the *Chi Ze* (尺澤). There is **overflow** (溢). The **overflowing pulse** swells at the *Cun Kou* (寸口), extending upward to the *Yu Ji* (魚際) bone.

There is **closure** (關) and **repulsion** (格). *Guan* means **closure**; *ge* means **repulsion**. When **Yin-qi is excessive**, **Yang-qi cannot circulate**, hence it is called **closure** (鬲). When **Yang-qi is excessive**, **Yin-qi cannot circulate**, hence it is called **closure** (關). When both **Yin and Yang are excessive**, neither can circulate properly, hence it is called **closure and repulsion** (關鬲). What does this mean? How is this explained?

Before the *Guan* (關), it is the movement of **Yang**. **Yang-qi** originates at the *Chi* (尺) and moves to the *Cun* (寸). The region before the *Guan* is called *Cun Kou* (寸口), where **Yang-qi** moves and acts. The pulse should be **nine *fen* (分) and floating**. Since **Yang** is an odd number, its count is nine, and the **Yang pulse is floating**, hence the *Cun Kou* pulse is **nine *fen* and floating**.

If it **exceeds**, it is called **excess** (太過). If it exceeds nine *fen*, it extends to **one *cun* (寸)**. The *Cun* pulse is originally floating, and if it becomes additionally **full and large**, it is an **excess pulse**. If it **lacks**, it is called **deficiency** (不及). If it does not reach nine *fen*, it may be **eight, seven, or six *fen***. The *Cun* pulse is originally floating; if pressed lightly, it diminishes and becomes small, which is **deficiency**. If it overflows upward to *Yu Ji* (魚際), it is called **overflow** (溢). The pulse swells at *Yu Ji* bone, **floating and large**, which is **Yang overflow**. If it is floating yet diminished and small, it is **Yang deficiency**. When **Yang is deficient**, **Yin invades and counteracts**, resulting in **Yin overflow**. This is called **external closure and internal repulsion** (外關內格). **External closure** means the internal pulse cannot emerge, which is **deficiency**, also called **Yin counteracting the pulse**. **Internal repulsion** means the external pulse cannot enter, which is **excess**, also called **overflowing pulse**. This is the pulse of **Yin counteraction**, the pulse where **Yin-qi counteracts the Yang position**.

After the *Guan* (關), it is the movement of **Yin**. **Yin-qi** originates at the *Cun* (寸) and moves to the *Chi* (尺). The region after the *Guan* is the *Chi* (尺) position, where **Yin-qi** moves and acts. The pulse should be **one *cun* and deep**. Since **Yin** is an even number, its count is ten, and the **Yin pulse is deep**, hence the *Chi* pulse is **one *cun* and deep**.

If it **exceeds**, it is called **excess** (太過). If it exceeds one *cun*, it may extend to **one and two, three, four, or five *fen***. The *Chi* pulse is originally deep; if pressed heavily, it becomes additionally **full and large**, which is **Yin excess**. If it **lacks**, it is called **deficiency** (不及). If it does not reach one *cun*, it may be **eight, seven, or six *fen***. The *Yin* pulse is originally deep and **soft**; if pressed heavily, it diminishes and becomes small, which is **Yin deficiency**. If it descends into the *Chi* as **covering** (覆), the **covering pulse** travels from the *Guan* to the *Chi Ze* (尺澤) acupoint. If the pulse is seen at one *cun*, it is **hidden and not visible**; when pressed deeply, it diminishes and becomes small, which is **Yin deficiency**. When **Yin is deficient**, **Yang invades and counteracts**, called **Yang covering** (陽覆), also **internal closure and external repulsion** (內關外格). **Internal closure** means the external pulse cannot enter, which is **Yin deficiency**, and **Yang invades and counteracts**. **External repulsion** means the internal pulse cannot emerge, which is **Yin excess**, also called **Yang covering**. This is the pulse of **Yang counteraction**, the pulse where **Yang-qi counteracts the Yin position**. Hence it is called **covering and overflow** (覆溢). When all **Yin is insufficient**, **Yang invades and counteracts**, resulting in **covering**. When all **Yang is insufficient**, **Yin invades and counteracts**, resulting in **overflow**. These are the **true藏 pulses**; if seen alone in their original form, they indicate **imminent death**. If seen in **partial Yin or partial Yang**, it also indicates **death**.

**Fourth Difficulty:** What is the **Yin-Yang law of the pulse**? The pulse has **Yin and Yang**; what are their regulations?

When exhaling, the **Heart and Lungs** are engaged. The **Heart and Lungs** are above the *Ge* (鬲), the **Yang** within the **Zang** (藏). Exhalation follows **Yang**, so the exhaled breath emerges from the **Heart and Lungs**. When inhaling, the **Kidneys and Liver** are engaged. The **Liver and Kidneys** are below the *Ge* (鬲), the **Yin** within the **Zang** (藏). Inhalation follows **Yin**, so the inhaled breath enters the **Kidneys and Liver**. Between exhalation and inhalation, the **Spleen** receives the flavors of grains. The pulse lies in the middle. The **Spleen** is the central state; above it are the **Heart and Lungs**, below it are the **Kidneys and Liver**, so the pulse between exhalation and inhalation belongs to the **Spleen**. The **Spleen and Stomach** are the **granaries and storehouses**, responsible for receiving the **Five Grains**. *Gu* (谷) means **grain**; *ku* (空) means **empty**. The **Spleen** receives the flavors of grains as the foundation for exhalation and inhalation.

The **floating pulse** is **Yang**. The floating pulse travels between the **skin and blood vessels**, above the **muscles**, which is the **Yang pulse**. The **deep pulse** is **Yin**. The deep pulse travels along the **tendons and beside the bones**, which is the **Yin pulse**. Hence it is called **Yin and Yang**. In summary, the pulse is **floating as Yang and deep as Yin**.

How do we distinguish between the **Heart and Lungs**, both of which are floating? The **Heart** is like **fire**, externally luminous, so the **Heart pulse is floating**. The **Lung** is like **metal**, positioned highest, so the **Lung pulse is floating**. How do we differentiate between the two, both being floating? If it is **floating, large, and dispersed**, it is the **Heart**. A **large pulse** is the **Zang pulse**; a **dispersed pulse** is the **Fu pulse**. If it is **floating, short, and hesitant**, it is the **Lung**. If it does not reach its proper position, it is **short**; if it is **fine and slow**, with **difficult movement** and **occasional cessation**, it is **hesitant**. A **floating, short, and hesitant pulse** is the **Lung pulse**—**short** refers to the **Zang**, and **hesitant** refers to the **Fu**.

How do we distinguish between the **Liver and Kidneys**, both of which are deep? The **Liver and Kidneys** are below the *Ge* (鬲), so their pulses are all deep. How can they be differentiated?

If it is **firm and long**, it is the **Liver**. The **Liver** belongs to **Wood**; **Wood** grows from the earth, hence its **firmness** is understood. Its **branches and leaves** extend to the sky, hence its **length**. **Firm** refers to the **Zang pulse**; **long** refers to the **Fu pulse**.

When pressed, it feels **soft and yielding**; when the finger is lifted, it returns **firm**—this is the **Kidney pulse**. The **Kidney** belongs to **Water**; its nature is **soft**, for **Water**’s essence is externally yielding—when pressed, it is **soft**. Yet **Water**’s essence is internally **firm**; when lifted, it returns **real**. **Soft** refers to the **Zang pulse**; **firm** refers to the **Fu pulse**.

The **Spleen** is the **central realm**. The **Spleen** belongs to **Earth**, occupying the center. **Realm** (*zhou*) carries the meaning of **prefecture and county**; **central realm** (*zhōng zhou*) carries the meaning of **Central Plain**. Thus, its pulse lies in the **middle**. Above it are the **Heart and Lung**; below it are the **Liver and Kidney**, while the **Spleen pulse** resides between them. This follows the **principles of yin and yang**.

The **Heart** is a **yang Zang**, positioned in the **upper burner**, with **yang dwelling in yang**, hence it is **yang within yang**. The **Lung** is a **yin Zang**, positioned in the **upper burner**, with **yin dwelling in yang**, hence it is **yin within yang**. The **Kidney** is a **yin Zang**, positioned in the **lower burner**, with **yin dwelling in yin**, hence it is **yin within yin**. The **Liver** is a **yang Zang**, positioned in the **middle burner**, with **yang dwelling in yin**, hence it is **yin within yang**. The **Spleen** is a **yin Zang**, positioned in the **middle burner**, with **Great Yin dwelling in yin**, making it **the utmost yin within yin**.

The pulses manifest as:
- **One yin and one yang**,
- **One yin and two yangs**,
- **One yin and three yangs**;

Or as:
- **One yang and one yin**,
- **One yang and two yins**,
- **One yang and three yins**.

Thus are spoken the **pulses of yin and yang**. Thus are spoken the **pulses of yin and yang**.

At the *cùnkǒu* [寸口], are there six pulses all moving together?
When inquiring within the *cùnkǒu*, are there six pulses all visibly manifesting?
Indeed: speaking of this—
Speaking of these six pulses.
It is not that six pulses move together.
It is not that these six pulses
all move at the *cùnkǒu*.
Rather, it refers to the pulse arriving as **floating, deep, long, short, smooth, or rough**.

Drawing upon the three *yin* and three *yang* pulses to correspond with the six *qi* [六气]:
Floating, smooth, and long belong to the three *yang* pulses;
Deep, short, and rough belong to the three *yin* pulses.

Floating is *yang*.
Floating is the *Shao Yang* pulse.
Smooth is *yang*.
Smooth is the *Yang Ming* pulse.
Long is *yang*.
Long is the *Tai Yang* pulse.
Deep is *yin*.
Deep is the *Shao Yin* pulse.
Short is *yin*.
Short is the *Jue Yin* pulse.
Rough is *yin*.
Rough is the *Tai Yin* pulse.

What is called **"one *yin* and one *yang**"** refers to the pulse arriving as deep yet smooth.
One *yin* is the **rough pulse**.
One *yang* is the **smooth pulse**.
If the pulse appears at the left hand’s *chi* [尺] department, this is the Kidney and Bladder—**the *Zang-Fu* [藏府] exterior-interior harmony in order**.
If it appears at the left *cùnkǒu*, then it is **a disorder of inversion**.

**"One *yin* and two *yang*"** refers to the pulse arriving as deep, smooth, and long.
One *yin* is the **rough pulse**.
Two *yang* are the **smooth and long pulses**.
This pulse appearing in the *yin* department is **the *yang* descending to dominate the *yin* department**.

**"One *yin* and three *yang*"** refers to the pulse arriving as floating, smooth, and long, yet occasionally deep.
One *yin* is the **deep pulse**.
Three *yang* are the **floating, smooth, and long pulses**.
Already at the *chi* department, it is floating, smooth, and long, yet again occasionally deep—this is **a hidden *yin* within *yang***.

What is called **"one *yang* and one *yin*"** refers to the pulse arriving as floating yet rough.
One *yang* is the **floating pulse**.
One *yin* is the **rough pulse**.
A floating and rough pulse belongs to the Lung—**it should appear at the right hand’s *cùnkǒu*, marking the original department’s *yin-yang* harmony in order**.
If it appears at the *guan* [关], then it is **a disorder of inversion**.

**"One *yang* and two *yin*"** refers to the pulse arriving as long yet deep and rough.
One *yang* is the **long pulse**.
Two *yin* are the **deep and rough pulses**.
The pulse residing in the *yang* department yet revealing *yin* characteristics—this is **blood and *qi* both depleted, *yin* dominating *yang***.

**"One *yang* and three *yin*"** refers to the pulse arriving as deep, rough, and short, yet occasionally floating.
One *yang* is the **floating pulse**.
Three *yin* are the **rough, deep, and short pulses**.
Already at the *cùnkǒu* department, it is deep, rough, and short, yet again occasionally floating—this is **a hidden *yang* within *yin***.

Each is named according to the channel’s location—**whether the disease is inverted or orderly**.
Each is observed through the twelve channels’ locations, examining the six *qi*’s [六气] changes in spring, summer, autumn, and winter, thus knowing whether the disease is inverted or orderly.

---
The **Five Difficulties** say:
*The pulse has lightness and heaviness—what does this mean?*
When diagnosing the pulse, there is lightness and heaviness—how does this correspond to *yin-yang* elevation and depression? Indeed:
At first, when holding the pulse,
First observe the Lung pulse as an example.
Suppose when initially diagnosing the right hand’s *cùnkǒu* Lung pulse,
If it weighs like **three mung beans** and aligns with the skin and fur—this is the Lung department.
*Mung beans* are beans.
The Lung governs skin and fur; like three beans in weight, between the skin and fur—this is the Lung pulse.
When diagnosing the Lung pulse, one must use a **light hand** to press.
If it weighs like **six mung beans** and aligns with the blood vessels—this is the Heart department.
The Heart governs blood vessels; next to the Lung, like six beans in weight.
When diagnosing the Heart pulse, one must use a **moderately heavy hand** to press.
If it weighs like **nine mung beans** and aligns with the muscles—this is the Spleen department.
The Spleen governs muscles; thus, after the Heart, like nine beans in weight.
When diagnosing the Spleen pulse, one must use a **neither-light-nor-heavy hand** to press.
If it weighs like **twelve mung beans** and aligns with the tendons and bones—this is the Liver department.
The Liver governs tendons; again below the Spleen, like twelve beans in weight.
When diagnosing the Liver pulse, one must use a **moderately heavy hand** to press.
Pressing until the bone, then lifting the finger swiftly—this is the Kidney department.
The Kidney governs bones; its pulse is rough, requiring a **heavy downward hand** to press until the bone.
Lifting the hand, if the pulse arrives swiftly and urgently—this is the Kidney pulse.
Thus it is said: **lightness and heaviness**.
The Heart and Lung are above the *ge* [鬲], the *Zang*’s *yang*—*yang* floats above, so it should be pressed lightly.
The Liver and Kidney are below the *ge*, the *Zang*’s *yin*—*yin* sinks below, so it should be pressed heavily.

---
The **Sixth Difficulty** says:
*The pulse has *yang* excess and *yin* deficiency, *yin* excess and *yang* deficiency—what does this mean?*
There are cases where the *yang* pulse is excessive and full while the *yin* pulse is depleted, and cases where the *yin* pulse is excessive and full while the *yang* pulse is depleted—how does this manifest? Indeed:
When floating, it is diminished.
Floating is the *yang* pulse; if floating yet diminished, it is *yang* deficiency.
The *yang* pulse is at *cùnkǒu*, originally floating and full; now, with a light hand, it is obtained as floating, yet further diminished and small—thus it is said: **the *yang* is deficient and insufficient**.
When deep, it is full and large.
Deep is the *yin* pulse; if deep yet full and large, it is *yin* excess.
The *yin* pulse is at the *chi* department, originally deep and slack; pressing heavily, it becomes even more full and large—this is **excessive *yin***.
Thus it is said: **the *yin* is excessive and the *yang* is deficient**.
Thus it is diagnosed as **the pulse of *yin* excess and *yang* deficiency**.
When deep, it is diminished.
Deep is the *yin* pulse; if deep yet diminished, it is *yin* deficiency.
The *yin* pulse is originally deep; pressing heavily, it becomes even more diminished and small—this is **the *yin* being deficient and insufficient**.
When floating, it is full and large.
Floating is the *yang* pulse; if floating yet full and large, it is *yang* excess.
The *yang* pulse is originally floating; pressing lightly, it becomes even more full and large—this is **excessive *yang***.
Thus it is said: **the *yang* is excessive and the *yin* is deficient**.
Thus it is diagnosed as **the pulse of *yang* excess and *yin* deficiency**.
This is the meaning of *yin-yang* deficiency and excess.
Again, summarizing the meaning of *yin-yang* deficiency and excess.

The **Seven Difficulties** state:

The *Classic* says that when *Shao-Yang* arrives, its pulse is alternately large and small, alternately short and long. *Shao-Yang* reigns in the first and second months; its *qi* is still weak and small, so its pulse advances and retreats irregularly, with no fixed size or length. When *Yang-Ming* arrives, the pulse is floating, large, and short. *Yang-Ming* reigns in the third and fourth months; its *qi* has just begun to sprout but is not yet flourishing, so the pulse comes floating, large, and short. When *Tai-Yang* arrives, the pulse is surging and long. *Tai-Yang* reigns in the fifth and sixth months; its *qi* is excessively strong, so the pulse comes surging, large, and long. When *Tai-Yin* arrives, the pulse is taut, large, and long. *Tai-Yin* reigns in the seventh and eighth months; it inherits the residual *yang* of summer, and *yin* *qi* is not yet strong, so the pulse comes taut, large, and long. When *Shao-Yin* arrives, the pulse is taut, fine, and faint. *Shao-Yin* reigns in the ninth and tenth months; *yang* *qi* declines while *yin* *qi* flourishes, so the pulse is taut, fine, and faint. When *Jue-Yin* arrives, the pulse is deep, short, and firm. *Jue-Yin* reigns in the eleventh and twelfth months; *yin* *qi* is strong, so the pulse comes deep, short, and firm.

Are these six pulses normal? — *Question:* These six pulses—do they belong to the harmonious pulse?
Or are they disease pulses? — *Question:* Do they belong to disease pulses?
*Answer:* They are all **Reigning Pulses**.

Which months do their *qi* reign for how many days?
— *Question:* For the three *yin* and three *yang*, which months do their *qi* reign, and for how many days?

*Answer:* After the Winter Solstice, the first *Jia-Zi* marks *Shao-Yang* reigning. The heavens complete a year through six cycles of sixty days. From the Winter Solstice onward, the first *Jia-Zi* marks the beginning of the new year’s *qi*—sometimes occurring at the start of *Xiao-Han*, sometimes after *Da-Han*. Thus, *Shao-Yang* *qi* has not yet emerged from the *yin* phase, so the pulse is alternately large, small, short, and long.

Next, the second *Jia-Zi* marks *Yang-Ming* reigning—this is the second cycle of *qi*; its warmth begins, but its *qi* is not yet strong, so the pulse comes floating, large, and short.

Next, the third *Jia-Zi* marks *Tai-Yang* reigning—this is the third cycle of *qi*; it is the peak of *yang*, so the pulse comes surging, large, and long.

Next, the fourth *Jia-Zi* marks *Tai-Yin* reigning—this is the fourth cycle of *qi*; it is the phase of summer heat and dampness, and autumn *qi* begins, riding upon the residual *yang* of summer, so the pulse comes taut, large, and long.

Next, the fifth *Jia-Zi* marks *Shao-Yin* reigning—this is the fifth cycle of *qi*; it is the phase of crisp clarity, so the pulse is taut, fine, and faint.

Next, the sixth *Jia-Zi* marks *Jue-Yin* reigning—this is the terminal cycle of *qi*; it is the phase of extreme *yin*, when water congeals like stone, so the pulse is deep, short, and firm.

Each reigns for sixty days. The three *yin* and three *yang* each reign for sixty days—six cycles of sixty days make three hundred sixty days, completing one year. Within a year, the three hundred sixty days are divided into four seasons.

This is the general principle of the reigning periods of the three *yin* and three *yang* according to the *Liu-Jia* cycles.

---
**The Eighth Difficulty:**

*Question:* Why does a person die when the *Cun-Kou* pulse is even and harmonious?

*Answer:* The twelve primary channels—[refer to the First Difficulty].

**The Eighth Difficulty:**

*Question:* Why does a person die when the *Cun-Kou* pulse is even and harmonious?

*Answer:* The *Cun-Kou* pulse reflects the state of the **root of life-giving *qi***—the twelve primary channels all depend on the **kidneys**. The kidneys are the source and foundation of the *qi* that generates and sustains the pulse. This "root of life-giving *qi*" refers to the fundamental origin of the twelve primary channels. The kidneys, as the root of life-giving *qi*, serve as the foundation for all twelve primary channels. The kidneys govern the **movement of *qi***—the dynamic *qi* between them is the **primal *qi*** inherited from one’s parents. Moreover, the **pulse of *qi* surging** originates from the space between the two kidneys. This is the source of the five *zàng* organs and six *fǔ* viscera, the root of the twelve primary channels. The five *zàng* and six *fǔ* are distributed into the twelve primary channels, with the kidneys as their ultimate foundation. The **gateway of respiration**—the kidneys flank the **Conception Vessel (任脈)** and **Governor Vessel (豚脈)**, extending upward to the throat, where breathing and exhalation occur, making it the gateway of respiration. The **origin of the Triple Burner (三焦)**—the human Triple Burner follows the **three primal *qi*** of Heaven and Earth, with the kidneys as its foundational source. It is also called the **Guardian of Pathogenic *Qi* (守邪之神)**. The left kidney corresponds to the **kidneys**, while the right governs the **Gate of Life (命門)**, where a spirit resides, preventing pathogenic *qi* from entering the **Zhi-Shi (志室)**. If pathogenic *qi* invades the **Zhi-Shi**, death ensues. **Zhi-Shi** is the name of an acupuncture point. Thus, *qi* is the root foundation of a person’s vitality. The movement of *qi* between the kidneys is the fundamental source of human life and vitality. If the root is severed, the branches and leaves wither. The **three *yin* and three *yang* channels of the hand** are the branches, while those of the **foot** are the roots. The **Chi (尺)** region represents a person’s foundational roots, and the *Cun-Kou* pulse reflects the branches and leaves. If the root of wood is severed, the wood dies; if the kidneys’ *qi* is severed, the person dies. When the *Cun-Kou* pulse is even and harmonious yet the person dies, it means the **internal life-giving *qi*** has been utterly depleted. The kidneys’ movement of *qi* is normally concealed within, but now the *Cun-Kou* pulse receives nourishment from ingested *gu* (谷氣, grain *qi*), though the pulse itself may appear even and harmonious. However, since the life-giving *qi* between the kidneys has already been severed, the twelve primary channels lose their support. Even if the *Cun-Kou* pulse remains even and harmonious, death still occurs.

---

**The Ninth Difficulty:**
*Question:* Why is there doubt that "灭" (*miè*) should be written as "臧" (*zāng*)?

**The Tenth Difficulty:**
*Question:* Why is "及" (*jí*) suspected to be a mistake for "反" (*fǎn*)?

**The Eleventh Difficulty:**
*Question:* Why should "一" (*yī*) likely be corrected to "二" (*èr*)?

---
**The Yellow Emperor’s *Eighty-One Difficulties* (黃帝八十一難經纂圖句解卷之二)**
*Compiled and annotated by Lu Guo, a native of Qin and Yue, and revised by Li Xiangzi, a native of Linchuan.*
**The Eighth Difficulty:**

When the *Cun-Kou* pulse is even and harmonious yet death occurs, what does this mean?
Indeed, the twelve primary channels—
*Refer to the First Difficulty.*

---

**The Ninth Difficulty:**
*Question:* How can one distinguish the diseases of the *zàng* organs and *fǔ* viscera?

*Answer:* The *zàng* organs and *fǔ* viscera’s diseases are discerned by examining the pulse’s **frequency (數)** and **slowness (迟)**. If the pulse is rapid, it indicates the six *fǔ* viscera are diseased. If the pulse is slow, it signifies the five *zàng* organs are afflicted. A rapid pulse denotes **heat (熱)**—it is a *yang* pulse, indicating symptoms of excess *yang* heat. A slow pulse denotes **cold (寒)**—it is a *yin* pulse, indicating symptoms of *yin* deficiency. All *yang* pulses relate to heat. When *yang* *qi* is disordered, the pulse becomes rapid, thus all *yang* pulses are associated with heat. All *yin* pulses relate to cold. When *yin* *qi* is deficient, the pulse becomes slow, thus all *yin* pulses are associated with cold. Therefore, one can distinguish the diseases of the *zàng* organs and *fǔ* viscera through these pulse variations.

---

**The Tenth Difficulty:**
*Question:* What does it mean when one pulse shows ten variations?

*Answer:* It refers to the **five pathogenic *qi***—*xū* (虚, vacuous), *shí* (實, solid), *zhèng* (正, upright), *wēi* (微, subtle), and *zéi* (贼, thief)—interacting with the **relative strength of *yin* and *yang*** (just and yielding). The five pathogenic *qi* arise when these interactions occur at their **original positions**, revealing **foreign pulses**. Each *zàng* organ has its own exterior and interior connections, and they **mutually invade** in sequence, resulting in one pulse manifesting ten variations. These ten variations correspond to the **cyclical dominance of the Five Phases (五行)**. Pathogenic *qi* refers to any irregularity not originating from the body’s inherent *qi*, but instead **externally disrupting** it to cause disease.

For instance, if the **heart pulse** is excessively taut and urgent, it is due to **liver pathogenic *qi*** invading the heart. The sages use the heart as an example for one *zàng* organ. In summer, the heart pulse should be **floating, large, and diffuse**, but if it instead becomes taut and urgent, it indicates **liver pathogenic *qi*** invading the heart. The liver is the mother, the heart the child; wood generates fire, and the mother invades the child—this is called **vacuous pathogenic *qi***.

If the heart pulse is slightly urgent, it is due to **gallbladder pathogenic *qi*** invading the **small intestine**. The small intestine is the heart’s *fǔ* viscus; the gallbladder is the liver’s *fǔ* viscus. When the heart pulse is slightly urgent, it indicates **gallbladder pathogenic *qi*** invading the small intestine.

If the heart pulse is excessively large, it is due to **heart pathogenic *qi*** itself invading the heart. Though the heart pulse may appear **full and strong**, it should fundamentally rely on **stomach *qi***. If the stomach *qi* is absent, the pulse becomes excessively large—this is called **upright pathogenic *qi*** of the primary channel itself, hence the term **self-invasion**.

If the heart pulse is slightly large, it is due to **small intestine pathogenic *qi*** itself invading the small intestine. A slightly large heart pulse indicates self-disease of the small intestine.

If the heart pulse is excessively relaxed and slow, it is due to **spleen pathogenic *qi*** invading the heart. A relaxed pulse belongs to the spleen; if the heart pulse is excessively relaxed and slow, it signifies fire as the mother and earth as the child, with the child invading the mother—this is called **solid pathogenic *qi***, as spleen pathogenic *qi* invades the heart pulse.

If the heart pulse is slightly relaxed, it is due to **stomach pathogenic *qi*** invading the small intestine. When lightly palpating the heart region and encountering a slightly relaxed pulse, it indicates **stomach pathogenic *qi*** invading and overwhelming the small intestine.

If the heart pulse is excessively rough and choppy, it is due to **lung pathogenic *qi*** invading the heart. A rough pulse belongs to the lungs; if the heart region exhibits a rough pulse, it signifies metal overwhelming fire—this is called **subtle pathogenic *qi***.

If the heart pulse is slightly rough, it is due to **large intestine pathogenic *qi*** invading the small intestine. When encountering a slightly rough pulse in the heart region, it indicates **large intestine pathogenic *qi*** invading and overwhelming the small intestine.

If the heart pulse is excessively deep, it is due to **kidney pathogenic *qi*** invading the heart. A deep pulse belongs to the kidneys; if the heart pulse is excessively deep, it signifies water subduing fire—this is called **thief pathogenic *qi***.

If the heart pulse is slightly deep, it is due to **bladder pathogenic *qi*** invading the small intestine. When palpating the heart region with a heavy touch and encountering a slightly deep pulse, it indicates **bladder pathogenic *qi*** invading and overwhelming the small intestine.

Each of the five *zàng* organs has its own relative strength of *yin* and *yang*. The *yin* and *yang* interactions within the five *zàng* organs are exemplified by the heart, with the rest following similarly. Thus, one pulse can manifest ten variations. Within one pulse region, there are multiple variations, totaling sixty instances. The heart region alone exhibits five pathogenic *qi* interactions, resulting in ten variations.

---

**The Eleventh Difficulty:**
*Question:* The text states, *"A pulse that does not complete fifty beats before stopping is a flat pulse."* If one *zàng* organ completes fifty beats, then all five *zàng* organs complete two hundred fifty beats—this is called an **even pulse**. If a pulse does not complete fifty beats before stopping, it means **one *zàng* organ has no *qi***.

**The Eleventh Difficulty (continued):**
When a pulse stops, it means pressing reveals a cessation beneath the fingers—this is called *stopping*. If one *zàng* organ lacks *qi*, which organ is it? If one *zàng* organ lacks *qi*, which *zàng-fu* organ is it?

Now, when inhaling, *qi* follows *yin* and enters. Inhalation draws through the lips into the liver, so inhalation *qi* follows the liver and kidneys. The liver is below the *ge* (stomach cavity), and the kidneys are *yin*. When exhaling, *qi* follows *yang* and exits. Exhalation releases through the heart and lungs, so exhalation *qi* emerges from the heart and lungs. The heart and lungs are above the *ge*, hence *yang*. Now, if inhalation cannot reach the kidneys but stops at the liver, then generally, inhalation and exhalation should follow *yin* and *yang*, ascending and descending through the five *zàng* organs, maintaining equilibrium in a healthy person. But here, exhalation emerges from the heart and lungs, while inhalation reaches only the liver before returning—never reaching the kidneys. This indicates the kidneys’ primordial *qi*, inherited from one’s parents, has already dissipated. Thus, the pulse does not complete fifty beats before stopping, and death is imminent. Therefore, if one *zàng* organ lacks *qi*, it is the kidney *qi* that is exhausted first. Hence, if one *zàng* organ’s primordial *qi* is depleted, it is the kidney’s primordial *qi* that is first severed, causing the pulse to fail before fifty beats.

---

**The Twelfth Difficulty:**
The text states, *"The pulse of the five *zàng* organs has already ceased within."* The pulses of the liver and kidneys are below the *ge*, hence called *within*. If the internal pulse has ceased, the practitioner should instead reinforce the *external*. The heart and lungs are above the *ge*, hence called *external*. Those who use acupuncture or medicine should instead reinforce the heart and lungs with needles or prescriptions.

If the pulse of the five *zàng* organs has already ceased *externally*, the heart and lung pulses have died outside. The practitioner should instead reinforce the *internal*. Those who use acupuncture or medicine should instead reinforce the kidneys and liver with needles or prescriptions.

How does one distinguish between internal and external cessation? If the pulse of the five *zàng* organs has ceased within, the *qi* of the kidneys and liver has died internally. Yet the physician reinforces the heart and lungs instead—treating the wrong organs. If the pulse of the five *zàng* organs has ceased externally, the heart and lung *qi* has died outside. Yet the physician reinforces the kidneys and liver instead—treating the wrong organs.

When *yang* is exhausted, reinforce *yin*. The heart and lungs, being above the *ge*, belong to *yang*. If their external pulses cease, the skin withers and hair falls out. The kidneys and liver, being below the *ge*, belong to *yin*. The physician reinforces them instead. When *yin* is exhausted, reinforce *yang*. If the kidneys and liver’s internal pulses cease, the bones weaken and tendons fail. The physician reinforces the heart and lungs instead. This is called *treating excess with excess*—reinforcing a patient already in excess. *Treating deficiency with deficiency*—reinforcing a patient already in deficiency. *Diminishing what is insufficient*—harming a patient already weak. *Augmenting what is already excessive*—overloading a patient with surplus *qi*. Such deaths are the physician’s fault.

---

**The Thirteenth Difficulty:**
The text states, *"Seeing the color but not finding the corresponding pulse."* If one sees a color but instead finds the pulse of its *overcoming* cycle, death follows. If one finds the pulse of its *generating* cycle, illness follows. How should one correlate the patient’s color with their pulse?

Now, the five *zàng* organs each have a color, all visible on the face: the heart is red, the liver green, the spleen yellow, the lungs white, and the kidneys black. These colors should correspond to the pulses at *cùn-kǒu* (wrist) and *chǐ* (inner arm).

For example:
- If the color is green, the pulse should be taut and urgent—green is the liver’s color, and taut/urgent is the liver’s pulse. This is the liver’s color and pulse corresponding.
- If the color is red, the pulse should be floating, large, and scattered—red is the heart’s color, and floating/large/scattered is the heart’s pulse. This is the heart’s color and pulse corresponding.
- If the color is yellow, the pulse should be moderate and large—yellow is the spleen’s color, and moderate/large is the spleen’s pulse. This is the spleen’s color and pulse corresponding.
- If the color is white, the pulse should be floating, rough, and short—white is the lung’s color, and floating/rough/short is the lung’s pulse. This is the lung’s color and pulse corresponding.
- If the color is black, the pulse should be deep, soft, and slippery—black is the kidney’s color, and deep/soft/slippery is the kidney’s pulse. This is the kidney’s color and pulse corresponding.

This is how the five colors should correspond to their respective pulses.

If the pulse is rapid, the skin of the *chǐ* (inner arm) is also hot—rapid is the heart’s pulse, and the inner arm’s skin is hot. If the pulse is taut and urgent, the liver’s pulse, the inner arm’s skin is also taut. If the pulse is moderate, the spleen’s pulse, the inner arm’s skin is also slack. If the pulse is rough, the lung’s pulse, the inner arm’s skin is also rough. If the pulse is slippery, the kidney’s pulse, the inner arm’s skin is also slippery.

The five *zàng* organs each have their own sounds, colors, odors, and tastes, which should correspond to the pulses at *cùn-kǒu* and *chǐ*:
- The liver’s pulse is taut, its color green, its sound a sigh, its odor rank, and its taste sour;
- The heart’s pulse is resonant, its color red, its sound laughter, its odor burnt, and its taste bitter;
- The spleen’s pulse is moderate, its color yellow, its sound singing, its odor fragrant, and its taste sweet;

The lung’s pulse is rough, its color white, its sound weeping, its odor fishy, and its taste pungent;
The kidney’s pulse is deep, its color black, its sound groaning, its odor rotten, and its taste salty.
This is called correspondence.

When they do not correspond, it is illness.
Suppose the liver is diseased, yet the color is white, there is much weeping, a fondness for pungent flavors, and a liking for fishy odors—this is contrary.
When sound, color, odor, and taste all belong to the lung, metal overcoming wood, it is called *zéi-xié* (predatory evil). If they do not correspond, death follows.

Suppose the color is green, yet the pulse is floating, rough, and short—if it is also large and moderate, this is called *xiāng-shèng* (mutual overcoming).
The green color is of the liver;
The floating, rough, and short pulse is of the lung.
When the lung overcomes the liver, it is predatory evil.
If the pulse is large and moderate, it is the spleen’s pulse, and when the liver overcomes the spleen, it is a minor evil—thus called mutual overcoming.

If the pulse is floating, large, and scattered, or small and slippery, this is called *xiāng-shēng* (mutual generation).
The floating, large, and scattered pulse is of the heart;
The liver wood can generate heart fire.
The small and slippery pulse is of the kidney;
The kidney water can generate liver wood.

The Classics say: Knowing one [principle] makes one a lower artisan, knowing two makes one a middle artisan, knowing three makes one an upper artisan.
An upper artisan succeeds nine out of ten cases, a middle artisan succeeds eight out of ten, and a lower artisan succeeds six out of ten—this is what is meant.

The artisan is one who achieves success in all endeavors and treatments. An upper artisan knows the three laws of **color, pulse, and skin**, understanding the principles of mutual generation and mutual overcoming, hence treating diseases with nine successes out of ten. A middle artisan knows two laws, unable to fully grasp all three, so treats diseases with eight successes out of ten. A lower artisan knows only one law, unable to fully comprehend the complete methods, and thus treats only the present ailment, achieving six successes out of ten.

The Fourteen Difficulties state:
The Fourteenth Difficulty asks: What is meant by *pulse of excess* (至脉)?

Pulse has **excess pulse** (至脉) and **deficient pulse** (损脉). Now, regarding the **excess pulse**:
The pulse beats in response to the hand’s movement. With one breath, it arrives twice—this is called *level* (平). *Level* refers to the pulse’s rhythm being neither too strong nor too weak, completing two beats per breath. Again, four beats are called *harmonious pulse* (平和之豚息). Three beats are called *deviation from the norm* (离经). *Norm* (经) means *usual* or *constant*. A great deviation from the norm (大几经豚) occurs when, in one day and night, the pulse returns to the *hand Taiyin* (手太阴) channel, which is the starting point of inquiry. Now, with three beats per breath, the pulse travels four and a half inches; with three beats per inhalation, it travels four and a half inches; the total per breath is nine inches, exceeding the *level pulse* (平脉), and not conforming to its usual rhythm, hence called *deviation from the norm*—its pulse is coarse and large.

Five beats are called *consumption of essence* (夺精). Such a pulse is fivefold, for *flavor returns to form*, *form returns to qi*, and *qi returns to essence* (精). Now, with four beats per breath, form is stripped, qi is exhausted, and essence has nowhere to go—it is as if stolen. Six beats are called *life’s end* (命绝). Within one breath, the pulse reaches nearly twelve beats, and life ceases without vitality. This is the **excess pulse** (至脉). The original text says: This is the *pulse of death* (死之脉). I have amended it to say: This is the **excess pulse** (至脉), summarizing its nature.

What is *deficient* (损)? Regarding the *deficient pulse* (损脉):
With one breath and one beat, it is called *deviation from the norm* (离经). Earlier, the **excess pulse** (至脉) was called *deviation from the norm*, meaning the pulse exceeds half its usual rhythm. Now, the *deficient pulse* (损脉) is called *deviation from the norm*, meaning the pulse travels less than half its usual rhythm—one breath and one inhalation yield only two beats, also called *deviation from the norm*.

With two breaths and one beat, it is called *consumption of essence* (夺精). With two breaths and one beat, in one day and night, the pulse completes fewer than thirteen full cycles through the body, traveling only 225 feet and 5 inches. The person’s *qi is exhausted*, *blood is dried*, their *spirit is dimmed*, and their *complexion withers*—their essence is consumed as if stolen, hence called *consumption of essence*.

With three breaths and one beat, it is called *death* (死). With three breaths and one beat, the pulse travels only one and a half inches, completing in one day and night only 67 feet and 5 inches—less than five full cycles. Such a sign portends death.

With four breaths and one beat, it is called *life’s end* (命绝). With four breaths and one beat, in one day and night, the pulse completes fewer than four full cycles. The *qi is spent*, the *life is severed*, the *organs are ruined*, and the *spirit departs*—hence, life’s end.

This is the **deficient pulse** (损脉). Summarizing its nature:
The **excess pulse** (至脉) arises from below and moves upward. With fewer breaths and more beats, it is called *excess*. **Excess pulse** diseases originate in *Yang*, for *Yang qi* ascends from below, hence its severity affects both upper and lower regions, progressively damaging six organs.

The *deficient pulse* (损脉) arises from above and moves downward. With more breaths and fewer beats, it is called *deficient*. **Deficient pulse** diseases originate in *Yin*, for *Yin qi* descends from above, and its pulse weakens, failing to reach one or two beats.

How does the *deficient pulse* manifest as illness?
A *deficient pulse* in the first instance harms the *skin and fur* (皮毛), causing *skin to clump* and *fur to fall*. The first deficiency affects the *lung*, which governs *skin and fur*—hence, *skin clumps* and *fur falls*.

The second deficiency harms the *blood vessels* (血脉), making *blood and vessels sparse*, unable to nourish the *five organs and six entrails*. The second deficiency affects the *heart*, which governs *blood*—when *blood and vessels are depleted*, the *five organs and six entrails* cannot flourish.

The third deficiency harms the *muscles*, causing *muscles to wither and thin*, and *food not to benefit the flesh*. The third deficiency affects the *spleen*, the *storage of food*—when the *spleen is injured*, one should eat *spring chilled foods*, *summer cool gases*, and *winter warm foods*, adjusting to the seasonal *cold and warmth* to regulate diet, then the *spleen’s function will align*.

The fourth deficiency harms the *sinews*, making them *relaxed and unable to self-support*. The fourth deficiency affects the *liver*, which governs *sinews*—when *sinews are slack*, they cannot maintain or uphold the body.

The fifth deficiency harms the *bones*, causing *bone weakness* and inability to rise from the bed. The fifth deficiency affects the *kidney*, which governs *bones*—when *bones are dry* and *marrow is depleted*, the *vitality is lost*. *Weakness* (痿) means *lack of strength to rise*. The opposite is true for **excess pulse** (至脉) diseases. The original text states: *Excess pulse leads to withdrawal disease* (至於收病), but I have corrected it to *the opposite*.

In the case of the *deficient pulse*, if *bone weakness* prevents rising from the bed, it is death. The *spleen* is above, the *kidney* is below—first deficiency affects the *lung*, second the *heart*, third the *spleen*, fourth the *liver*, and fifth the *kidney*. From *lung* to *kidney*, all five organs are deficient—the *kidney governs bones*, and *bone weakness* leads to death.

In the case of **excess pulse** (至脉), if *skin clumps* and *fur falls*, it is also death. From *kidney* to *lung*, all five organs are deficient—the *lung reaches skin and fur*, and *skin clumping* with *fur falling* also leads to death.

However, if the *lung* is deficient, one should *augment its qi*. When *cold invades the body* and *food is improper*, the *lung is harmed*—since the *lung governs qi*, and its qi has been depleted by *cold exposure* and *improper diet*, one should *increase its qi* to treat it.

If the *heart* is deficient, one should *regulate its *Honor and Defense* (荣卫)*. *Worry, grief, and overthinking harm the heart*—since the *heart governs blood*, and its blood has been depleted by *worry, grief, and overthinking*, one should *adjust the *Honor and Defense* (荣卫)* to treat it.

If the *spleen* is deficient, one should *adjust its diet* and *moderate its cold and warmth*. *Overwork and fatigue harm the spleen*—when the *spleen’s function is impaired*, one should eat *spring chilled foods*, *summer cool gases*, and *winter warm foods*, following the seasonal *cold and warmth* to regulate diet, then the *spleen’s function will align*.

If the *liver* is deficient, one should *soothe its center*.

When *anger* causes *inverted qi* (逆氣), with *qi* rising but not descending, it harms the *liver*. The *liver governs anger*, and its *qi* is swift (急氣); thus, one should eat *sweet* (甘) foods to soothe it. *Sweet* in nature is gentle, and *sweet* belongs to the *spleen* (脾土), the *Earth* element dwelling in the *Middle Palace* (中宮). By using *sweet* flavors to temper the *liver’s center* (其中), its harm is mitigated.

If the *kidney* (腎) is injured, one should *nourish its essence* (精). Prolonged sitting in damp places or exerting oneself in water harms the *kidney*, which governs *essence*. When the *kidney channel* (腎經) is damaged, *gentle, sweet* foods should be taken to replenish its *essence-qi* (精氣). This is the method of treating *injured channels* (损至).

Furthermore, it broadly discusses the *principles of diagnosing channel injuries* (损至病法度). The pulse has *one beat per two breaths* (一呼再至), *one beat per two inhalations* (一吸再至). Between *one exhalation* (一呼), the pulse arrives *twice* (豚凡二至); between *one inhalation* (一吸), the pulse also arrives *twice* (豚亦二至). There is also *one beat per three breaths* (一呼三至), *one beat per three inhalations* (一吸三至). Between *one exhalation*, the pulse nearly arrives *three times* (脉幾三至); between *one inhalation*, the pulse also arrives *three times* (豚亦三至). Similarly, *one beat per four breaths* (一呼四至), *one beat per four inhalations* (一吸四至)—between *one exhalation*, the pulse nearly arrives *four times* (脉幾四至); between *one inhalation*, the pulse arrives *four times* (豚亦四至). *One beat per five breaths* (一呼五至), *one beat per five inhalations* (一吸五至)—between *one exhalation*, the pulse arrives *five times* (豚凡五至); between *one inhalation*, the pulse arrives *five times* (豚亦五至). *One beat per six breaths* (一呼六至), *one beat per six inhalations* (一吸六至)—between *one exhalation*, the pulse arrives *six times* (豚凡六至); between *one inhalation*, the pulse arrives *six times* (豚亦六至). *One beat per single breath* (一呼一至), *one beat per single inhalation* (一吸一至)—between *one exhalation*, the pulse should arrive *twice* (豚當二至), yet here it arrives *once* (今反骗至); between *one inhalation*, the pulse should arrive *twice* (豚當二至), yet here it arrives *once* (今反一至). There is also *two exhalations per single beat* (再呼一至), *two inhalations per single beat* (再吸一至). Between *two exhalations and two inhalations*, the pulse should arrive *eight times* (豚當八至), yet here it arrives *only twice* (今反止得二至). Or, *one beat per two breaths and inhalations* (呼吸再至). Annotation states: *"One beat per two breaths"* (一呼再至), *"one beat per two inhalations"* (一吸再至)—this refers to the *four beats* (四至) of the *even pulse* (平豚). Annotation states: *"One beat per two breaths and inhalations"* (呼吸之问止二至)—this refers to the *two beats* (二至) of the *uneven pulse* (敗豚). *[Note: The original text mentions "禺不明此句" (Yǔ does not understand this sentence) and requests later scholars to clarify, but no further annotation is provided.]*

When the pulse arrives in such ways, how does one distinguish its *disease* (疾病)? When the pulse and breath arrive in such patterns, how does one diagnose the *illness* (疾病)?

Thus: If the pulse arrives *one beat per two breaths* (一呼再至), *one beat per two inhalations* (一吸再至), neither *overly strong* (不大) nor *overly weak* (不小), it is called *even* (平). If the pulse arrives *twice per exhalation* (一呼而豚二至) and *twice per inhalation* (一吸而豚二至), without excess or deficiency, it is called the *even and harmonious pulse* (平和之豚).

If the pulse arrives *three times per exhalation* (一呼三至), *three times per inhalation* (一吸三至), it indicates *adapted disease* (適得病). If the pulse arrives *three times per exhalation* (一呼而豚三至), *three times per inhalation* (一吸而豚三至), and its pulse is *coarse and large* (其豚粗大), it is called *deviating from the channel* (離經), indicating *adapted disease* (適然始得病). If the pulse is *large at the front* (前大) and *small at the back* (後小), it indicates *headache and dizziness* (頭痛目眩). The *front large* (前大) refers to a *large pulse at the radial end* (寸外大); the *back small* (後小) refers to a *small pulse at the ulnar end* (寸内小). The *upper section follows Heaven* (上部法天), governing *diseases from the chest upward to the head* (胸以上至頭之上有疾). The *radial end* (寸口) is the *upper section*, so when the head and brain ache and the eyes feel dizzy, it is due to this. If the pulse is *small at the front* (前小) and *large at the back* (後大), it indicates *chest fullness and shortness of breath* (胸滿短氣). The *front small* (前小) refers to a *small pulse at the radial end* (寸外小); the *back large* (後大) refers to a *large pulse at the ulnar end* (寸内大). This governs *chest and diaphragm congestion* (胸膈滿塞) and *breathing difficulty* (氣息短促).

If the pulse arrives *four times per exhalation* (一呼四至), *four times per inhalation* (一吸四至), the *disease is severe* (病欲甚). The *even pulse* (平豚) has only *four beats* (四至); now, if the pulse arrives *four times per exhalation* (一呼而豚四至), totaling *eight beats* (共得八至), the pulse is *double the normal rate* (豚倍常經), indicating the *disease is severe but not yet critical* (病欲甚乍不及).

If the pulse is *overflowing and large* (洪大), it indicates *suffering, agitation, and fullness* (苦煩滿). Within the *eight-beat pulse* (八至), if the pulse is *overflowing and large* (豚洪大), the *disease lies in the three yang channels* (病在三陽), where *yang excess causes agitation and fullness* (陽盛煩燥痞滿). If the pulse is *deep and fine* (沉細), it indicates *abdominal pain* (腹中痛). Within the *eight-beat pulse* (八至), if the pulse is *deep and fine* (豚沉細), the *disease lies in the three yin channels* (病在三陰), as *yin governs the interior* (陰主於内), causing *abdominal pain* (腹肚疼痛). If the pulse is *slippery* (滑), it indicates *injury by heat* (傷熱). Within the *eight-beat pulse* (八至), if the pulse is *slippery* (豚滑), it indicates *internal heat* (中傷熱). If the pulse is *rough* (澀), it indicates *injury by mist and dew* (傷露寒氣). Within the *eight-beat pulse* (八至), if the pulse is *rough* (脉涩), it indicates *injury by dampness from mist and dew* (傷露寒氣).

If the pulse arrives *five times per exhalation* (一呼五至), *five times per inhalation* (一吸五至), the person will be *exhausted* (當困). If the pulse arrives *five times per exhalation* (一呼而豚五至), *qi* and *blood* are laboriously depleted (氣血勞走), causing the person to be *exhausted* (铃困). If the pulse is *deep and fine at night* (沉細夜加), it indicates the *disease worsens at night* (主夜问疾加). Within the *ten-beat pulse* (十至), if the pulse is *deep and fine* (豚沉細), it indicates *nighttime exacerbation* (主夜问疾加). If the pulse is *floating and large during the day* (浮大昼加), it indicates the *disease worsens during the day* (主昼间疾加). Within the *ten-beat pulse* (十至), if the pulse is *floating and large* (豚浮大), it indicates *daytime exacerbation* (主昼间疾加). Even if the pulse is neither *large nor small* (既不浮大，又不沉細), though the person is *exhausted*, it can still be treated (虽困可疗治). If the pulse has *irregular strength* (其有小大者), it is *difficult to treat* (為難治). Within the *ten-beat pulse* (十至), if the pulse is *extremely floating and large* (脉极浮大), it indicates *fire excess and exhaustion* (火盛铃灭). If the pulse is *extremely deep and fine* (脉极況细), it indicates *water weakness and depletion* (水弱叉竭).

If the pulse arrives *once per exhalation* (一呼一至), *once per inhalation* (一吸一至), it is called *injured* (损). The pulse should arrive *four times per breath and inhalation* (呼吸當四至), yet here it arrives *only twice* (今止二至), hence it is called the *injured pulse* (损豚). Though the person can still walk (人虽能行步),

「Though the patient should still lie in bed,
and though the illness is external while lying in bed,
this is because the blood and qi are both deficient.

The reason for lying in bed is due to the deficiency of blood and qi.

*Twice per exhalation* (再呼一至).
*Twice per exhalation and inhalation*, the pulse should arrive eight times (再呼再吸当八至), yet here it arrives only four times (今止四至).
*Pulse arrives twice per breath cycle* (呼吸再至).
*—This sentence is ambiguous; see prior annotation.*

It is called the *pulse of no soul* (无魂).
The *pulse of no soul* (无魂者) indicates impending death.

If exhalations are many while inhalations are few (呼多至少),
it signifies that the soul has already departed.
The soul belongs to *yang*, and *yang* governs life.
When the soul departs, one knows death is near.

Though the person can still walk, it is called a *walking corpse* (行尸).
Though the person can still walk, it is called a *corpse that walks* (死尸在行).

*The upper region has pulse* (上部有脉).
The *cun* position is the upper region; the *cun* department has pulse.
*The lower region lacks pulse* (下部无脉).
The *chi* position is the lower region; the *chi* department lacks pulse.

The patient will vomit.
The patient will naturally induce vomiting.
If there is no vomiting, death follows.
No vomiting means that *sheng* (生气, vital breath) is solely exhausted within.

If the upper region lacks pulse while the lower region has pulse,
though exhausted, no harm will come.
If the *cun* department lacks pulse while the *chi* department has pulse,
though the illness is extreme, no harm will ensue.

This is because, as with a person’s *chi* (尺) or a tree’s roots.
The reason no harm comes is that a person’s *chi* pulse is like a tree’s root.
Though branches and leaves may wither and decay, the root will regenerate.
The *cun* department is like the tree’s branches and leaves,
while the *chi* department is like the tree’s root.
Since a person has the *chi* pulse,
even without the *cun* pulse, life remains possible.

The pulse has its root.
The root lies in the *chi* department; if the *chi* department has pulse,
it means the pulse has its root.

A person possesses *yuan* (元气, original breath),
thus one knows death will not come.
The moving *qi* between the two kidneys
is the *yuan* breath received from one’s parents.
If the *chi* department has pulse, it means the person’s *yuan* breath is unbroken,
thus one knows death will not come.

The *Fifteen Difficulties* state:
*The Classic says the spring pulse is taut* (经言春脉弦).*」*

The spring pulse beats like the taut string of a bow or zither—tight and firm.
The summer pulse is *gōu* (钩).
The summer pulse beats like the curve of a hook.
The autumn pulse is *máo* (毛).
The autumn pulse beats light and floating like fine hair.
The winter pulse is *shí* (石).
The winter pulse beats heavy and deep like a stone.

Is this the *wáng* (王) pulse?
Asked: Is this the pulse of *wáng* (王) vitality?
Or is it a disease pulse?
Asked: Is this the pulse of illness?

Yet: *xuán* (弦), *gōu* (钩), *máo* (毛), and *shí* (石) are the pulses of the four seasons.
*Xuán* (弦) is the spring *wáng* (王) pulse.
*Gōu* (钩) is the summer *wáng* (王) pulse.
*Máo* (毛) is the autumn *wáng* (王) pulse.
*Shí* (石) is the winter pulse of *wáng* (王).
None are disease pulses.

The spring pulse is *xuán* (弦) because it belongs to the liver—*shù fāng* (束方), the wood of *jiǎ-yǐ* (甲乙).
Spring belongs to *shù fāng* (束方), *jiǎ-yǐ* (甲乙), the liver wood, and its pulse is *xuán* (弦).
When all things first emerge, they have no branches or leaves.
All things begin with their roots and yellow buds in spring, before branches and leaves appear.
Thus, when its pulse arrives, it is soft, weak, yet long.
Hence it is called *xuán* (弦).
Long refers to the pulse’s tautness.
Soft and weak yet long describes its pulse as faintly *xuán* (弦).
Faintly *xuán* (弦) means the stomach *qi* is present.

The summer pulse is *gōu* (钩) because it belongs to the heart—*nán fāng* (南方), the fire of *bǐng-dīng* (丙丁).
Summer belongs to *nán fāng* (南方), *bǐng-dīng* (丙丁), the heart fire, and its pulse is *gōu* (钩).
When all things flourish, their branches hang downward, leaves spread out, all curving like hooks.
Summer is when all things are nourished to their peak—branches and leaves lush, flowers and leaves scattered, all curving downward like hooks.
Thus, when its pulse arrives swiftly and departs slowly, it is called *gōu* (钩).
Swift arrival is the vigor of *yáng* (阳), seen in the *cùn* (寸) position.
Slow departure is the depletion of *yīn* (阴), seen in the *zhōng* (尺中) position.
Swift arrival describes its pulse as surging.
Swift arrival and slow departure describe its pulse as faintly surging, meaning the stomach *qi* is present.

The autumn pulse is *máo* (毛) because it belongs to the lung—*xī fāng* (西方), the metal of *gēng-xīn* (庚辛).
Autumn belongs to *xī fāng* (西方), *gēng-xīn* (庚辛), the lung metal, and its pulse is *máo* (毛).
When all things reach their end, grasses and trees shed their flowers and leaves, leaving only bare branches, like fine hair.
Autumn is the season of *sù-shā* (肃杀), when grasses and trees drop their flowers and leaves, leaving only branches—sparse like hair.
Thus, when its pulse arrives, it is light, empty, and floating, hence called *máo* (毛).
The lung floats above, its *qi* governing skin and hair, so its pulse is floating like hair.

The winter pulse is *shí* (石) because it belongs to the kidney—*běi fāng* (北方), the water of *rén-guǐ* (壬癸).
Winter belongs to *běi fāng* (北方), *rén-guǐ* (壬癸), the kidney water, and its pulse is *shí* (石).
When all things are stored away.
All things retreat and hide in winter.
In deep winter, water congeals like stone.
In deep winter, the earth freezes, and water hardens like stone.
Thus, when its pulse arrives, it is deep, damp, and smooth, hence called *shí* (石).
Deep is the proper quality of the winter pulse.
Deep and damp yet smooth implies faint depth, meaning the stomach *qi* is present.

These are the pulses of the four seasons.
A summary of the pulses of the four seasons.

「What if there are changes? If the pulse shifts from its seasonal norm, how should it be treated?
Now: In spring, the pulse is *prickly* (弦), but if it reverses, it is a sign of illness.
What is meant by *reversal*? In spring, the liver pulse should be prickly, but if it opposes the prickly pulse, then it is a liver disease.
What is called *excessive reversal*?
Now: When the *qi* arrives with excessive strength, it is called *too much*, and the illness lies externally.
Spring is the season of *Lesser Yang* (少阳) dominance, and its pulse is faintly prickly, implying the presence of stomach *qi*.
Now, if the pulse *qi* arrives with excessive strength, it is called *too much*.
Excessive strength means *Yang* is too vigorous.
The illness lies externally, with symptoms of frequent cleanliness (*善洁*), greenish face (*面青*), and quick temper (*善怒*).
When the *qi* arrives faint and weak, it is called *not enough*, and the illness lies internally.
The pulse of *Jue Yin* (厥阴) manifests in tendons (*筋*), and its pulse is prickly.
Now, if it becomes faint and weak, it is called *not enough*.
Since *Yin* resides in the center, the illness is internal.
Symptoms include fullness in the limbs (*四肢满*), closed pain (*闭疼*), difficult urination (*波便难*), and tetanic cramps (*转筋*).
When the *qi* arrives with a dull and faint rhythm, like a leaf trembling in the wind on a *yú* (榆) tree, it is called *level* (平).
Spring combines *Lesser Yang* and *Jue Yin*, and their pulses arrive like spring wind rustling willow leaves—moist, weak, and harmonious, hence called *level pulse*.
Level means the presence of stomach *qi*.
If the pulse is *excessively strong and smooth*, like sliding along a long pole, it is called *disease* (病).
Excessively strong and smooth means *too much* (太) prickliness.
Like sliding along a long pole means the pulse is long yet not soft.
This is the *prickly pulse*, and since stomach *qi* is diminished, it is a sign of illness.
If it is urgent and forceful, like a newly strung bowstring, it is called *death* (死).
Forceful, rigid, and excessively strong, like a tightly strung bowstring, this is a pulse of pure prickliness with no stomach *qi*, hence *death*.
A faintly prickly pulse is called *level*.
Faintly prickly means slightly resembling the prickly pulse, not faint while being prickly.
Faintly prickly is one part stomach *qi* and two parts prickly *qi*, both acting together to form a faintly prickly pulse.
If the prickly *qi* is dominant and stomach *qi* is diminished, it is a sign of illness.
If the pulse is purely prickly with no stomach *qi*, it is *death*.
Three parts of pure prickly *qi* with no stomach *qi* means the *true zàng* (藏) is exposed, hence *death*.
Spring relies on stomach *qi* as its foundation.
The *qi* of the Five *Zàng* (藏) must be harmonized with the stomach *qi*; if it acts alone, it will falter, just as an object too rigid cannot act alone—if it is harmonized and softened, it will become stable.
The stomach is the sea of water and grain, and humans receive *qi* from grain.
Grain enters the stomach, which then distributes it to the Five *Zàng* and Six *Fǔ* (府).
Thus, the liver relies on stomach *qi* as its foundation, and the remaining Four *Zàng* follow this principle similarly.

In summer, the pulse is *crooked* (钩), and if it reverses, it is a sign of illness.
What is meant by *reversal*?»

In summer, the pulse should be *crooked* (钩). If it opposes the *crooked* (钩) pulse—meaning if it is *real* (实) and *strong* (强)—this is called *excess* (太过), and the illness lies externally. A pulse that is *floating* (浮) and *dissipating* (大散) belongs to the heart. Now, if the pulse is *real* and *strong* in opposition, this is *excess* (太过), with external symptoms of flushed face, dry mouth, and frequent laughter.

If the pulse comes *empty* (虚) and *feeble* (微), this is called *deficiency* (不及), and the illness lies internally. In summer, the heart is weak while the lesser yin (少阴) of the kidneys flourishes; now, if the pulse is *empty* and *feeble* in opposition, this is *deficiency* (不及). The internal symptoms are restlessness of the heart, heartache, and heat in the palms—this is *death* (死).

When the pulse comes *stacked* (累累), like beads following one another, or *smoothly* (如循琅牙), it is called *level* (平). A *full* (满) and *flourishing* (盛) pulse, resembling a *pearl* (珠) in shape at the middle of the hand, is akin to the *pearl* (珠) pulse—thus called *heart level* (心平).

If the pulse comes *increasingly rapid* (益数), like a *hawk* (鹞) lifting its foot, it is called *illness* (病). The heart pulse should only be *floating* (浮) and *dissipating* (散), not *rapid* (数). Now, if the pulse is *rapid* like a hawk lifting its foot, this is called *heart illness* (心病).

If the pulse *bends forward* (前曲) and *lingers backward* (后居), like grasping a leather belt with a hook, it is called *death* (死). *Bending forward* (前曲) means the pulse is *crooked* (钩) and *weak* (无力). *Lingering backward* (后居) means it is *stiff* (倨) and *unmoving* (不动), *strained* (劲直), and *grasping* (操执)—like grasping a leather belt with a hook, hence *death* (死).

In summer, a *feeble* (微) yet *crooked* (钩) pulse is called *level* (平). The pulse is *feeble* but resembles *crookedness* (钩), not *feeble* while being *crooked*—it has *stomach qi* (胃气), hence called *level crooked pulse* (平豚). If the pulse is *excessively crooked* (钩多) with little *stomach qi* (胃气), it is called *illness* (病). The pulse is not *feeble* but *crooked*, indicating *stomach qi* is weak—thus it is *illness* (病). If the pulse is merely *crooked* (钩) with no *stomach qi* (胃气), it is called *death* (死). When three parts of the pulse are *crooked* (钩) and there is no *stomach qi* (胃气), the *true zàng* (藏) is exposed—this is *death* (死).

Summer relies on *stomach qi* (胃气) as its foundation, just like spring. The pulse of autumn should be *hair-like* (毛). If it opposes the *hair-like* (毛) pulse—meaning if it is *real* (实) and *strong* (强)—this is called *excess* (太过), and the illness lies externally. The autumn pulse should be *hair-like* (毛); now, if it becomes *real* and *strong* in opposition, this is *excess* (太过), with external symptoms of pale face, frequent sneezing, sadness, and reluctance to laugh or cry.

If the pulse comes *empty* (虚) and *feeble* (微), this is called *deficiency* (不及), and the illness lies internally. The lung pulse is *light* (轻) and *empty* (虚), *floating* (浮); now, if pressing it makes it *even more empty* (益虚微), this is a lack of *stomach qi* (胃气). The symptoms are wheezing, coughing, and *alcohol-induced* (酒淅) *shaking* (淅).

When the pulse comes *gentle* (蔼蔼), like the canopy of a small carriage, and pressing it makes it *larger* (益大), it is called *level* (平). The canopy of a small carriage is *light* (轻) and *floating* (浮), *gentle* (蔼蔼). Pressing it makes it *large* (大), indicating *stomach qi* (胃气) is present—hence called *level* (平).

If the pulse neither *rises* (上) nor *falls* (下), like following a *hawk’s feather* (鹞羽) in a *hesitant* (涩) manner, it is called *illness* (病). The lung, being *Metal* (金) and belonging to summer’s residual *yang* (阳), should have its pulse *ascend* (上). The lung’s *qi* (气) is of *yin* (阴) nature, so its pulse *descends* (下). Now, if the pulse neither ascends nor descends but follows the *hesitant* (涩) motion of a hawk’s feather, it is called *illness* (病).

If pressing it feels *dissipating* (消索), like *wind blowing hair* (风吹毛), it is called *death* (死). *Wind blowing hair* (风吹毛) describes a *floating* (飘腾) and *unstable* (不定) pulse, lacking a foundation. Now, if pressing the pulse feels like *dissipating* (消散) *threads* (索) and *wind blowing hair* (风吹毛), this is a pulse that is merely *hair-like* (毛) with no *stomach qi* (胃气)—hence *death* (死).

In autumn, a *feeble* (微) yet *hair-like* (毛) pulse is called *level* (平). The pulse is *feeble* (微) but resembles *hair-like* (毛), not *feeble* while being *hair-like*—it is a combination of one part *stomach qi* (胃气) and two parts *hair-like qi* (毛气) moving together, hence called *feeble hair-like pulse* (微毛). If the pulse is *excessively hair-like* (毛多) with little *stomach qi* (胃气), it is called *illness* (病). The pulse is not *feeble* but *hair-like*, indicating *stomach qi* is weak—thus it is *illness* (病). If the pulse is merely *hair-like* (毛) with no *stomach qi* (胃气), it is called *death* (死). When three parts of the pulse are *hair-like* (毛) and there is no *stomach qi* (胃气), the *true zàng* (藏) is exposed—this is *death* (死).

Autumn relies on *stomach qi* (胃气) as its foundation. All Five *Zàng* (藏) derive their *qi* (气) from the stomach; the stomach is the foundation of the Five *Zàng*. The *qi* of the *Zàng* (藏) cannot naturally reach the *hand taiyang* (手太阳), but must rely on *stomach qi* (胃气) to extend there. If *stomach qi* (胃气) cannot accompany it to the *hand taiyang* (手太阳), the *true zàng* (藏) *qi* (气) appears alone—this is when the *Zàng* illness overpowers the *stomach qi*, hence called *death* (死).

In winter, the pulse should be *stone-like* (石). If it opposes the *stone-like* (石) pulse—meaning if it is *superficial* (浮) and *hesitant* (涩)—this is called *yin* (阴) illness. Why is it called *reversal* (反)?

The winter pulse should be *deep* (沉) like a stone. If it opposes this *depth* (沉), meaning it is *superficial* (浮) and *hesitant* (涩), this is called *excess* (太过), and the illness lies externally. The winter pulse should be *gentle* (濡) and *hesitant* (涩); now, if it opposes and is *superficial* (浮) and *feeble* (虚), this is *deficiency* (不及), with symptoms of *inverted qi* (逆气), lower abdominal pain, *urgent diarrhea* (泄如下重), and coldness in the feet with *inverted* (逆) *qi*.

When the pulse comes *large above* (上大) and *small below* (下兑), *gentle* (濡) and *slippery* (滑) like a *sparrow’s beak* (雀之喙), it is called *level* (平). A *sparrow’s beak* (雀之喙) is a pulse that is *large* (大) upon arrival and *small* (兑) upon departure. The *large above* (上大) indicates the *foot taiyang* (足太阳) pulse arriving *large* (大). The *small below* (下兑) indicates the *foot shaoyin* (足少阴) pulse departing *small* (兑). When *yin* and *yang* are appropriately balanced, it signifies *stomach qi* (胃气) is strong—hence called *level* (平).

If the pulse comes *hesitant* (啄啄) and *connected* (连属), with a *slightly curved* (微曲) quality within, it is called *illness* (病). *Hesitant* (啄啄) means it does not flow smoothly, hence called *connected* (连属). If the pulse is *hesitant* (啄啄) and *slightly curved* (微曲) within, the *spleen pulse* (脾脉) overpowers the *kidney pulse* (肾病)—thus it is *illness* (病).

If the pulse arrives like *unwinding threads* (解索) and departs like *striking a stone* (弹石), it is called *death* (死). A pulse that arrives like *unwinding threads* (解索) is *empty* (虚) and *slow* (慢), lacking a foundation. A pulse that departs like *striking a stone* (弹石) is *rapid* (疾)—hence *death* (死).

In winter, a *feeble* (微) yet *stone-like* (石) pulse is called *level* (平). The pulse is *feeble* (微) but resembles *stone-like* (石), not *feeble* while being *stone-like*—it is a combination of one part *stomach qi* (胃气) and two parts *deep qi* (沉气) moving together, hence called *feeble stone-like pulse* (微石). If the pulse is *excessively stone-like* (石多) with little *stomach qi* (胃气), it is called *illness* (病). The pulse is not *feeble* but *stone-like*, indicating *stomach qi* is weak—thus it is *illness* (病). If the pulse is merely *stone-like* (石) with no *stomach qi* (胃气), it is called *death* (死). When three parts of the pulse are *stone-like* (石) and there is no *stomach qi* (胃气), the *true zàng* (藏) is exposed—this is *death* (死).

Winter relies on *stomach qi* (胃气) as its foundation, just like spring. The stomach is the sea of water and grain. It is fifteen *cùn* (寸) wide and twenty-six *cùn* (寸) long. When *flexed* (横屈), it holds three *dǒu* (斗) and five *shēng* (升) of water and grain, with two *dǒu* (斗) of grain and one *dǒu* (斗) and five *shēng* (升) of water remaining within. It governs the reception of the Four Seasons’ *qi* (气), hence all rely on *stomach qi* (胃气) as their foundation.

The Four Seasons—spring, summer, autumn, and winter—all draw upon *stomach qi* as their foundation. This is called the *variations of the seasons that bring illness*.
