If in spring the *feeble pulse* (豚) is excessive while *stomach qi* is deficient, or in summer the *uneven pulse* (钧) is excessive while *stomach qi* is deficient, or in autumn the *hair-like pulse* (毛) is excessive while *stomach qi* is abundant, or in winter the *stone-like pulse* (石) is excessive while *stomach qi* is deficient—these are all the *variations of the seasons that bring illness*. This is the critical juncture of life and death.

When the *qi* of the Five *zàng* harmonizes with *stomach qi*, life ensues. If the *true zàng* is exposed, death follows.

All the critical junctures of life and death hinge upon the stomach.

The spleen is the central region. [Note: *Difficulties* recorded in the *Four Hard Cases*.] Its balanced state is imperceptible.

The spleen’s pulse, when balanced, governs the Four Seasons in concealment, hence it is imperceptible. Only when it declines does it become manifest.

When the spleen’s *jing* is weakened and fails, the *feeble pulse* (豚) takes form. It arrives like a sparrow’s pecking.

If the pulse is *large above and deficient below*, soft and slippery—this is the *sparrow’s beak pulse*, where the kidney’s *jing* overcomes the spleen’s *jing*, signifying *deficiency*.

If it moves intermittently, stopping and starting like a leaking roof—this is the *leaking-roof pulse*, where the kidney’s *jing* overwhelms the spleen’s *jing*, signifying *excess*.

Thus, when the spleen declines, the *feeble pulse* (豚) manifests as the *sparrow’s beak* or *leaking-roof* pulses.

[End of Volume Two of the *Yellow Emperor’s Eighty-One Difficult Cases*, annotated by Mu Tu, with commentary from *The Original Meanings*.] #1: *Original text omitted; restored from *The Original Meanings*.]

**#2 Bladder [Ureter]:** This passage below contains the character *evil* (邪) in *The Collected Commentaries* (《集注》).

**#3 Power [Strength]:** According to the context, this should likely be *"then"* (乃).

**#4 None:** Doubtful; likely should be *"none"* (无).

**#5 Pulse:** *The Original Meanings* (《本义》) reads *"qi"* (气).

**#6 Lung:** Doubtful; likely a miswriting for *"pulse"* (脉).

**#7 Emptiness:** According to the following context, this should likely be *"blood"* (血).

**#8 At:** This passage below contains the character *"with"* (与) in *The Collected Commentaries* (《集注》).

**#9 Extend:** Doubtful; likely should be *"groan"* (呻).

**#10 This:** According to the context, this should likely be *"like"* (比).

**#11 Ailment:** According to the meaning, this should likely be *"foolish"* (愚).

**#12 Spleen:** Doubtful; likely should be *"lung"* (肺).

**#13 Thus:** Above this, *The Original Meanings* (《本义》) and *The Collected Commentaries* (《集注》) contain the twenty characters: *"How should one treat and repair this?"* (治损之法奈何).

**#14 Eat:** Doubtful; likely should be *"ling"* (玲).

**#15 Qi:** Doubtful; likely should be *"eat"* (食).

**#16 [Person]:** Originally *"filial"* (孝); corrected according to the context.

**#17 One:** Above this, *The Original Meanings* (《本义》) and *The Collected Commentaries* (《集注》) contain the twenty characters: *"One exhalation to six arrivals, one inhalation to six arrivals, is the pulse of death; deep and subtle, death comes at night; floating and large, death comes by day."* (一呼六至，一吸六至，为死脉，沉细夜死，浮大昼死也).

**#18 [Ending particle]:** Originally *"[person]"* (者); corrected according to *The Original Meanings* (《本义》) and *The Collected Commentaries* (《集注》).

**#19 Like:** *The Collected Commentaries* (《集注》) reads *"pulse"* (脉).

**#20 Pulse:** Originally *"arrives"* (来); corrected according to *The Original Meanings* (《本义》) and *The Collected Commentaries* (《集注》).

**#21 Its:** Below this, likely omitted the character *"pulse"* (脉).

**#22 Autumn:** Following the pattern above, likely omitted the character *"belonging to"* (属).

**#23 Floating:** Originally *"trust"* (孚); corrected according to the context.

**#24 Winter:** Following the pattern above, likely omitted the character *"belonging to"* (属).

**#25 Exterior:** *The Collected Commentaries* (《集注》) in the Fifteenth Difficulty, Lü’s annotation, reads *"nourish"* (养).

**#26 Symptoms of illness: wheezing and coughing, sprinkling and shivering:** *The Collected Commentaries* (《集注》) in the Fifteenth Difficulty, Ding’s annotation, reads *"Its internal symptoms are wheezing and coughing, wine-like fever, and chills with alternating hot and cold."* (其内证喘咳酒浙寒热).

**#27 Blow hair:** Below this, *The Original Meanings* (《本义》) and *The Collected Commentaries* (《集注》) contain the two characters: *"death by"* (日死).

**#28 Yin:** *The Original Meanings* (《本义》) and *The Collected Commentaries* (《集注》) read *"illness"* (病).

**#29 Guard:** According to the context, this should likely be *"tight"* (弦).

**#30 Many:** According to the surrounding context, this should likely be *"few"* (少).

**Volume Three of the *Yellow Emperor’s Eighty-One Difficult Cases*, Annotated and Illustrated with Commentaries by Lu Guo, Qin Yue Ren, and Fan Zi of Linchuan, with Additional Notes by Li Xian Zi and Ye Ju Sentence-by-Sentence Explanation**

**Difficulty #16:** The pulse has three regions and nine candidates. The three regions are the *cun*, *guan*, and *chi*. The nine candidates are three in the upper region, three in the middle, and three in the lower—thus nine in total. There are yin and yang. The *cun* position, where the yang pulse appears in nine divisions, is superficial. The *chi* region, where the yin pulse appears in one *cun*, is deep. There are degrees of lightness and heaviness. When the yang pulse is superficial, the hands feel light; when the yin pulse is deep, the hands feel heavy. There are sixty methods. The pulse begins with six methods—whether floating, long, short, smooth, or rough—across the three yin and three yang regions. Now, these sixty methods are combined into sixty. One pulse changes with the four seasons. The pulse follows the transformations of the four seasons; see the ten variations of one pulse in Difficulty #10. The sages are distant. In the time of Qin Yue Ren, the sages were long gone. Each follows their own methods—how then can they be distinguished? It is said that the three regions are one method, the nine candidates are one method, yin and yang are one method, lightness and heaviness are one method, and the sixty methods are one method. Since the methods and symbols are numerous, the difficulties can be distinguished.

Now: Observe the patient’s inner and outer symptoms. If one examines the clarity of their complexion and the five colors, one can immediately know the inner and outer symptoms. Formerly, this character was mistakenly taken as a different word and changed to *shì* (视). How does the disease manifest? How does the disease present itself?

---

When examining the **Liver pulse**, the Liver *qi* is taut yet soft and long.
In its **external symptoms**, the patient’s complexion is clear and clean.
The **Liver** governs the **Gallbladder** (*dǎn*), the repository of cold purity, hence the patient is fond of cleanliness.
Their face is green, for the Liver’s color is green, thus the face appears green.
They are prone to anger, as the **Gallbladder** is the organ of rectitude, overseeing judgment, and the Liver in the emotions manifests as anger.

In its **internal symptoms**, there is a moving *qi* to the left of the navel (*qì*), which, when pressed, feels firm and painful.
Liver accumulation is called *féi qì*, located to the left of the navel, and when palpated, it is firm and painful.
The patient’s limbs feel full and constricted.
The **Liver (Wood)** and **Spleen (Earth)**—the Spleen governs the limbs; if it is not diseased, Earth fears nothing, thus the limbs are full and constricted.

There is **twitching of the genitals** (*yǐn sōu*).
The Liver *qi* courses through the **yin organ**, causing **twitching** (*cèng bō*) and **straining urination** (*xiǎo biàn sè*).
The patient has **difficult defecation** (*biàn nán*).
The **Liver** and **Kidney** reside in the lower abdomen (*xià pì*), and when the Liver is diseased, *qi* reverses, failing to descend, thus causing **constipation** (*dà biàn jiān nán*).
There is **spasmodic cramping** (*zhuǎn jīn*).
The Liver nourishes the sinews with blood; when the Liver is diseased, the sinews **spasm** (*jīn zhuǎn*).
If these symptoms are present, it is **Liver disease**.
If these symptoms are absent, it is not **Liver disease**.

When examining the **Heart pulse**, the Heart *qi* is floating, expansive, and dispersed.
In its **external symptoms**, the patient’s face is red, for the Heart’s color is red, thus the face appears red.
Their mouth is dry, as the Heart’s pulse network lies beneath the tongue; when the Heart is oppressed by heat, the mouth becomes dry and parched.
They are fond of laughter, for the Heart in sound manifests as laughter.

In its **internal symptoms**, there is a moving *qi* above the navel (*qì*), which, when pressed, feels firm and painful.
Heart accumulation is called *fù liáng*, located above the navel, and when palpated, it is firm and painful.
The patient experiences **heartache and vexation**, with **palms hot and throat dry**.
The Heart is the sovereign of the five *zàng* organs; when the four other organs are diseased, the Heart knows it—but if there is pain in the Heart itself, how much more so when the primary disease afflicts it!
**Constant pain** is **Heart Enclosure** (*xīn bāo yōng*).
The Heart itself does not receive disease, but if it does, then **morning diagnosis leads to evening death**, or **evening diagnosis leads to morning death**—this is **Enclosure** (*bāo yōng*), a disease of the **Heart Enclosure** (*xīn bāo*), which is the pulse network of the **Hand Lesser Yin** (*shǒu yǎn yīn*), emerging from the tip of the middle fingers, descending into the palm, and flexed to locate at the **Labor Official** (*Láo Guān*) acupuncture point.
When the Heart Enclosure is diseased, the palms feel hot and **moist** (*wǎn*).
If these symptoms are present, it is **Heart disease**.
If these symptoms are absent, it is not **Heart disease**.

When examining the **Spleen pulse**, the Spleen *qi* is moderate yet expansive.
In its **external symptoms**, the patient’s face is yellow, for the Spleen’s color is yellow, thus the face appears yellow.
They are prone to **sighing** (*yì*), as the Spleen in transformation manifests as sighing.
They are fond of **sweet flavors**, for the Spleen governs sweetness, and sweetness is what they crave.
In its **internal symptoms**, there is a moving *qi* at the navel (*qì*), which, when pressed, feels firm and painful.
Spleen accumulation is called *pǐ qì*, located at the navel, and when palpated, it is firm and painful.
The patient experiences **abdominal distension and indigestion**, with **food failing to digest**.
When **dampness** (*shī qì*) prevails, it causes **distension** (*péng zhàng*), and since **yang** *qi* is in the lower abdomen, digestion is impaired.

They have **heaviness in the body and joint pain**.
Their **spirit is sluggish**, and they **favor excessive sleep**.
Their limbs **fail to contract** (*sì zhī quán wú shōu shí*).
If these symptoms are present, it is **Spleen disease**.
If these symptoms are absent, it is not **Spleen disease**.

When examining the **Lung pulse**, the Lung *qi* is floating, absolute, and short.
In its **external symptoms**, the patient’s face is white, for the Lung’s color is white, thus the face appears white.
They are prone to **sneezing** (*shàn tì*), as the nose is the pathway of pure *qi*, belonging to the Lung; when the Lung is affected by **wind-cold**, it passes through the nose, causing **sneezing**.
They are **melancholic and discontent**, for **sorrow** is the Lung’s emotional manifestation.

The Lung governs **Autumn** (*qīu*), its season of **melancholy** (*chóu*).
The Spleen is Earth, the Lung is Metal; the Spleen is the **mother** of the Lung.
The Spleen governs **laughter** (*gē*), and when a child is ill, the mother mourns—
thus in Autumn, **melancholy** prevails, and there is no joy.

They **desire to weep**.
If **there is sound**, it is weeping.
In its **internal symptoms**, the right side of the abdomen (*qí yòu*) has **moving qi** (*dòng qì*).
Pressing it reveals a **firm, painful sensation** (*lǎo ruò tòng*).
This **accumulation** (*jī*) in the Lung is called **"Breathing Noble"** (*xī guì*), located to the right of the navel (*qí*).
When palpated, it feels **fixed and painful**.

Their disease manifests as **shortness of breath and coughing** (*chuǎn kè*).
The Lung is the **"Five Zàng's canopy"** (*wǔ zàng huá gài*), most fond of **purity and cold** (*qīng lěng*).
When the Lung is struck by **wind-cold**, the **pathway of qi** becomes **constricted** (*sè*), causing **rapid breathing and hacking coughs** (*chuǎn jí kè sòu*).
They experience **shivering and alternating cold and heat** (*sǎ xiāo hán rè*).
When the Lung is **empty**, they shiver with cold; when **full**, they feel heat and **restlessness** (*wèn*).

If these symptoms are present, it is the Lung.
If these symptoms are present, it is a **Lung pattern**.
If these symptoms are absent, it is not.
If these symptoms are absent, it is not a **Lung pattern**.

Now, if one examines the **Kidney pulse** (*shèn mài*).
The Kidney pulse is **deep, short, and firm** (*chén duǎn yǐ dùn*).
In its **external symptoms**, the face is black.
The Kidney’s color is black, hence the face appears black.
They are prone to **fear** (*kǒng*).
The Kidney’s emotional manifestation is **fear**.

They **yawn** (*qiàn*).
When the **Root of the Great Yang** (*Jùyáng*) is empty, they yawn.
In its **internal symptoms**, the lower abdomen (*qí xià*) has **moving qi**.
Pressing it reveals a **firm, half-painful sensation** (*bàn tòng*).
This **accumulation** in the Kidney is called **"Galloping Noble"** (*bēn tún*), located beneath the navel (*qí*).
When palpated, it feels **firm with half-pain**.

Their disease is **inverted qi** (*nì qì*).
When Kidney *qi* is insufficient, it harms the **Conception Vessel** (*chōng mài*), causing **inverted qi**.
The lower abdomen is **urgently painful** (*shǎo fù jí tòng*).
The pulse of **Foot Lesser Yin** (*zú shào yīn*) runs through the lower abdomen,
and with **Foot Jue Yin** and **Foot Greater Yin**, the three Yin pulses converge beneath the navel (*qí*).
Now, since the Kidney is diseased, the lower abdomen aches.

They **leak like a downward burden** (*xiè rú xià zhòng*).
This is the **pattern of "Leaking"** (*wáng xiè*), also called **"Leaking at the Cubit"** (*chǐ xīn xiè*).
It involves **urgent straining and heaviness after defecation** (*guǒ jí hòu zhòng*), frequently reaching the **rectum** (*yòu*) yet unable to pass,
with **pain in the urethral region** (*jīng zhōng tòng*).
The **inner leg** experiences cold and **inverted pulse** (*zú nèi huái shàng wǔ cùn*).
Five inches above the inner ankle is the **moving pulse of Foot Lesser Yin** (*zú shào yīn*), hence the inner leg feels cold and **inverted pulse**.

If these symptoms are present, it is the Kidney.
If these symptoms are present, it is a **Kidney pattern**.
If these symptoms are absent, it is not.
If these symptoms are absent, it is not a **Kidney pattern**.

The **Seventeenth Difficulty** states:
*"The classics say: Some diseases bring death.
Some illnesses result in death.
Some diseases heal without treatment.
Some illnesses, though untreated, spontaneously resolve and stabilize.
Some persist for years without end.
Some illnesses drag on year after year, never ceasing.
But can one determine life, death, survival, or extinction by simply cutting the pulse?*
Life, death, survival, or extinction—can one know them by cutting the pulse?
Yet:
*One can fully comprehend them.*
Life, death, survival, or extinction are all known through the pulse.

If diagnosing a disease, one finds:
**The eyes do not wish to see others** (*mù bù yù jiàn rén*).
When examining an illness, if the patient **closes their eyes and dislikes seeing others**,
this is a **Liver pattern**.
The pulse should be **Liver pulse—strong, urgent, and long** (*jiāng mài qiáng jí ér cháng*).
The **root pulse of the Liver** (*Jīn*) is **firm, soft, and long** (*shèn ruǎn ér cháng*).
Now, if the pulse is **strong and urgent**, this disease is present.
But if one instead finds **Lung pulse—floating, short, and rough** (*fú duǎn ér sè*), death is certain.
A Liver disease presenting with Lung pulse means **true spirit** (*zhen guǐ*) is **cloaked by false** (*kè*), and Metal overcomes Wood, hence death is inevitable.

If diagnosing a disease, one finds:
**The eyes are open but thirsty**, with **abdominal tightness** (*xīn xià lǎo*).
A Heart disease pattern presents with **open eyes and thirst**,
and **abdominal tightness** (*xīn xià lǎo gù bù kāi*).
The pulse should be **tight, firm, and rapid** (*jǐn shí ér shù*).
Since there is a Heart disease, there should be a **Heart pulse** (*xīn mài*), which is tight, firm, and rapid.
But if one instead finds **deep, sluggish, and faint pulse** (*chén rú ér wēi*), death is certain.
A Heart disease presenting with Kidney pulse means **Water comes to subdue Fire** (*shuǐ lái kè huǒ*), and a Yang disease reveals **Yin pulse** (*yín mài*), hence death is inevitable.

If diagnosing a disease, one finds:
**Blood is vomited**.
Blood is spat from the mouth.
**Nasal discharge** (*qiàn*) and **clear nasal mucus** (*niǔ*) are also present.
All the above belong to **Lung disease**.
The pulse should be **deep and fine** (*chén xì*).

---

**Blood belongs to Yin**; if **blood is vomited** (*tǔ xuè*) or **nasal discharge** (*qiàn*) occurs, the pulse should be **deep and fine** (*chén xì*), which aligns with the disease pulse.
But if instead it is **floating, large, and firm** (*fú dà ér lǎo*), death is certain.
If one does not find a **Lung pulse** (*fèi mài*) but instead detects a **floating, large, and firm Heart pulse** (*xīn mài*), the disease and pulse are contradictory—**Fire comes to subdue Metal** (*huǒ lái kè jīn*)—hence death is inevitable.

If diagnosing a disease, one finds:
**Delirious speech** (*zhān yán*) and **erratic speech** (*wàng yǔ*).
**Delirious speech** refers to **incoherent murmuring** (*ní bō ér yǔ*).
**Erratic speech** means **speech without rhyme or reason** (*yán yǔ zài wàng*).
The body should have **heat**.
Heat accumulating in the Heart causes **disordered speech**, and the body should exhibit **heat**.
The pulse should be **surging and large** (*hóng dà*).
If the body is hot and the pulse is large, the pulse and disease correspond.
But if instead **limbs contract in reverse** (*shǒu zú jué nì*) and the pulse becomes **hostile and fine** (*chǒu zàn ér wēi*), death is certain.
If the body should be hot but instead **limbs are cold and withdrawn** (*shǒu zú yàn líng*), and the pulse should be **surging and large** but is instead **hostile and fine**, this is a **Yang pulse presenting as Yin** (*yáng mài jiàn yīn mài*), with the pulse and disease contradictory—hence death is inevitable.

If diagnosing a disease, one finds:
**A distended abdomen with diarrhea** (*dà fù ér xiè*).
If **Dampness qi** (*shī qì*) prevails, **abdominal distension** (*zhàng*) occurs; if the Spleen cannot restrain, **diarrhea** (*xiè*) follows.
The pulse should be **fine and rough** (*wēi xì ér sè*).
Since there is a **Spleen condition** (*pí zhèng*), this pulse should appear.
But if instead it is **tight, large, and slippery** (*jǐn dà ér huá*), death is certain.
If one does not find a **Spleen pulse** (*pí mài*) but instead detects a **tight, large, and slippery Liver pulse** (*gān mài*), **Liver Wood subdues Spleen Earth** (*gān mù kè pí tǔ*), with the disease and pulse contradictory—hence death is inevitable.

The **Eighteen Difficulties** (*Shíbā Nán*) state:
**The pulse has three regions** (*mài yǒu sān bù*).

「Thus, death is certain.

If the body should be hot but instead the hands and feet feel aversion to warmth, if the pulse should be **tight and large** (*hóng dà*) but instead appears **fine and weak** (*xiàng xì*), this is **Yang pulse** (*yáng mài*) encountering **Yin pulse** (*yìn mài*), where the pulse and disease are contradictory—hence death is inevitable.

If the disease presents as **abdominal distension with diarrhea** (*dà fù ér xiè*), then:

**Dampness qi** (*shī qì*) prevails, causing **abdominal distension** (*zhàng*); if the Spleen cannot restrain, **diarrhea** (*xiè*) follows.

The pulse should be **fine and rough** (*wēi xì ér sè*).
Since there is a **Spleen condition** (*pí zhèng*), this pulse should appear.
But if instead it is **tight, large, and slippery** (*jǐn dà ér huá*), death is certain.
If one does not find a **Spleen pulse** (*pí mài*) but instead detects a **tight, large, and slippery Liver pulse** (*gān mài*), **Liver Wood subdues Spleen Earth** (*gān mù kè pí tǔ*), with the disease and pulse contradictory—hence death is inevitable.

The **Eighteen Difficulties** (*Shíbā Nán*) state:
**The pulse has three regions** (*mài yǒu sān bù*).

---

**The three regions—cubit, guan, and inch—constitute the pulse’s three divisions.**
Each division encompasses **four channels** (*jīng*).
Within each division, there are **two channels** (*èr jīng*), so across the six divisions, there are **twelve channels** (*shí'èr jīng*) in total.
Yet here it mentions only **four channels**, referring specifically to:
**Hand Greater Yang** (*shǒu Tàiyáng*), **Hand Yang Brightness** (*Yángmíng*), **Foot Greater Yang** (*zú Tàiyáng*), and **Foot Lesser Yin** (*shǎo yīn*).
These two channels follow the **nature of Water and Fire** (*shuǐ huǒ zhī xìng*), each having its own **principles and order** (*gāngjì*), yet unable to **mutually transform and ascend or descend** (*biàn tōng shàng xià*).
The remaining **eight channels**—those of the **Hand** that generate in the **Foot**, and those of the **Foot** that generate in the **Hand**—are what the classics mean when stating that each division contains **four channels**.

**Hand Lesser Yin** (*shǒu Tàiyīn*)—**Lung** (*fèi*)—and **Hand Yang Brightness** (*Yángmíng*)—**Large Intestine** (*dà cháng*)—are of the **Metal** (*jīn*) category.
These two channels belong to the **Lung** and **Large Intestine**, thus classified as **Metal**.
**Foot Lesser Yin** (*zú shǎo yīn*)—**Kidney** (*shèn*)—and **Foot Greater Yang** (*zú Tàiyáng*)—**Bladder** (*pángguāng*)—are of the **Water** (*shuǐ*) category.
These two channels are the **Kidney** and **Bladder**, belonging to **Water**.
**Metal generates Water** (*jīn shēng shuǐ*).
**Lung Metal** (*fèi jīn*) resides above, while **Kidney Water** (*shèn shuǐ*) below is generated by it.
Water flows downward and cannot ascend—thus, it is classified as the **lower division**.
Water has no tendency but to descend; its nature is to flow downward, and if it ascends, it is therefore in the **lower division**.

**Foot Jue Yin** (*zú jué yīn*)—**Liver** (*gān*)—and **Foot Shao Yang** (*shǎo yáng*)—**Gallbladder** (*dǎn*)—are of the **Wood** (*mù*) category.
These two channels are the **Liver** and **Gallbladder**, belonging to **Wood**.
They generate **Hand Greater Yang** (*shǒu Tàiyáng*)—**Heart** (*xīn*)—and **Hand Lesser Yin** (*shǒu shǎo yīn*)—**Small Intestine** (*xiǎo cháng*)—of the **Fire** (*huǒ*) category.
These two channels are the **Heart** and **Small Intestine**, belonging to **Fire**, generated by **Liver Wood**.
Fire ascends and cannot descend—thus, it is classified as the **upper division**.
Fire’s nature is to ascend upward; it cannot descend to generate in the **Foot**, but instead generates **Hand Pericardium** (*xīn bāo luò*)—**Shao Yang Fire** (*shǎo yáng huǒ*), which is the **Fire of the Right Cubit** (*yòu chǐ xiàng huǒ*)—hence, it is in the **upper division**.

**Hand Pericardium** (*shǒu xīn bāo luò*) and **Hand Shao Yang** (*shǒu shǎo yáng*)—both **Fire** (*huǒ*)—
The **Heart** (*xīn*) is the **Sovereign Fire** (*jūn huǒ*), while this is the **Ministerial Fire** (*xiàng huǒ*).
Now, these two channels are the **Pericardium** (*xīn bāo luò*) and **Triple Burner** (*sān jiāo*).
They generate **Foot Lesser Yin** (*zú shǎo yīn*)—**Spleen** (*pí*)—and **Foot Yang Brightness** (*zú Yángmíng*)—**Stomach** (*wèi*)—of the **Earth** (*tǔ*) category.
These two channels are the **Spleen** and **Stomach**, belonging to **Earth**, generated by **Ministerial Fire**.
Thus, they are in the **middle division**.
Earth governs the **Middle Palace** (*zhōng gōng*), hence it is in the **middle division**.
All these are examples of the **Five Phases** (*wǔ xíng*) **parent-child mutual generation and nourishment** (*zǐ mǔ gēng xiāng shēng yǎng*).

To the right: **Cubit Metal** (*jǐn jīn*) generates **Left Inch Water** (*zuǒ cuì shuǐ), Water** generates **Left Guan Wood** (*zuǒ guān mù*), Wood generates **Left Cubit Sovereign Fire** (*zuǒ jǐn jūn huǒ*), Sovereign Fire generates **Right Inch Ministerial Fire** (*yòu cuì xiàng huǒ*), Ministerial Fire generates **Right Guan Earth** (*yòu guān tǔ*)—thus it is said that **parent-child generation** (*zǐ mǔ xiāng shēng*) occurs in the pulse.

**The pulse has three divisions and nine manifestations** (*mài yǒu sān bù jiǔ hòu*), each governing what?
**The pulse has three divisions and nine manifestations** (*mài yǒu sān bù jiǔ hòu*), each belonging to which **Zang-Fu** (*zàng fǔ*)?

Indeed:
The **three divisions** (*sān bù*) are the **cubit, guan, and inch** (*cùn guān chǐ*).
**Cubit division** (*cùn kǒu*), **Yang** (*yáng*); **Guan division** (*guān bù*), **Yin-Yang balance** (*yīn yáng zhī zhōng*); **Inch division** (*chǐ bù*), **Yin** (*yīn*).
The **nine manifestations** (*jiǔ hòu*) are **superficial, middle, and deep** (*fú zhōng shēn*).
Within each of the three divisions, there are **three manifestations** (*sān hòu*), making nine in total—hence it is said that there are **nine manifestations**.

**Superficial** (*fú*) is **Yang** (*yáng*), **deep** (*shēn*) is **Yin** (*yīn*), and **middle** (*zhōng*) is **Stomach qi** (*wèi qì*).
**Superficial** is the **Fu organs** (*fǔ*), **deep** is the **Zang organs** (*zàng*), and **middle** is the pulse of the **Middle Burner** (*zhōng jiāo*).

The **upper division** (*shàng bù*) follows **Heaven** (*tiān*), governing diseases from the **chest upward to the head** (*xiōng yǐshàng zhì tóu*).
The **upper division** is both **hand cubit pulses** (*liǎng shǒu cùn kǒu*), which govern **Heaven’s** presence above.
**Cubit exterior** (*cùn wài*) governs the **head** (*tóu*), **cubit interior** (*cùn nèi*) governs the **chest** (*xiōng*).

The **middle division** (*zhōng bù*) follows **Humanity** (*rén*), governing diseases from the **diaphragm downward to above the navel** (*gé xià zhì qí shàng*).
The **middle division** is the **second finger of both hands’ guan pulses** (*liǎng shǒu guān bù dì èr zhǐ*).
**Half-finger prior** (*bàn zhǐ yǐqián*) governs the **diaphragm below** (*gé xià*), **half-finger posterior** (*bàn zhǐ yǐhòu*) governs **above the navel** (*qí shàng*).

The **lower division** (*xià bù*) follows **Earth** (*dì*), governing diseases from the **navel downward to the feet** (*qí yǐxià zhì zú*).
The **lower division** is the **inch pulse of both hands** (*liǎng shǒu chǐ mài*) and the **third finger** (*dì sān zhǐ*).
**Half-finger prior** governs **below the navel** (*qí xià*), **half-finger posterior** governs the **feet** (*zú*).

This is the method of **examining and ordering** (*shěn chà*) the pulse from **head to foot** (*cóng tóu zhì zú zhī cìshì*).」

Though the original *Yuanwen* text by Zhi (元文刺字) is now superseded by the Shaanxi *Ding* school’s teachings, the following remains:

If a person suffers from **stagnant, long-standing accumulations**, can one discern this through pulse examination?
*—Asking whether stagnant, chronic accumulations can be known through the pulse.*

**Answer:** When examining the right flank, one finds **accumulated qi**.
*—When diagnosing an accumulation disease, if the illness manifests in the right flank, one finds the lung pulse knotted.*

The knotted pulse governs **concrete accumulations**, and since the lung is housed in the right side, a knotted pulse in the lung channel means there is an accumulation in the right flank. The more knotted the pulse, the more severe the accumulation. If the lung channel’s pulse is excessively knotted, the right flank’s accumulation is likewise severe.

If the pulse is **slightly knotted**, the accumulation is slight.
*—If the lung channel’s pulse is not excessively knotted, the right flank’s accumulated qi is likewise mild.*

If no lung pulse is found, yet there is accumulated qi in the right flank—how so?
*—Asking: Though the lung is housed in the right side, and there is an accumulation in the right flank, why is there no knotted pulse in the lung channel?*

**Answer:** Though the lung pulse may not be visibly knotted, the right-hand pulse will be **depressed and submerged**.
*—Even if no knotted pulse appears in the lung channel, if the right-hand pulse is depressed and submerged, it indicates accumulated qi in the right flank.*

A **submerged pulse** (*fù mài*) conceals internal disease accumulations;
A **floating-knotted pulse** (*fú jié*) reveals external organ diseases.

Does the same method apply to external **painful lumps**? Or does it differ?
*—Asking whether the diagnostic method for external stagnant lumps differs from that of right-flank accumulations.*

**Answer:**
A **knotted pulse** (*jié mài*) arrives and departs with intermittent pauses, lacking regularity—this is called *knotted*.
*—A knotted pulse arrives slowly, pausing intermittently without fixed rhythm, hence ‘knotted.’*

A **submerged pulse** (*fù mài*) travels beneath the tendons.
*—A submerged pulse runs deep beneath the tendons, requiring extreme pressure against the bone to detect.*

A **floating pulse** (*fú mài*) travels along the surface of the flesh.
*—A floating pulse moves superficially over the flesh, feeling insufficient upon pressing, yet excessive when lifted.*

The same applies to both left and right sides.
*—The same principles govern left and right flanks.*
Left and right refer to the **left and right flanks**; examining the **external** (*biǎo*) reveals organ diseases, while examining the **internal** (*qiào*) reveals visceral diseases. The methods are consistent as described.

If a pulse is **knotted and submerged**, yet there is no internal accumulation—
*—Though the pulse is knotted and submerged, if there is no internal accumulation, this is a case where the pulse does not correspond to the disease—**a death sentence.***

If a pulse is **floating and knotted**, yet there is no external lump—
*—Though the pulse is floating and knotted, if there is no external stagnant lump, this too is a case where the pulse does not correspond to the disease—**a death sentence.***

If there is an accumulation, yet the pulse is neither knotted nor submerged—
*—If there is an accumulation disease but the pulse is neither knotted nor submerged, this is a case where the pulse does not correspond to the disease—**a death sentence.***

If there is a painful lump, yet the pulse is neither floating nor knotted—
*—If there is a stagnant lump disease but the pulse is neither floating nor knotted, this too is a case where the pulse does not correspond to the disease—**a death sentence.***

Thus, **pulse and disease must correspond**; any discrepancy is **a death sentence**.

---
**The Nineteen Difficulties** say:
The classics state that pulses have **inversion and conformity**.
*—The pulses of men and women exhibit inversion and conformity.*

What does **inversion and conformity** mean? How does **yang embracing yin** or **yin embracing yang** apply?
*—Men and women have their usual pulses, yet sometimes they invert. How so?*

**Answer:**
Men are born in the **Tiger hour (寅)**, the hour of **Wood**, which is **yang**.
*—The original qi arises at the **Rat hour (子)**, the hour of birth. For men, counting thirty cycles from the Rat hour leftward to the Tiger hour (寅) takes ten months—thus, men are born in the tenth month. The Tiger hour belongs to the **eastern Wood direction (甲乙木)**, which is yang.*

Women are born in the **Applicability hour (申)**, the hour of **Metal**, which is **yin**.
*—For women, counting thirty cycles from the Rat hour rightward to the Applicability hour (申) also takes ten months—thus, women are born in the tenth month. The Applicability hour belongs to the **western Metal direction (庚辛金)**, which is yin.*

Thus, **men’s pulses are found above the *guan* (关)**.
*—The region above the *guan* is the *cun* (寸) pulse, which belongs to yang. Since men’s yang qi is abundant, their pulses are typically strong at the *cun* position.*

Women’s pulses are found **below the *guan***.
*—The region below the *guan* is the *chi* (尺) pulse, which belongs to yin. Since women’s yin qi is abundant, their pulses are typically strong at the *chi* position.*

Thus, **men’s *chi* pulses are always weak**.
*—Men’s qi originates in *Shao Yang* (少阳) and peaks in *Tai Yang* (太阳). When *Shao Yang* arrives, the pulse is alternately large and small, short and long—this corresponds to the *Yijing* **Gùn (履) hexagram**, where one yang resides in the *chi* position. When *Yang Ming* (阳明) arrives, the pulse is floating, large, and short—this corresponds to the *Yijing* **Lín (临) hexagram**, where two yangs reside in the *guan* position. When *Tai Yang* arrives, the pulse is robust and long—this corresponds to the *Yijing* **Tài (泰) hexagram**, where three yangs reside in the *cun* position. Since the three yangs rise from Earth, men’s pulses are strong at the *cun* and weak at the *chi*.*

Women’s *chi* pulses are always strong.
*—Women’s qi originates in *Tai Yin* (太阴) and peaks in *Jué Yin* (厥阴). When *Tai Yin* arrives, the pulse is taut, large, and long—this corresponds to the *Yijing* **Gòu (姤) hexagram**, where one yin resides in the *cun* position. When *Shao Yin* (少阴) arrives, the pulse is taut, fine, and subtle—this corresponds to the *Yijing* **Dùn (遁) hexagram**, where two yins reside in the *guan* position. When *Jué Yin* arrives, the pulse is submerged, short, and firm—this corresponds to the *Yijing* **Pǐ (否) hexagram**, where three yins reside in the *chi* position. Since the three yins descend from Heaven, women’s pulses are submerged at the *cun* and strong at the *chi*.*

**Inversion** occurs when a man exhibits a woman’s pulse or a woman exhibits a man’s.
*—If a man’s *chi* pulse, which is usually weak, becomes strong, or if a woman’s *chi* pulse, which is usually strong, becomes weak—what does this indicate?*

**Answer:**
If a man exhibits a woman’s pulse, it signifies **deficiency**, and the disease lies **internally**.
*—The *cun* pulse is the ‘day yang’ (*rì yáng*). Since men’s physiology relies on yang, if the yang pulse is not found in the *cun* but instead appears in the *chi*—where women’s pulses are typically strong—this indicates insufficiency. Since yin governs the internal, the disease is internal.*

---

If the pulse is found on the **left**, the disease lies on the **left**.
*—When the left hand exhibits a woman’s pulse as strong, the disease will be on the body’s left side.*
If the pulse is found on the **right**, the disease lies on the **right**.
*—When the right hand exhibits a woman’s pulse as strong, the disease will be on the body’s right side.*
**The pulse determines the location of the disease.**
*—The disease manifests where the abnormal pulse is detected.*

If a woman exhibits a man’s pulse, it signifies **excess**.
*—The *chi* pulse in women is naturally floating; if it further appears in the *cun*—where men’s pulses are typically strong—this is called **excess**.*
Moreover, since women’s physiology relies on **yin**, their *cun* pulse is usually moderate. If instead a floating pulse is detected,
*—the disease lies in the four limbs.*
The four limbs are the origin of all **yang**; when a woman exhibits a man’s pulse, it indicates **yang qi** is excessive, hence the disease is in the four limbs.

If the pulse is found on the **left**,
*—when the left hand exhibits a man’s pulse, the disease will be on the left hand and foot.*
if the pulse is found on the **right**,
*—when the right hand exhibits a man’s pulse, the disease will be on the right hand and foot.*
**the pulse determines the location of the disease!**

**Twenty Difficulties** state:
The Classics say pulses have **latency**.
*—Pulses may be hidden or concealed.*
Where are they concealed, and how can one speak of **latency**?
*—In which channel or organ does the pulse hide, allowing one to diagnose **latency**?*
**Answer:**
It refers to **yin** and **yang** **mutually counteracting**—**mutually concealing** each other.
*—The *cun* is not solely **yang**, nor the *chi* solely **yin**; even the upper muscles are **yang**, and the lower muscles are **yin**. Sometimes **yang** pulses invade **yin** positions, or **yin** pulses invade **yang** positions, or **yang** pulses conceal **yin**, or **yin** pulses conceal **yang**.*

When a pulse resides in a **yin** position but **yang** characteristics appear,
*—this is called **yang counteracting yin**.*
the inner *chi* (below the muscles) is **yin**—if the pulse there is floating, slippery, and long,
*—it indicates **yin** showing **yang** characteristics; when **yin** is deficient and **yang** invades, it is called **yang covering** or **yang counteracting yin**.*
even if the pulse is **#19 deep, slow, and short**,
*—this is called **yang concealing yin**.*
if the *chi* pulse is already floating, slippery, and long, but the *cun* (and *guan*) is moderate, short, and slow,
*—this is **yang concealing yin**.*

When a pulse resides in a **yang** position but **yin** characteristics appear,
*—this is called **yin counteracting yang**.*
the *cun* and upper muscles are **yang**—if instead a **#20 deep, short, and slow** **yin** pulse is detected,
*—it is **yin** invading **yang** positions, or **yin overflowing**.*
even if the pulse is sometimes floating, slippery, and long,
*—this is called **yin concealing yang**.*
if the *cun* and *guan* pulse is moderate, slow, and short, but the *chi* pulse is floating, slippery, and long,
*—this is **yin concealing yang**.*

**Double yang** manifests as **mania**.
*—When the pulse is floating, slippery, and long, and further exhibits **fullness and rapidity**—this is called **double yang**—hence the patient raves about grand matters, boasts of their own virtues, acts wildly, and climbs heights in disarray.*
**Double yin** manifests as **epilepsy**.
*—When the *chi* pulse is moderate, short, and slow, and further exhibits **fullness and excess**—this is called **double yin**—hence the patient stiffens and collapses to the ground, closes their eyes without waking, and only regains consciousness after **yin** has exhausted and **yang** returns. This is named **epilepsy**.*

**Yang loss** causes **phantom visions**.
*—**Yang loss** means absence of **yang qi**; when the *cun* pulse is extremely fine and weak, the patient may falsely see apparitions as if encountering ghosts.*
**Yin loss** causes **blindness**.
*—**Yin** is the **essence-qi**; when **yin qi** is lost, the five **zang** organs’ **qi** no longer nourishes the eyes, resulting in blindness. If the *chi* pulse is extremely fine and weak...*

**Twenty-One Difficulties** state:
The Classics say that when a person’s **form** is diseased but their pulse is not,
*—it is called **life**.*
**Form** being diseased means the body is emaciated, and the hands and feet are numb;
*—the pulse, however, does not exhibit disease—it rises and falls with breathing, without excessive deviation or irregularity. This is diagnosed as **life**.*
When the pulse is diseased but the **form** is not,
*—it is called **death**.*
If the pulse is diseased while the **form** is still undiseased,
*—even if the person does not yet suffer from colds, fevers, or other illnesses, they will die regardless.*

**What is meant by this?**
*—How does the distinction between **life** and **death** arise?*
**Answer:**
When a person’s **form** is diseased but their pulse is not,
*—it is not that the pulse is truly undiseased.*
Though the body may show signs of illness or pain, the pulse does not yet exhibit abnormal rhythms—this is not because the pulse is undiseased,
*—but because the pulse’s rhythm does not yet correspond to the severity of the form’s illness.*
This is the **great principle**.
*—The pulse is the movement of **blood**; breathing is the movement of **qi**. The pulse does not move on its own but is driven by **qi**; when the rhythm of **qi**’s breathing does not align with the pulse’s rhythm, this is the reason.*

**Twenty-Two Difficulties** state:
The Classics say the pulse has **abnormal movement**.
*—There are pulses that deviate from their usual rhythm,*
**which give rise to disease.**
*—abnormal movement is the source of disease.*
If one pulse becomes two diseases,
*—how does this occur?*
**How does the pulse’s abnormal movement transform into two types of disease—those of **blood** and **qi**?**
**Answer:**
The Classics refer to **abnormal movement** as **qi**.
*—**Qi** is **yang**; **yang** governs the **defensive-qi** (*wèi*). When **evil** invades **yang**, **qi** first receives **heat**, manifesting in the pulse as **abnormal movement**.*
The disease it generates is **blood**.
*—When **qi** is diseased, it transmits to **blood**; **blood** is **yin**; **yin** governs the **nutritive-qi** (*yíng*). When **blood** stagnates and fails to nourish, disease arises later.*

When **evil** is in **qi**,
*—**yang** is **qi**; when **evil** invades **yang**, **qi** moves abnormally in the pulse.*
**abnormal movement** is **qi**.
When **evil** is in **blood**,
*—**blood** is the disease that arises.*
**qi** is diseased, transmitting to **blood**; **blood** stagnates and fails to flow, giving rise to disease.

**Qi** governs **tightness**.
*—**Tightness** (*jū*) refers to the pulse’s **floating-slippery** rhythm, as **qi** flows without obstruction.*
**Blood** governs **moistening**.
*—**Moistening** (*rú*) means **nourishment**; when **blood** fails to nourish, it is called **dryness** (*zāo*).*
When **qi** stagnates and fails to flow,
*—this is **qi** being diseased first.*
When **qi** and **blood** are heated, they overflow and move erratically, hence **abnormal movement**.
*—If **evil wind** assaults them, **qi** will stagnate and fail to flow.*
When **blood** stagnates and fails to **moisten**,
*—this is **blood** being diseased later.*
**Qi** transmits the **evil** to **blood**, which is then further assaulted by **evil wind**, so **blood** stagnates and fails to **moisten**, causing disease.

Thus, **abnormal movement** arises first,
*—and the disease follows afterward.*
**Qi** first exhibits **abnormal movement** in the pulse,
*—then **blood** generates the disease.*

The Twenty-Third Difficulty states:
The **Three Yin and Three Yang** of the hands and feet.
Hand **Three Yin**, hand **Three Yang**, foot **Three Yin**, foot **Three Yang**.
Can the **measurements** of the **pulse-vessels** be understood?
*—The **classical texts** (*Jīng*) and **pulse-registers** (*Tóng*) describe the length and breadth; can this be understood?*

Indeed:
The **Three Yang** **pulse-vessels** of the hands,
*—from the hands to the head,*
—span five *cùn* (a traditional Chinese unit of measurement), and five times six equals three *zhàng* (a traditional Chinese unit of measurement).

The **Hand Taiyang** **pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—originating at the lateral side of both small fingers,*
—ascends along the arm to the front of the **ear-jewel** (*ěrzhūzǐ*),
*—spanning five *zhàng*,*
—and both hands together measure one *zhàng*.
The **Hand Yangming** **pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—arising from the lateral side of the index fingers of both hands,*
—ascends along the arm and terminates at the **nose**,
*—left to right, right to left,*
—spanning five *zhàng*, and both hands together measure one *zhàng*.
The **Hand Shaoyang** **pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—originating at the lateral side of both ring fingers,*
—ascends along the arm and terminates at the front of the **ear**,
*—spanning five *zhàng*,*
—and both hands together measure one *zhàng*,
*—hence the total of five times six equals three *zhàng*.*

The **Three Yin** **pulse-vessels** of the hands,
*—from the hands to the chest,*
—span three *zhàng* and five *cùn* (三尺五寸),
*—three times six equals one *zhàng* and eight *zhàng*,*
*—five times six equals three *zhàng*,*
*—totaling two *zhàng* and one *zhàng* (二丈一尺).*

The **Hand Shaoyin** **pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—originating in the **heart** (*xīn*),*
—descends to connect with the **small intestine** (*xiǎocháng*),
*—ascends to the **lungs** (*fèi*),*
—emerges below the **axillary region** (*yèxià*),
*—follows the arm,*
—and exits at the lateral side of the **small finger** (*xiǎozhǐ*),
*—spanning three *zhàng* and five *cùn*,*
—and both hands together measure seven *zhàng* (*cùn*).
The **Hand Taiyin** **pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—originating in the **middle burner** (*zhōngjiāo*),*
—descends to connect with the **large intestine** (*dàcháng*),
*—returns to follow the **stomach** (*wèi*),*
—belongs to the **lungs** (*fèi*),
*—emerges below the axillary region,*
—descends to the **elbow** (*zhǒu*),*
—enters the **wrist pulse orifice** (*cùnkǒu*),*
—ascends to the **fish boundary** (*yújì*),*
—and exits at the lateral side of the **thumb** (*dàzhǐ*),
*—spanning three *zhàng* and five *cùn*,*
—and both hands together measure seven *zhàng*.
The **Hand Jueyin** **pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—originating in the **chest** (*xiōngzhōng*),*
—belongs to the **heart-wrapper** (*xīnpàogé*), **Sanjiao** (*Sānjiāo*),*
—emerges below the **rib-axillary region** (*xiēyèxià*),
*—follows the upper arm,*
—enters the **elbow** (*zhǒu*),*
—descends to exit at the lateral side of the **ring finger** (*xiǎozhǐ*),
*—spanning three *zhàng* and five *cùn*,*
—and both hands together measure seven *zhàng*,
*—hence the total of two *zhàng* and one *zhàng*.*

The **Three Yang** **pulse-vessels** of the feet,
*—from the feet to the head,*
—span eight *zhàng* (八尺), and six times eight equals four *zhàng* and eight *zhàng* (四丈八尺).

The **Foot Taiyang** **pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—originating at the lateral side of both small toes,*
—ascends along the **knee** (*xī*),*
—crosses the **buttock** (*bǐnzhōng*),*
—follows the back up to the head,
*—and descends to enter the **inner canthus** (*mùnèipí*),*
*—spanning eight *zhàng*,*
—and both feet ascending measure one *zhàng* and six *zhàng*.
The **Foot Yangming** **pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—originating at the lateral side of the second toe of both feet,*
—follows the **foot meridian** (*jīng*),*
—ascends alongside the **umbilicus** (*qià*), two *cùn* lateral on either side,
*—and terminates at the **forehead** (*éjiǎo*) and **hairline** (*fàjí*),*
*—spanning eight *zhàng*,*
—and both feet together measure one *zhàng* and six *zhàng*.
The **Foot Shaoyang** **pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—originating at the lateral side of the fourth toe of both feet,*
—ascends along the **outer side of both knees** (*liǎngxīwàikāng*),*
—enters the **flank** (*jìxié*),*
—ascends to follow the **outer canthus** (*mùwàipí*),*
*—spanning eight *zhàng*,*
—and both feet together measure one *zhàng* and six *zhàng*,
*—hence the total of four *zhàng* and eight *zhàng*.*

The **Three Yin** **pulse-vessels** of the feet,
*—from the feet to the chest,*
—span six *zhàng* and five *cùn* (六尺五寸),
*—six times six equals three *zhàng* and six *zhàng*,*
*—five times six equals three *zhàng*,*
*—totaling three *zhàng* and nine *zhàng* (三丈九尺).*
*—Both feet together contain six **Yin pulse-vessels** (*yīntà*), hence six times six equals three *zhàng* and six *zhàng*.*

The **Foot Taiyin** **pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—originating at the medial side of the big toe,*
—follows the **inner side of the foot meridian** (*jīngnèikāng*),*
—ascends to cross the **Hand Jueyin pulse-vessel** (*xiāngqián*),*
—ascends along the **abdomen** (*fù*),*
—belongs to the **spleen** (*pí*) and connects with the **stomach** (*wèi*),*
—links to the **root of the tongue** (*shétígēn*),*
*—spanning seven *zhàng* and five *cùn* (七尺五寸),*
—and both sides measure one *zhàng* and five *zhàng* (一丈五尺).

The **Foot Jueyin** **pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—originating above the **big toe’s hairline** (*jùmáozhōngshàng*),*
—follows the **upper medial side of the foot** (*fùshùshàngkāng*),*
*—one *cùn* from the **inner ankle** (*nèihuái*),*
—eight *cùn* above the **ankle** (*huái*),*
—crosses the **Foot Taiyin pulse-vessel** (*xiānghòu*),*
—follows the **abdomen** (*fù*),*
—enters the **pubic hair** (*yīnmáo*),*
—circles the **genitals** (*yīnqì*),*
—reaches the **lower abdomen** (*xiǎofù*),*
—lateral to the **stomach** (*wèi*) and belongs to the **liver** (*gān*),*
—connects with the **gallbladder** (*dǎn*),*
—follows the **throat** (*hónglǒng*),*
—enters the **crown of the head** (*shàngkē*),*
—links to the **eyes** (*mù*),*
—and exits at the **forehead** (*éjiǎo*),*
*—spanning six *zhàng* and five *cùn*,*
—and both sides together measure one *zhàng* and three *zhàng* (一丈三尺).

The **Foot Shaoyin** **pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—originating below the **small toe** (*xiǎozhǐ*),*
—ascends obliquely to the **sole of the foot** (*zúxīn*),*
—ascends along the **inner thigh** (*yángùnèi*),*
—pierces the **spine** (*bèi*),*
—belongs to the **kidneys** (*shèn*),*
—connects with the **urinary bladder** (*pángguāng*),*
—pierces the **liver** (*gān*),*
—enters the **lungs** (*fèi*),*
—follows the **throat** (*hónglǒng*),*
—lateral to the **root of the tongue** (*shétígēn*),*
*—spanning six *zhàng* and five *cùn*,*
—and both sides together measure one *zhàng* and three *zhàng*,
*—hence the total of three *zhàng* and nine *zhàng*.*

In humans, the **two feet’s tender pulse-vessels** (*jiāomài*),
*—from the feet to the eyes,*
—span seven *zhàng* and five *cùn* (七尺五寸),
*—two times seven equals one *zhàng* and four *zhàng* (一丈四尺),*
*—two times five equals one *zhàng* (一尺),*
*—totaling one *zhàng* and five *zhàng* (一丈五尺).*

Humans possess **Yin-tread** (*yīntà*) and **Yang-tread** (*yángtà*) **two pulse-vessels** (*liǎngmài*).
*—The two feet together contain four **pulse-vessels** (*sìmài*).*

The **Yang-tread** **pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—originating at the **heel** (*gēnzhōng*),*
—ascends along the **outer ankle** (*wàihuái*) upward,
*—and enters the **Wind Pool** (*fēngchí*).*

The **Yin-tread** **pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—also originating at the heel,*
—is the **separate collateral** (*biéluò*) of the **Foot Shaoyin** **pulse-vessel**,
*—behind the **sacrum** (*zìrán*),*
—ascends above the **inner ankle** (*nèihuái*),*
—straight upward along the **inner thigh** (*yángùnèi*),*
—enters the **Yin** (*yīn*),*
—follows the **abdomen** (*fù*) upward to the **chest** (*xiōngguó*),*
—enters the **sternal notch** (*quēpén*),*
—ascends to exit at the **human’s forehead** (*rénwèngqián*),*
—enters the **inner lateral side of the neck** (*xiàngnèikāng*),*
—belongs to the **inner canthus** (*mùnèipí*),*
—and joins the **Foot Taiyang pulse-vessel** (*Tóng*),
*—spanning seven *zhàng* and five *cùn*,*
—and both sides together measure one *zhàng* and five *zhàng*.

The **Governing Vessel** (*Dūmài*) and **Conception Vessel** (*Rènmài*),
*—each spanning four *zhàng* and five *cùn* (四尺五寸),*
*—two times four equals eight *zhàng* (八尺),*
*—two times five equals one *zhàng* (一尺),*
*—totaling nine *zhàng* (九尺).*

The **Governing Vessel** (*Dūmài*)
*—originates at the **Lower Extremity Acupuncture Point** (*xiàjízhīyú*),*
—ascends alongside the **spine** (*bèi*),*
—enters and belongs to the **brain** (*nǎo*),*
*—spanning four *zhàng* and five *cùn*.*

The **Conception Vessel** (*Rènmài*)
*—originates below the **Middle Extremity Acupuncture Point** (*zhōngjízhīyú*),*
—ascends to the **pubic hairline** (*shàngmáojì*),*
—follows the **abdomen** (*fù*) upward to the **Cinnabar Elbow** (*guānyán*),*
—reaches the **throat** (*yànhóu*),*
*—spanning four *zhàng* and five *cùn*.*

The combined length of the **Governing Vessel** (*Dūmài*) and **Conception Vessel** (*Rènmài*) totals nine *zhàng*.

In all, the **pulse-vessels** span sixteen *zhàng* and two *cùn* (一十六丈二尺).
*—The **Three Yang pulse-vessels** of the hands total three *zhàng*,*
*—the **Three Yin pulse-vessels** of the hands total two *zhàng* and one *zhàng*,*
*—the **Three Yang pulse-vessels** of the feet total four *zhàng* and eight *zhàng*,*
*—the **Three Yin pulse-vessels** of the feet total three *zhàng* and nine *zhàng*,*
*—the **two feet’s tread pulse-vessels** total one *zhàng* and five *zhàng*,*
*—and the **Governing Vessel** (*Dūmài*) and **Conception Vessel** (*Rènmài*) total nine *zhàng*,*
*—summing to sixteen *zhàng* and two *cùn*.*

This is what is called the **lengths of the Twelve Meridian Pulse-Vessels** (*shí’èrjīngmàichángduǎn*).
*—The above are the lengths of the Twelve Meridian Pulse-Vessels.*

There are **Twelve Meridians** (*jīng*).
*—See the First Difficulty for annotations.*

There are **Fifteen Collaterals** (*luò*).
*—Each meridian has one collateral,*
—and outside the **Twelve Meridian Collaterals** (*shí’èrluò*), there are the **Yang Collaterals** (*yángluò*), **Yin Collaterals** (*yīnluò*), and the **Great Collateral of the Spleen** (*pílù*), totaling **Fifteen Collaterals** (*shíwǔluò*).*

「Where does it begin and where does it end?
*—Asking about the starting and terminating points of the Meridians and Collaterals.*
Yet the Meridians and Collaterals are those **Dao/Way**s that conduct **qi** and blood,
*—and harmonize Yin and Yang*—
to nourish the body.

**Answer:** The Meridians are the **direct paths** (*jìng*), and the Meridian Pulse-Vessels are the **channels** (*mài*) that serve as the **Dao/Way**s for the circulation of **qi** and blood,
*—the pathways that disperse and harmonize Yin and Yang*—
to nourish and flourish the entire body.
Their origins trace back to the **Middle Burner** (*Zhōngjiāo*).
*—The starting point of the Meridians and Collaterals is the Middle Burner,*
located between the two nipples,
*—named the **Sovereign Center Acupoint** (*Shànzhōngxué*), also called the **Sea of Qi** (*Qìhǎi*),*
for **qi** arises from here.

*—Annotations:*
The **Hand Taiyin** (*Shǒutàiyīn*) and **Yangming** (*Yángmíng*)*
*—are linked to the Lungs and Large Intestine.*
The **Yangming** refers to the **Foot Yangming** (*Zúyángmíng*) and **Taiyin** (*Tàiyīn*).
*—From the Large Intestine, it proceeds to the Stomach and Spleen.*
The **Taiyin** refers to the **Hand Shaoyin** (*Shǎoyīn*) and **Taiyang** (*Tàiyáng*).
*—From the Spleen, it proceeds to the Heart and Small Intestine.*
The **Taiyang** refers to the **Foot Taiyang** (*Zútàiyáng*) and **Shaoyin** (*Shǎoyīn*).
*—From the Small Intestine, it proceeds to the Bladder and Kidneys.*
The **Shaoyin** refers to the **Hand Jinxu** (*Jīnxǔ*) and **Shaoyang** (*Shǎoyáng*).
*—From the Kidneys, it proceeds to the **Pericardium** (*Xīnbāoluò*) and **Sanjiao** (*Sānjiāo*).*
The **Shaoyang** refers to the **Foot Shaoyang** (*Zúshǎoyáng*) and **Jueyin** (*Juéyīn*).
*—From the Sanjiao, it proceeds to the Gallbladder and Liver.*
The **Jueyin** returns to annotate the **Hand Taiyin**.
*—The blood and qi terminate at the Liver,*
on the **next day** (*#24 lǐngrì*) at the **auspicious time** (*#25 liángshí*),*
*—then return to begin again at the Lungs.*

The **Fifteen Collaterals** (*#26 shíwǔluò*)
*—all originate from their respective Meridians.*
These **Fifteen Collaterals** are all derived from the **Twelve Meridians** as their **root origins** (*běnyuán*),*
*—to mutually flow and communicate,*
like an endless circle.
*—The Meridians and Collaterals end and then begin anew,*
just as a ring revolves in perfect circular motion,
*—without any fixed starting point.*
They **irrigate and nourish** one another.
*—The Meridians and Collaterals flow in cycles,*
mutually **irrigating and nourishing** within their networks.
At dawn, they **converge at the radial pulse** (*cùnkǒu*)—
*—the grand assembly of the Pulse-Vessels,*
where the **Hand Taiyin** (*Shǒutàiyīn*) **Pulse-Vessel** (*mài*) manifests—
*—to govern a hundred diseases and determine life and death.*

The **radial pulse** (*cùnkǒu*)
*—is the grand assembly of the Pulse-Vessels,*
where the **Hand Taiyin** (*Shǒutàiyīn*) **Pulse-Vessel** (*mài*) moves.
*—Thus, any ailments of the Five Zang and Six Fu*
are all reflected in the **qi**.
*—Then one can judge the auspicious or inauspicious,*
life or death.
*—Moreover, the annual cycles of Yin and Yang rising and falling*
converge on the **first day of Spring** (*Lìchūn*) of each year,
*—while the Yin and Yang of day and night converge at the auspicious time,*
and the **Honor and Defense** (*róngwèi*) **system** of the body returns to the **Hand Taiyin** (*Shǒutàiyīn*).
*—This aligns with the Heavenly measure of 13,500 breaths,*
and according to the **root Meridian** (*běnjīng*), it converges at the **radial pulse** (*cùn*) and **radial artery** (*rényíng*).
*—The radial artery, located on the left hand,*
belongs to **Shaoyin** (*Shǎoyīn*),*
*—and is indeed the place where the Meridian Pulse-Vessels converge at dawn.*
I have revised the lower text to read: **"converge at the radial pulse" (*cùnkǒu*)—the Sea of Qi** (*qìjiāng*),*
*—let the readers judge for themselves.*

The **sutra** says:
*—"To clearly know the beginning and end,*
the cycles of Yin and Yang are fixed.*
*—The **qi** of the Yang Meridians begins at the Lungs and ends at the Liver,*
and separately from the Yang,
*—one knows the source of disease.*
Separately from the Yin,
*—one knows the period of life and death.*

What does this mean?
*—How should this be understood?*

Thus:

The **beginning and end**—these are the **pulse’s** (*mài*) **cycles**. The subtle and wondrous lies in the **radial pulse-vessel** (*cùntǒng*), which must not be overlooked. To examine it, one follows the **Meridian Classics**, beginning with **Yin and Yang**. Therefore, the **radial pulse-vessel’s** framework truly lies in the **Yin and Yang of beginning and end, ending and beginning**.

The **radial pulse** (*cùnkǒu*)—the **Qi Mouth** (*qìkǒu*)—where **Yin and Yang qi** converge at dawn#27, like an unbroken ring, hence called **"beginning."** The **Yin and Yang qi**, from the early dawn’s auspicious hour, converge at the **radial pulse** (*cùnkǒu*)—like an unbroken ring, cycling endlessly, hence called **"beginning."**

According to the original text’s **radial pulse** (*cùn*), **radial artery** (*rényíng*), I presumptuously revised it to **Qi Mouth** (*qìkǒu*).

**"End"** refers to the **Three Yin and Three Yang Meridians** ceasing—when they cease, death follows, each with its own **manifest signs**, hence called **"end."**

The **manifest signs** of **Foot Shaoyin** (*Zú Shǎoyīn*) **qi** ceasing:
—**teeth lengthen and wither**, flesh **softens and recedes**.

The **manifest signs** of **Foot Taiyin** (*Zú Tàiyīn*) **qi** ceasing:
—**flesh swells and retracts**.

The **manifest signs** of **Foot Jueyin** (*Zú Juéyīn*) **qi** ceasing:
—**tongue root shrinks**.

The **manifest signs** of **Hand Taiyin** (*Shǒu Tàiyīn*) **qi** ceasing:
—**skin withers, hair snaps**.

The **manifest signs** of **Hand Shaoyin** (*Shǒu Shǎoyīn*) **qi** ceasing:
—**face darkens like a pear**.

The **manifest signs** of the **Three Yin** **qi** ceasing:
—**eyes dazzle and gleam**.

The **manifest signs** of the **Six Yang** **qi** ceasing:
—**sweat beads like pearls**, hence called **"death each has its form,"** **"end" is death itself**.

*(Note: In the *Huangdi Eighty-One Difficulties Classic*, the diagram’s third volume #1—according to the text’s meaning—likely should read **"take"** instead of *"foot."*)*

---
#2 **Zá** (*Zá*): The *Jízhù* (*Collection of Commentaries*) **16th Difficulty**’s **Dīng** (*丁*) commentary translates it as **"follow"** (*xún*).

#3 **Bù** (*不*): According to the text’s meaning, it likely should read **"door-wood"** (*ménmù*).

#4 **Liver and Kidney below, Spleen** (*Gān Shèn zài xià Pí*): The *Jízhù* **16th Difficulty**’s **Yú** (*虞*) commentary renders it as **"the Liver and Kidney govern the lower body"** (*yī Gān Shèn zhǔ xià bù*).

#5 **Cǐ** (*此*): Following the pattern of the preceding passage, it likely should be **"Liver"** (*Gān*).

#6 **Shànwèi** (*善味*): Earlier in the *Běn Yì* (*Original Meanings*) and *Jízhù*, the phrase reads **"good reflections"** (*shànsī*), with the *Jízhù* further noting: **"The Spleen, in terms of *zhì* (*志/intent*), is reflection"** (*Pí zhě, zài zhì wéi sī yě*).

#7 **Qiàn** (*欠*): The *Běn Yì* reads **"good yawns"** (*shànqiàn*).

#8 **Bàn tòng** (*半痛*): Following the precedent of the earlier text, **"half-pain"** should be translated as **"tenacious and painful"** (*láo ér tòng*).

#9 **Wáng** (*王*): Based on the context, it likely should be **"five"** (*wǔ*).

#10 **Chǐ** (*尺*): Doubtful; possibly should read **"great"** (*dà*).

#11 **Néng** (*能*): The *Běn Yì* and *Jízhù* render it as **"closed"** (*bì*).

#12 **Zài** (*在*): According to the text’s meaning, it likely should be **"madness"** (*kuáng*).

#13 **Dàcháng** (*大肠*): Originally **"Hand Taiyang"** (*Shǒu Tàiyáng*), corrected per context.

#14 **Gù** (*故*): Earlier in the *Běn Yì* and *Jízhù*, the phrase reads **"Earth governs the Central Palace"** (*tǔ zhǔ zhōnggōng*).

#15 **Zuǒ** (*左*): Originally **"in"** (*zài*), corrected per context.

#16 **Zhěn** (*诊*): The *Běn Yì* and *Jízhù* render it as **"bound"** (*jié*).

#17 **Qì** (*气*): Below this, the text likely omits the word **"flourishing"** (*shèng*).

#18 **Shèng** (*盛*): Below this, the *Běn Yì* and *Jízhù* add: **"This is its constant state"** (*yī shì qí cháng yě*).

#19 **Suī** (*虽*): Below this, the *Běn Yì* and *Jízhù* include the word **"season"** (*shí*).

#20 **Tài** (*太*): Originally **"not"** (*bù*), corrected per meaning.

#21 **Gè** (*各*): Doubtful; possibly should read **"united"** (*hé*).

#22 **Èr** (*二*): Originally **"[omitted]"** (*[rú]*), corrected per *Běn Yì* and *Jízhù*.

#23 **Rén** (*人*): Originally **"now"** (*jīn*), corrected per context.

#24 **Rì** (*日*): Originally **"eyes"** (*mù*), corrected per meaning.

#25 **Liángshí** (*良时*): Doubtful; possibly should read **"Gèn Time"** (*Gèn shí*), as seen in the **30th** and **37th Difficulties**’ commentaries.

#26 **Cháo** (*朝*): Originally **"Spleen"** (*Pí*), corrected per *Běn Yì* and *Jízhù*.

#27 **Cháo** (*朝*): Below this, the *Běn Yì* and *Jízhù* add the word **"uses"** (*shǐ*).

---
*(Yellow Emperor’s Eighty-One Difficulties Classic: Annotated Diagram and Sentence Explanations, Volume Four—Composed by Lúguó* (*卢国*) *Qín Yuèrén* (秦越人) *and Revised by Lǐ Xiàn* (李駉) *and Yě Zǐ* (子野))

**Difficulty Twenty-Four** states:

When the **qi** of the **three Yin** and **three Yang** channels of the hands and feet has already ceased, how can one determine the prognosis? Can one thereby discern its **virtue/power** (吉凶) and fate?

The **three Yin** and **three Yang** channels of the hands, and the **three Yin** and **three Yang** channels of the feet—when their **qi** ceases at death, what are the signs by which one may determine the prognosis? Can one thereby know their **virtue/power** (吉凶) and life or death?

Thus: When the **qi** of the **foot minor Yin** ceases, the bones wither.

In reply: The **foot minor Yin** is the **kidney** channel, which internally nourishes the bone marrow. When the **kidney** **qi** ceases, the marrow follows suit, withering and drying. The **foot minor Yin** is the channel of winter. The **kidney**, belonging to winter, moves in concealment yet warms the marrow. If the marrow lacks warmth, the flesh no longer clings to the bone. When bone and flesh are estranged, the flesh becomes flaccid and retracted. The flesh flaccid and retracted, thus the teeth grow long and wither. The teeth are the bone’s remnant. The flesh that supports the teeth withers, causing the teeth to lengthen and dry—this is called teeth being parched and lacking luster. The hair lacks moisture. The brain is the marrow’s sea, and the **kidney** governs the marrow. The hair is what the brain nourishes, so its splendor resides in the hair. Now that the marrow has withered, the hair lacks moisture. Hair without moisture is a sign that the bones die first.

On the **5th day** (戊), the condition worsens; on the **6th day** (己), death occurs. **5th** and **6th** are **Earth**; the **kidney** is **Water**. **Earth** can overcome **Water**, hence the saying: "On the **5th day**, the condition worsens; on the **6th day**, death occurs."

When the **qi** of the **foot major Yin** ceases, the pulse fails to nourish the lips. The **foot major Yin** is the **spleen** channel, the **spleen**’s internal organ. Its flesh is the lips, for the **spleen**’s **virtue/power** (华) lies in the four white sections of the lips, and its abundance fills the flesh. The lips are the termination of the flesh. The **spleen** internally nourishes the flesh and externally manifests in the lips. If the pulse fails to nourish, the flesh becomes rough and lacks luster. If the blood and flesh fail to nourish, the flesh becomes coarse and unrefined, lacking luster. When the flesh is coarse and lacks luster, the **spleen** becomes distended. The flesh distended, the lips invert. When the lips invert, the flesh dies first. Lips inverted and lacking luster are the signs of the flesh’s death. On the **1st day** (甲), the condition worsens; on the **2nd day** (乙), death occurs. **1st** and **2nd** are **Wood**; the **spleen** is **Earth**. **Wood** can overcome **Earth**, hence the saying: "On the **1st day**, the condition worsens; on the **2nd day**, death occurs."

When the **qi** of the **foot jue minor Yin** ceases, the tendons contract, drawing the scrotum upward while the tongue rolls back. The **foot jue minor Yin** is the **liver** channel, which governs the tendons. All human movement is the work of tendon **qi**. It also ascends to the tongue and descends to the scrotum. When the **liver** **qi** ceases, all tendons contract and stiffen, causing the scrotum to retract and the tongue to roll inward. The **jue minor Yin** is the **liver** channel. The **liver** is the internal organ of tendons. The **liver**’s essence **qi** nourishes the tendons. The tendons converge at the **yin** organ. The vertical tendons above and below the horizontal bone in the **yin** hair are called the **central tendons** (宗筋), hence the tendons converge at the **yin** organ. They also network at the tongue’s root. The **liver**’s collateral vessels branch from the tongue’s base. If the pulse fails to nourish, the tendons contract and stiffen. If the **liver**’s **qi** fails to nourish, the tendons shorten and stiffen. The tendons contracted and stiffened, the scrotum and tongue follow. When the tendons contract, the scrotum and tongue are drawn inward. Thus, the tongue rolls back and the scrotum retracts—this is the tendons dying first. A rolled tongue and retracted scrotum are the signs of the tendons’ death. On the **7th day** (庚), the condition worsens; on the **8th day** (辛), death occurs. **7th** and **8th** are **Metal**; the **liver** is **Wood**. **Metal** can overcome **Wood**, hence the saying: "On the **7th day**, the condition worsens; on the **8th day**, death occurs."

When the **qi** of the **hand major Yin** ceases, the skin and hair scorch. The **hand major Yin** is the **lung** channel, which governs the skin and nourishes the hair and complexion. When the **lung** **qi** ceases, the skin and hair scorch and wither. The **hand major Yin** is the **lung**. The **lung** belongs to the **hand major Yin**, warming the skin and hair with its moving **qi**. If the **qi** fails to nourish, the skin and hair scorch. When the skin and hair scorch, the **fluids** depart. The **fluids** that regulate excretion and sweat are called **jin**; those that permeate the hollow spaces are called **ye**. If the skin and hair scorch, the **fluids** are all lost. When the **fluids** are lost, the skin and joints are damaged. If the skin and joints are damaged, the skin withers and the hair breaks. When the hair breaks, it is a sign that the hair dies first. On the **3rd day** (丙), the condition worsens; on the **4th day** (丁), death occurs. **3rd** and **4th** are **Fire**; the **lung** is **Metal**. **Fire** overcomes **Metal**, hence the saying: "On the **3rd day**, the condition worsens; on the **4th day**, death occurs."

When the **qi** of the **hand minor Yin** ceases, the pulse fails to circulate. The **hand minor Yin** is the **heart** channel, which governs the pulse. When the true **heart** **qi** ceases, the blood vessels fail to circulate. If the pulse fails to circulate, the blood does not flow. If the **heart**’s **qi** ceases, the blood fails to move. When the blood does not flow, the complexion’s luster departs. The blood nourishes the body, giving it radiance and luster. If the blood fails to flow, the body’s color and moisture are all lost. Thus, the face turns black like a **pear** (梨), a sign that the blood dies first. The **pear** is a fruit consumed by humans, its hue yellow-black. When the **heart**’s blood disperses, the face lacks nourishment, resembling a **pear**’s yellow-black color—this is the sign of the blood’s death. On the **9th day** (壬), the condition worsens; on the **10th day** (癸), death occurs. **9th** and **10th** are **Water**; the **heart** is **Fire**. **Water** overcomes **Fire**, hence the saying: "For **heart** disease, on the **9th day**, the condition worsens; on the **10th day**, death occurs."

When the **qi** of all three **Yin** channels ceases, the eyes become dizzy and blind, turning inward. The three **Yin** channels are the **three Yin** of the hands and feet. **Dizziness** (眩) means confusion...

「暝，闭也。The five **zàng**’s **pīn** all belong to the **three Yin**, all converging at the eyes. When the **three Yin**’s **qi** is exhausted, the eyes grow dizzy and blind—**míng** (暝) means closure.

The **eyes closing** signifies **lost will** (*shī zhì* 失志). Human **five wills** all belong to **Yin**: the **liver**’s will is **anger**, the **heart**’s will is **joy**, the **spleen**’s will is **thought**, the **kidney**’s will is **fear**, and the **lung**’s will is **grief**. When the **three Yin** are exhausted, the five **zàng** lose their wills—thus, there is no joy, anger, thought, grief, or fear. The five wills vanish, hence the term **lost will**. **Lost will** means the will dies first.

When **Yin** and **Yang** separate, one feels **desolate** and **loses will**. Death is when the eyes grow dizzy#5.

To observe the death of the five wills, simply observe the **dizziness** and **chaos** of the eyes.

When the **six Yang**’s **qi** is exhausted, **Yin** and **Yang** separate. The **six Yang** are the **three Yang** of the hands and feet. The **hand three Yang** connect to **Heaven’s qi**—**day Yang**—while the **foot three Yang** connect to **Earth’s qi**—**night Yin**. When **Heaven** and **Earth’s** **Yin** and **Yang** are blocked, it is called **separation**.

When **Yin** and **Yang** separate, the **shèng** and **lǐ** are depleted. **Shèng** refers to the **jīn-yè** (津液) leaking out; **lǐ** refers to the **texture** where **patterns** converge. When **Yin** and **Yang** are severed, the **shèng** and **lǐ** open and leak without closure.

Thus, **absolute sweating** emerges—**sweat**#6 like **strung pearls**, pouring forth without flowing. **Shèng** and **lǐ** are the **sweat voids**; once the **sweat voids** open, sweat clings to the flesh like **beads**, yet does not disperse. This is the sign that **qi** dies first.

Sweat pouring but not flowing—this is the sign that **qi** dies first. If one dies in the **morning**, the signs appear the **previous evening**; if one dies in the **evening**, the signs appear the **following morning**. This is what is meant.

In all cases where these signs appear and death follows, it will occur on the same day.»

The **Great Canon of the Channels and Networks, Chapter Twenty-One#7: Twenty-Five Difficulties**

There are twelve channels. [See the first difficulty for annotation.] The five **Zang** and six **Fu**, totaling eleven, share one channel—what channel is this?

In total, the five **Zang** and six **Fu** govern eleven channels, yet there remains one channel unknown. Indeed, this one channel is the **hand Shào Yīn** (minor yin) and the **heart’s governing vessel’s** collateral branch.

The five **Zang** each have one **tún** (vessel), making eleven **tún** in total, while the heart has two **tún**, together forming the twelve channels.

The **hand Shào Yīn** is the **true heart tún**, serving as the **Jūn Huǒ** (sovereign fire);
The **hand Xīn Zhǔ** (heart’s governing vessel) is the **heart’s wrapping tún**, serving as the **Xiàng Huǒ** (ministerial fire).

The **heart’s governing vessel** and the **San Jiāo** (triple burner) form its outer sheath.
The **heart’s governing vessel** is the **hand Yàn Yīn** (ulnar yin);
The **San Jiāo** is the **hand Shào Yáng** (minor yang).
These two channels merge as the outer sheath—
Both have names yet no tangible form.

The **heart’s wrapping** lies beyond the **màn zhī** (diffuse fat), with fine aponeurotic membranes like silk, connecting to the heart and lungs.
The **San Jiāo** is detailed in the thirty-first difficulty.
Both have names yet no tangible form.

Thus, it is said that there are twelve **channels** (经). The **hand Xīn Zhǔ** (heart’s governing vessel) and the **hand Shào Yáng** (minor yang) govern the five **zàng** (viscera) and six **fǔ** (bowels), completing the twelve **channels** (经).

The twenty-sixth difficulty states:
*"There are twelve channels, fifteen collaterals (络). What are the remaining three collaterals?"*
Each of the twelve channels has one collateral, so there should be twelve collaterals. Yet it is said there are fifteen collaterals—what are these three collaterals?

Now it is explained:
There are **yang** (陽) collaterals and **yīn** (陰) collaterals.
The **yang** (陽) **tǎ** (踏) channel is on the left lateral ankle, with its collateral on the right medial ankle;
The **yīn** (陰) **tǎ** (踏) channel is on the right medial ankle, with its collateral on the left lateral ankle.
There is also the **spleen’s great collateral** (脾之大络).
The **spleen’s great collateral** originates from the **spleen** (脾), named **Dà Bāo** (great wrapping), with its orifice (穴) located below the navel (脐).
The **yang** (陽) collateral is the collateral of **yang** (陽) **jiāo** (矫, *correction/rectification*);
The **yīn** (陰) collateral is the collateral of **yīn** (陰) **jiāo** (矫, *correction/rectification*).
This clearly distinguishes the **yang** (陽) collaterals and **yīn** (陰) collaterals.
Thus, there are fifteen collaterals (络).
With these three collaterals, the total becomes fifteen collaterals.

The **Eight Extraordinary Channels** (奇经八脉) are discussed in the thirty-third chapter.
The twenty-seventh difficulty states:
*"There are eight extraordinary channels (奇经), which are not confined to the twelve regular channels (十二经). What does this mean?"*
**Qí** (奇) means *collateral* (络), *irregular* (零), and *unpaired* (不偶).
These eight **channels** (豚) do not follow the **Dao/Way** (道) of the regular **yīn-yáng** (阴阳) channels but instead correspond to the outer sheaths (表裹) of the **Eight Extraordinary Channels**.
They run along alternate and irregular paths (别道奇行), hence they are called **Eight Extraordinary Channels** (奇经).

Now it is explained:
There is the **Yang Wéi** (陽維, *Yang Restraint*).
**Wéi** (维) means *to hold* (持).
The **Yang Wéi** holds all the **yang** (陽) channels.

There is the **Yīn Wéi** (陰維, *Yin Restraint*).
The **Yīn Wéi** holds all the **yīn** (陰) channels.

There is the **Yang Jīng** (陽踏, *Yang Correction*).
**Tǎ** (踏) means *swift* (捷疾) and *vigorous* (健).
This channel governs the mechanism of walking (行步之机要) and the movement of the feet (动足之所由).

There is the **Yīn Jīng** (陰踏, *Yin Correction*).
Similar in function.

There is the **Chōng** (冲, *Penetrating*).
The **Chōng** channel is the **sea of the twelve regular channels**.
**Chōng** means *to communicate*.
It states that this channel, descending to the feet and ascending to the head, communicates and receives the **qi** and blood of the twelve regular channels.

There is the **Dū** (督, *Governor*).
**Dū** means *central*.
It is the central governing channel of the **yang** channels.

There is the **Rèn** (任, *Conception*).
**Rèn** means *to bear* (□#10).
It is the foundation of human life and nurturing.

There is the **Dài** (带, *Girdle*) channel.
**Dài** means *to bind*.
It gathers and binds all the channels, ensuring their harmonious regulation.

In total, these eight channels do not adhere to the regular channels, hence they are called the **Eight Extraordinary Channels**.

These eight extraordinary channels do not follow the fixed regulations of the twelve regular channels; they run along alternate paths, hence they are called **Eight Extraordinary Channels**.

The regular channels number twelve, the **luò** (络) networks fifteen, making a total of twenty-seven **qi** pathways.
Together, the regular channels and **luò** networks comprise twenty-seven **qi** pathways, flowing upward and downward in sequence.

Why do the **Eight Extraordinary Channels** not adhere to the regular channels?
It is asked: The **luò** networks and the twelve regular channels do not follow fixed regulations.

It is explained:
The Sage constructed canals and ditches to facilitate water flow, preparing for contingencies.
The answer is: The earth has twenty-four waterways, corresponding to the twenty-four regular channels and **luò** networks.
The Sage, beyond the twenty-four channels of water, further constructed canals and ditches to illustrate the **Eight Extraordinary Channels**.
Canals and ditches are the pathways that facilitate the flow of water, serving as safeguards against unforeseen circumstances.

When heaven sends rain, the canals and ditches overflow.
When heaven’s timely rains descend, the canals and ditches become full.
At such times, mist and fog wander freely, and the Sage cannot further regulate them.
When heaven’s rains and mists overflow the canals and ditches, the Sage allows them to flow freely without further intervention.

This is like the **luò** channels overflowing; the regular channels cannot further restrain them.
When the **Eight Extraordinary Channels** overflow, even the twelve regular channels cannot restrain them, allowing them to flow along their alternate paths.

The *Twenty-Eight Difficulties* states:
As for the **Eight Extraordinary Channels**, since they do not adhere to the twelve regular channels, where do they originate and terminate?

It is explained:
The **Dū** channel—
The first of the **Eight Extraordinary Channels**—
arises at the **lower extremity’s acupuncture point** (下极之俞).
The **Chángqiáng** (长强) acupuncture point, located on the sacrum, is where the **Dū** channel originates.
It ascends along the spine.
The **Dū** channel converges with the **Rèn** channel and runs along the spine.
It ascends to **Fēngfǔ** (风府).
It ascends to the **Fēngfǔ** acupuncture point, located three *cùn* above the hairline.
It enters and belongs to the brain.
It ascends into the brain.

The **Rèn** channel—
The second of the **Eight Extraordinary Channels**—
arises below **Zhōngjí** (中极).
The **Rèn** channel ascends from the **Zhōngjí** acupuncture point, located four *cùn* below the navel.
**Zhōngjí** below refers to the **Qūgǔ** (曲骨) acupuncture point, where the **Rèn** channel originates.
