It ascends along the inner lower abdomen to the **pubic hairline**.
It follows the interior of the abdomen, ascending to the **Guānyuán** (关元) acupuncture point, located three *cùn* below the navel.
It ascends to the throat and pharynx.

The **Chōng** channel—
The third of the **Eight Extraordinary Channels**—
arises at **Qìchōng** (气冲), alongside the **foot Yangming** channel.
The **Qìchōng** acupuncture point is located two *cùn* on either side of the pubic hair in the lower abdomen, where the **foot Yangming** channel’s **qi** emerges.
It ascends alongside the navel, dispersing in the chest.

The **Dài** channel—
The fourth of the **Eight Extraordinary Channels**—
arises at **Jìxié** (季胁).
**Jìxié** is located below the **jīn** (筋, *tendon*), connecting to the space between the body and the hip bone, where the **Dài** channel originates.
It encircles the body.
*Huí* (回) means *to encircle*.
It encircles the body like a girdle.

The **Yáng Jīng** channel—
The fifth of the **Eight Extraordinary Channels**—
arises at the **heel’s center**.
It originates from the center of the heel.
It ascends along the outer ankle.
It follows the **Chōng** acupuncture point on the outer ankle bone.
It ascends into **Fēngchí** (风池).
It ascends into the **Fēngchí** acupuncture point, located in the depression at the back of the neck, within the hairline.

The **Yīn Jīng** channel—
The sixth of the **Eight Extraordinary Channels**—
also arises at the **heel’s center**.
It originates from the center of the heel.
It ascends along the inner ankle.
It follows the **Zhàohǎi** (照海) acupuncture point on the inner ankle bone.
It ascends to the throat and pharynx.
It intersects and communicates with the **Chōng** channel.

The **Yáng Wéi** and **Yīn Wéi**—
These are the **halters of yīn-yáng**, governing and holding the **yīn-yáng** channels.
These two channels are among the **Eight Extraordinary Channels**.
They **luò** (络) throughout the body.
They **luò** bind and regulate the channels of the body.
When they overflow and accumulate, they cannot circulate and irrigate the regular channels.
When these two channels overflow and accumulate, they cannot cycle and nourish the twelve regular channels.

Thus, the **Yáng Wéi** arises at the convergence of all **yang** channels.
The **Yáng Wéi** channel originates where all **yang** channels converge.
The **Yīn Wéi** arises at the intersection of all **yīn** channels.
The **Yīn Wéi** channel originates where all **yīn** channels intersect.

The Sage constructed canals and ditches; when they overflow, the water flows into deep lakes, and even the Sage cannot restrain them.
The Sage’s canals and ditches facilitate water flow, but when they overflow, the water flows into deep lakes, beyond even the Sage’s control.
The flow of canals and ditches is analogous to the **Eight Extraordinary Channels** described below.
When a person’s channels are vigorous, the **qi** enters the eight extraordinary channels without circulating, so the twelve regular channels cannot restrain them.
When a person’s channels are vigorous and the **qi** enters the **Eight Extraordinary Channels**, flowing along alternate paths, the twelve regular channels cannot control them.

If they receive **xiéqi** (邪气, *pathogenic qi*), accumulation leads to swelling and heat.
When the eight extraordinary channels receive pathogenic **qi** and accumulate, they result in swelling and heat.
They are treated with **biānshè** (砭射, *acupuncture and bloodletting*).
They are treated by piercing with **biān** (砭) stones.

The Twenty-Ninth Difficulty states: How do the **Eight Extraordinary Channels** manifest as disease? When discussing the **Eight Extraordinary Channels**, one must inquire about their pathological conditions.

Now it is said: The **Yang Linking Channel** (阳维) pertains to **yang**; the **Yang Linking Channel** maintains and governs all the **yang** channels. The **Yin Linking Channel** (阴维) pertains to **yin**; the **Yin Linking Channel** maintains and governs all the **yin** channels. When **yin** and **yang** cannot mutually maintain each other, the **yang** fails to govern its respective channels, and the **yin** fails to govern its respective channels. Then one feels **chàngrán** (怅然, *disheartened*) and loses resolve. **Chàngrán** means *startled*. When startled, one loses resolve and feels **huǎnghū** (恍惚, *dazed*). The **qi** becomes **róngróng** (溶溶, *languid and slow*), unable to sustain itself. **Róngróng** signifies *sluggish and weak*; thus, it cannot gather and maintain itself. **Yin Linking Channel** disease manifests as **Yin Tread Channel** (阴踏) disorder. When the **yin** channels are vigorous, their **qi** disperses into the **Yin Tread Channel**, resulting in **Yin Tread Channel** disease. When **yang** is slack and **yin** is urgent, this denotes the meaning of **xūshí** (虚实, *deficiency and excess*). In **Yin Tread Channel** disease, the **qi** ascends rapidly from the inner ankle, while the outer ankle rises sluggishly. Additionally, it causes the foot to become stiff and straight, with the **Five Networks** (五络) obstructed.

The **Yang Linking Channel** disease manifests as **Yang Tread Channel** (阳踏) disorder. When the **yang** channels are vigorous, their **qi** disperses into the **Yang Tread Channel**, resulting in **Yang Tread Channel** disease. When **yin** is slack and **yang** is urgent, the **Yang Network** (阳络) lies at the outer ankle, and in disease, its **qi** ascends rapidly from the outer ankle while the inner ankle rises sluggishly. Additionally, it causes one to run wildly without restraint.

The **Penetrating Channel** (冲) as disease: When the **Penetrating Channel** receives disease, it causes **nìqì** (逆气, *rebellious qi*) and **guǒjí** (裹急, *abdominal constriction*). When **kidney qi** is insufficient, it injures the **Penetrating Channel**, thus **rebellious qi** refers to **zhàngnì** (胀逆, *distension and reversal*), and **abdominal constriction** refers to **fùtòng** (腹痛, *abdominal pain*).

The **Governing Channel** (督) as disease: When the **Governing Channel** receives disease, the spine becomes rigid and **jué** (厥, *swoon*) occurs. Since the **Governing Channel** runs along the spine, disease manifests as rigidity of the spine and **jué** (swooning).

The **Conception Channel** (任) as disease: When the **Conception Channel** receives disease...

When the **Conception Channel** receives disease, it causes internal **knots and blockages**—in men, this manifests as the **Seven Types of Hernia** (七疝). When **qi** and **blood** are deficient and **cold-heat imbalances** arise, internal blockages form. The Seven Types of Hernia are:
- **Hammer Hernia** (锤疝),
- **Coiled Hernia** (盘疝),
- **Cold Hernia** (寒疝),
- **Symptomatic Hernia** (症疝),
- **Adjacent Hernia** (附疝),
- **Wolfish Hernia** (狼疝),
- **Qi Hernia** (气疝).

In women, it manifests as **clumped masses** (痕聚). *痕脊#19* refers to masses shaped by external influences. The Eight Types of Masses are:
- **Snake-like Mass** (蛇痕),
- **Fatty Mass** (脂痕),
- **Greenish Mass** (青痕),
- **Yellowish Mass** (黄痕),
- **Dry, Dull Mass** (燥痴),
- **Blood Mass** (血痕),
- **Vixen-like Mass** (狐痕),
- **Tortoise-shell Mass** (鳖痕).

When the **Belt Channel** (带) receives disease, the **Belt Vessel** (带豚) is affected, causing:
- **abdominal fullness** (腹满),
- **distended abdomen** (腹肚膨满),
- **waist weakness**, as if sitting in water (腰溶溶若坐水中).

When the **Yang Linking Vessel** (阳维) receives disease, it causes **alternating cold and heat** (苦寒热). Since the Yang Linking Vessel governs the **Defensive Qi** (卫), cold and heat arise.

When the **Yin Linking Vessel** (阴维) receives disease, it causes **heart pain** (苦心痛). Since the Yin Linking Vessel governs the **Nourishing Qi** (荣), which is **blood**, and the heart generates blood, pain ensues.

These are the diseases of the **Eight Extraordinary Vessels** (奇经八脉).

The **Nourishing Qi** flows—does it always accompany the **Defensive Qi**?

*Nourishing* (荣) means *flourishing*. The hundred bones and nine orifices are thereby nourished and made radiant by this blood and qi.

*Defensive* (卫) means *to guard*. The resolute and fierce qi circulates outside the vessels, moving through the body by day and through the organs by night, guarding and protecting the body.

Does the **Nourishing Qi** accompany the **Defensive Qi**? Yes: the Classics say that humans receive qi from grains. Humans have no roots or stalks; food is their life, so humans receive qi from grains.

Grains enter the stomach. The stomach is the sea of water and grains. The stomach is like a marketplace#20, where grains enter. Then it transmits to the five organs and six palaces.

When food qi enters the stomach, it distributes essence to the liver, enriching the tendons. When food qi enters the stomach, kidney qi#21 ascends to the heart, enriching the vessels. When drink enters the stomach, it disperses essence and qi, ascending to the spleen. The spleen’s qi distributes essence, ascending to the lungs and descending to the bladder.

The five organs and six palaces all receive qi. The five organs and six palaces all receive grain qi. The refined portion becomes **Nourishing Qi**. The stomach transforms water, transmitting it to the heart to generate blood; blood is **Nourishing Qi**, so the refined grain qi becomes **Nourishing Qi**.

The turbid portion becomes **Defensive Qi**. The stomach transforms grains into qi, transmitting it to the lungs, which govern qi; qi is **Defensive Qi**, so the turbid grain qi becomes **Defensive Qi**.

**Nourishing Qi** circulates within the vessels. **Nourishing Qi** and blood belong to yin; yin governs#22 the interior, so **Nourishing Qi** flows within the blood vessels.

**Defensive Qi** circulates outside the vessels. **Defensive Qi** belongs to yang; yang governs the exterior, so **Defensive Qi** flows outside the blood vessels.

**Nourishing Qi** flows ceaselessly. The **Nourishing** and **Defensive Qi** circulate endlessly, never resting.

At fifty, the great convergence is renewed.
A person in one day and night completes fifty cycles of **Nourishing Qi** and **Defensive Qi**.
The next day at the **Gèn** hour, they converge again at the **Hand Taiyin** meridian.

Yin and yang interpenetrate.
Yin and yang continuously alternate and flow through one another,
like a ring with no beginning or end.
Like a ring with no discernible start or finish.
Thus, it is known that **Nourishing Qi** and **Defensive Qi** follow one another.
Thus, it is known that **Nourishing Qi** and **Defensive Qi** circulate together.

The **Thirty-First Difficulty** asks:
What are the **Sanjiāo (Three Burners)**?
The **Upper Burner**, **Middle Burner**, and **Lower Burner**—
how do they manifest?
What do they receive and bestow?
How do they arise?
Where do they originate?
How do they begin?
Where do they begin?
How do they end?
Where do they end?
Where is their governance?
Where is the method of treatment?
Can this be made clear?
Can this principle be understood?

Indeed, the **Sanjiāo** are the pathways of water and grain.
The **Sanjiāo**, along with the **Cìdú** (secondary conduits), govern the waterways from which water flows.
Water flows in from the **Upper Burner** and out from the **Lower Burner**.
This is where **qi** begins and ends.
The **Qi Sea** is within the abdomen.
Again, the **Qi Sea** is three inches below the navel, where one perceives the beginning and end of **qi**.

The **Upper Burner** is below the heart, above the **Diàogē** (esophageal junction), at the upper entrance of the stomach.
This indicates the location of the **Upper Burner**.
It governs the interior without exiting.
It governs the reception and containment of water and grain without expulsion.
Its treatment point is at **Tánzhōng** (one inch below the **Yùtáng**, between the nipples, in the depression).
**Tánzhōng** is the name of this acupuncture point.
Directly between the nipples is this point.
**Tánzhōng** serves as the ministerial official, governing **qi**, distributing yin and yang, harmonizing the mind, and giving rise to joy and delight.

The **Middle Burner** is within the stomach’s precincts, neither above nor below.
This indicates the location of the **Middle Burner**.
It governs the rotting and ripening of water and grain.
It governs the orderly transformation of water and grain into **qi** and blood.
Its treatment point is at **Qíbàng** (one inch lateral to the navel), named the **Tiāngōng** acupuncture point.

The **Lower Burner** is at the upper opening of the bladder.
This indicates the location of the **Lower Burner**.
It governs the separation of purity and turbidity, exiting without entering, to transmit and guide.
Above, it responds to the water and grain received—
it separates them, with the pure becoming urine and the turbid becoming feces, transmitting them outward.
Its treatment point is one inch below the navel, named the **Yīnjiāo** acupuncture point.

Thus, they are called the **Sanjiāo**.
In summary, their **fu** (storage) is at **Qījiē**.

The **Qījiē** is the pathway of **Yīn** and **Yáng**, located two inches lateral to the pubic hair in the lower abdomen.
This point is where the **foot Yangming** meridian’s **qi** originates.
The **foot Yangming** (Stomach) meridian transforms grain into **qi**, while the **Sanjiāo** governs the **qi** of the Three Elements.
Thus, its **fu** (storage) is at **Qījiē**.
One text states **Chōng** instead.
This is not the language of **Biǎn Que** (扁鹊), but rather **Lǚ Shì**’s (吕氏) second recording, which notes that this was a variation in earlier editions.
**Qīchōng** is the root of the Twelve Meridians and the **fu** where all meridians circulate their **qi**—this meaning also holds.

In the fifth chapter of *藏府配像* (The Five Zang and Six Fu Corresponding to Images), there are six questions (#29):
The Thirty-Second Difficulty asks:
*"The Five Zang are all equal.
The Five Zang are all situated within the abdomen.
Yet why are the Heart and Lungs alone positioned above the **Liè** (鬲)?"*
How can it be that the Heart and Lungs alone reside above the **chest Liè**?
The answer is:
The Heart governs **blood**, and the Lungs govern **qi**.
Blood is the **Róng** (Honor), and **qi** is the **Wèi** (Defense).
The Heart is the sovereign, dwelling high and overseeing all—
it generates **blood**, which becomes the **Róng**.
The Lungs are like the **Huàgài** (华盖, ornamental canopy), also high in position—
they generate **qi**, which becomes the **Wèi**.
Together, they ascend and descend, called the **Róng-Wèi**.
Blood flows according to **qi**, and **qi** moves, depending on it,
sometimes ascending, sometimes descending, yet always following in tandem.
Circulating through the meridians and networks,
the **Róng** completes its cycle around the body.
Flowing and returning within the Twelve (#31) Meridians and Fifteen Networks,
it spreads through the body but does not distribute evenly.
Thus, the Heart and Lungs are positioned above the **Liè**.

The Heart and Lungs are the masters of **blood** and **qi**, and they also connect with **Tianqi** (天气, celestial **qi**).
Hence, they are above the **Liè**.

The Thirty-Third Difficulty asks:
*"The Liver is green, symbolizing Wood.
The Liver’s color is green, corresponding to the **East**’s **Jiǎ** (甲) and **Yǐ** (乙) Wood.
The Lungs are white, symbolizing Metal.
The Lungs’ color is white, corresponding to the **West**’s **Gēng** (庚) and **Xīn** (辛) Metal.
The Liver sinks when it meets Water.
When the Liver encounters Water, it sinks.
Wood floats when it meets Water.
Wood is the **qi** of the Liver, yet when it meets Water, it rises instead of sinking.
The Lungs float when they meet Water.
When the Lungs encounter Water, they float.
Metal sinks when it meets Water.
Metal is the **qi** of the Lungs, yet when it meets Water, it *reverts* (#30) and does not float.
What is the meaning of this?"*
Why does the Liver—Wood—sink, and the Lungs—Metal—float?

The answer is:
The Liver is not purely Wood.
The **East**’s **Jiǎ** and **Yǐ** Wood: the Liver belongs to Wood,
yet it does not purely inherit the **qi** of Wood.
**Yǐ** is the *corner* (角).
**Jiǎ** is **Yáng**, and **Yǐ** is **Yīn**;
**Yǐ** carries a Metal resonance (#32) rather than a purely Wood one (#33).
Wood is **Yáng**, and Metal is **Yīn**—
**Yīn** and **Yáng** intermingle.
**Gēng** is *soft* (柔).
The **East**’s **Jiǎ** and **Yǐ** Wood fear the **West**’s **Gēng** and **Xīn** Metal—
**Yǐ** submits to **Gēng** as a wife,
**Gēng** being **Yáng** and **Yǐ** **Yīn**—
a yielding **Yīn**.

In grand terms, it is **Yīn** and **Yáng**.
Speaking of the greater **Yáng** aspect of Wood,
**Gēng** shares both **Yīn** and **Yáng**—
**Jiǎ** is **Yáng**, and **Yǐ** is **Yīn**.
In smaller terms, it is *husband and wife*.
Speaking of the Wood’s lesser aspect,
nothing surpasses the union of husband and wife.
Since **Jiǎ** and **Yǐ** Wood fear **Gēng** and **Xīn** Metal,
**Yǐ**—the younger sister—marries **Gēng** as his wife,
**Yǐ** being the **Yīn** wife, **Gēng** the **Yáng** husband.
**Gēng** releases its subtle **Yáng** (#34).
**Jiǎ** is **Yáng**; this releases the **qi** of **Jiǎ**.
Yet it absorbs the subtle **Yīn** **qi** of **Yǐ**.
**Yǐ** is **Yīn**; it also contains Metal’s nature,
absorbing the **Yīn** **qi** of **Yǐ**.
Its intent delights in Metal.
The **Jiǎ**’s essence finds joy in Metal’s nature.
Moreover, it travels through **Yīn** pathways more often.
Wood receives **qi** in the seventh month,
grows strong in the tenth month,
and thrives from the seventh to twelfth month—
mostly through **Yīn** pathways.
In the first month, it faces its official,
and in the second month, its sovereign reigns—
then it follows **Yáng** pathways.
Thus, the Liver sinks when it meets Water.
**Yǐ** unites with **Gēng**, adhering to the husband’s nature,
so the Liver sinks when it meets Water.

The Lungs are not purely Metal.
The **West**’s **Gēng** and **Xīn** Metal: the Lungs belong to Metal,
yet they do not purely inherit Metal’s **qi**.
**Xīn** is the *merchant* (商).
**Gēng** is **Yáng**, and **Xīn** is **Yīn**;
**Xīn** carries a Fire resonance (#35).
*Merchant* is the sound of Metal.
Fire is **Yáng**, and Metal is **Yīn**—
**Yīn** and **Yáng** intermingle.
**Dīng** (丙) is *soft* (柔).
The **West**’s **Gēng** and **Xīn** Metal fear the **South**’s **Dīng** and **Dìng** (丁) Fire—
**Xīn** marries **Dīng** as his wife,
**Dīng** being **Yáng** and **Xīn** **Yīn**—
a yielding **Yīn**.

In grand terms, it is **Yīn** and **Yáng**.
Speaking of the greater aspect of Lung-Metal,
nothing surpasses **Yīn** and **Yáng**—
**Gēng** is **Yáng**, and **Xīn** is **Yīn**.
In smaller terms, it is *husband and wife*.
Speaking of the lesser aspect of Lung-Metal,
nothing surpasses the union of husband and wife—
**Gēng** and **Xīn** Metal fear **Dīng** and **Dìng** Fire,
so **Xīn**—the younger sister—marries **Dīng** as his wife,
**Xīn** being the **Yīn** bride, **Dīng** the **Yáng** husband.
**Xīn** releases its subtle **Yīn** (#36).
**Xīn** is **Yīn**; this releases the husband’s subtle **Yīn** **qi**.
Yet it unites with Fire.
**Xīn** Metal aligns with **Dīng** Fire in marriage.
Its intent delights in Fire.
The Lungs’ Metal essence finds joy in Fire’s nature.
Moreover, it travels through **Yáng** pathways more often.
Metal receives **qi** in the first month,
gains vitality in the fourth month,
and thrives from the first to sixth month—
mostly through **Yáng** pathways.
By the seventh month, it faces its official,
and in the eighth month, its sovereign reigns—
then it follows **Yīn** pathways.
Thus, the Lungs float when they meet Water.
**Dīng** unites with **Xīn**, adhering to the husband’s nature,
so the Lungs float when they meet Water.

The Lungs, when mature, revert to sinking.
The Lungs are born floating but mature and sink.
The Liver, when mature, reverts to floating.
Why is this?
The Liver is born sinking but matures and floats.
Thus, it is known that **Xīn** returns to **Gēng**.
**Gēng** is purely Metal in nature,
while **Xīn** carries a Fire resonance,
so when the Lungs are born, they float,
yet when they mature and revert to sinking,
it is known that **Xīn**’s Fire resonance
returns to **Gēng**’s Metal nature.
**Yǐ** returns to **Jiǎ**.
**Jiǎ** is purely Wood in nature,
while **Yǐ** carries a Metal resonance,
so when the Liver is born, it sinks,
yet when it matures and reverts to floating,
it is known that **Yǐ**’s Metal resonance
returns to **Jiǎ**’s Wood nature.

The Thirty-Fourth Difficulty asks:
*"The Five Zang each have sound, color, odor, and taste;
can this be understood?"*
The Liver, Heart, Spleen, Lungs, and Kidneys all possess sounds,
colors, fragrances and odors,
and flavors.
Can this principle be understood?
The answer is:
*"Ten transformations."*

The discussion of the Five Zang states that there are purely five transformations,
but when combined with the Six Fu, there are ten transformations.

The Liver’s color is **qīng** (青).
The variations of the five colors lie within Wood,
and the five colors of the Zang are derived from the Liver’s Wood *qi* (氣)
permeating and nourishing one another,
so each manifests its own kind—its color is seen.
The Liver is the essence of Wood,
the *zhèn* (震) *qi*,
its color **qīng** (青),
and it occupies the **shù fāng** (束方) position.
Its odor is **zào** (躁).
When influenced by Fire, its odor becomes **zào** (躁).
Its taste is **suān** (酸).
Earth receives Wood’s taste and becomes **suān** (酸),
as the interplay of straight and crooked yields **suān** (酸),
and **suān** (酸) is taken for its astringent quality.
Its sound is **hū** (呼).
Metal and Wood correspond,
and when they interact, the sound is **hū** (呼).
Its fluid is **dì** (泣).
Water conducts *qi* and nourishes the Child,
so the Liver produces **dì** (泣).

The Heart’s color is **chì** (赤).
Wood’s dispersed color, when in Fire, is **chì** (赤).
Its odor is **jiāo** (焦).
The variations of the five odors lie within Fire,
and the five odors of the Zang arise when Fire flourishes—**jiāo** (焦) and **kǔ** (苦) emerge.
Its taste is **kǔ** (苦).
The Spleen governs sweetness and receives flavors,
while Fire, derived from Earth, makes Earth **kǔ** (苦),
and **kǔ** (苦) is taken for its dry and purgative quality.
Its sound is **yán** (言).
Metal and Fire correspond in harmony,
as husband and wife meet, and the sound is **yán** (言).
Its fluid is **hàn** (汗).
Water and Fire in mutual balance,
steamed, become **hàn** (汗).

The Spleen’s color is **huáng** (黄).
Wood’s dispersed color, when in Earth, is **huáng** (黄).
Its odor is **xiāng** (香).
Fire transforms Earth, so its odor is **xiāng** (香).
Its taste is **gān** (甘).
The Spleen’s Earth flavor is **gān** (甘),
and **gān** (甘) can receive flavors,
taken for its gentle and soothing quality,
to nourish the Five Zang through the five tastes,
each following its kind to match its flavor—
in its own Zang, it is **gān** (甘).
Its sound is **gē** (歌).
Metal and Earth generate one another,
as mother and child meet, and the sound is **gē** (歌).
Its fluid is **xián** (涎).
Water conducts fluids, and in the Spleen, they become **xián** (涎).

The Lungs’ color is **bái** (白).
Wood’s dispersed color, when reaching the Lungs, becomes **bái** (白).
Its odor is **xīng** (腥).
Fire’s transformation, when in Metal, is **xīng** (腥).
Its taste is **xīn** (辛).
Earth receives flavors, and the Lung’s master flavor is **xīn** (辛),
taken for its ability to disperse and open.
Its sound is **kū** (哭).
The five tones arise from Metal,
and Metal emits the five tones
to express the Five Zang,
each following its kind to manifest its sound—
Metal, in its own nature, is **kū** (哭),
for the Lungs belong to Metal,
and Metal is the *shāng* (商) tone,
the sorrow of autumn,
and in the emotions, it is sorrowful weeping.
Its fluid is **tì** (涕).
Water conducts fluids, and in the Lungs, they become **tì** (涕).

The Kidneys’ color is **hēi** (黑).
Wood’s dispersed color, when reaching the Kidneys, becomes **hēi** (黑).
Its odor is **fǔ** (腐).
Fire governs odors, and in Water, they become **fǔ** (腐).
Its taste is **xián** (咸).
Earth receives flavors, and in Water, they are conducted downward as **xián** (咸),
for **xián** (咸) is taken for its ability to soften and yield.

Its sound is **shēn** (呻).
When the child meets the mother, the sound of tender **shēn** (呻) is emitted.
Its fluid is **tuò** (唾).
All oral fluids arise from Water,
Water conducts the five fluids,
distributing them to the Five Zang,
thus each Zang has its own fluid,
and in the Kidneys, it becomes **tuò** (唾).

These are the Five Zang’s sounds, colors, odors, and tastes.
In summary, the Five Zang have five sounds, five colors, five odors, five fluids,
each sequentially nourishing the others.

Do the Five Zang contain seven **shén** (神)?
The Five Zang each contain seven **shén** (神).

The Liver contains **hún** (魂).
That which follows the spirit in coming and going is called **pò** (魄);
the Liver contains **hún** (魂), as the auxiliary of the spirit’s **qì** (氣).

The Lungs contain **pò** (魄).
That which combines with the vital essence in entering and exiting is called **pò** (魄);
the Lungs contain **pò** (魄), as the auxiliary of the vital essence’s **qì** (氣).

The Heart contains **shén** (神).
The Heart is the dwelling of the luminous spirit;
it contains **shén** (神), where the vital essence transforms into form.

The Spleen contains **yì** (意) and **zhì** (智).
When the Heart recalls something, it is called **yì** (意);
**yì** (意) governs thought, being the origin of discernment and judgment.
**Zhì** (智) governs memory.
The Kidneys contain **jīng** (精) and **zhì** (志).
The two spirits intermingle,
uniting to form **jīng** (精);
in the Heart, it becomes **zhì** (志),
for **zhì** (志) is that which the Heart directs itself toward.

*(End of the fourth section of the Yellow Emperor’s Eighty-One Difficulties Canon, annotated by Mo Tu.)*

The **Five Viscera and Palaces in Correspondence with the Images** (藏府配像第五):

The **qi** (氣) stirs and follows the blood—
the **Lungs** are the **market** where the five flavors converge,
as the **Spleen** governs the **transformation** of the **grain** into **virtue** (德).
The **Kidneys** store the **turbulent qi** (浊气),
while the **Heart** governs the **spirit** (神) and the **Liver** holds the **planning** (画).

The **Spleen** is the **central market** of the twelve **organs**,
where the **grain** is refined into **virtue** (德).
The **Heart** is the **central palace** of the **Liver** and **Kidneys**,
where the **spirit** (神) and **will** (志) are **perfected**.

The **Liver** is the **central palace** of the **Lungs** and **Spleen**,
where the **planning** (画) and **discernment** (画) are **unified**.
The **Lungs** are the **central palace** of the **Heart** and **Kidneys**,
where the **spirit** (神) and **turbulent qi** (浊气) are **harmonized**.

The **Spleen** is the **central palace** of the **Liver** and **Heart**,
where the **grain** is **cooked** into **virtue** (德).
The **Kidneys** are the **central palace** of the **Lungs** and **Spleen**,
where the **turbulent qi** (浊气) and **virtue** (德) are **refined**.

The **Heart** is the **central palace** of the **Liver** and **Kidneys**,
where the **spirit** (神) and **will** (志) are **fulfilled**.
The **Liver** is the **central palace** of the **Lungs** and **Spleen**,
where the **planning** (画) and **discernment** (画) are **clarified**.

The **Lungs** are the **central palace** of the **Heart** and **Kidneys**,
where the **spirit** (神) and **turbulent qi** (浊气) are **balanced**.
The **Spleen** is the **central palace** of the **Liver** and **Heart**,
where the **grain** is **transformed** into **virtue** (德).

The **Kidneys** are the **central palace** of the **Lungs** and **Spleen**,
where the **turbulent qi** (浊气) and **virtue** (德) are **nourished**.

*(From the Yellow Emperor’s Eighty-One Difficulties Canon Illustrated and Annotated, Volume Five, compiled by Lu Guo Qin Yue Ren, annotated by Linchuan Xi Fanzi Li Ao Zi Ye.)*

Thirty-Fifth Difficulty says: The **Five Zàng** each have their own **domain** (所).
The **domain** refers to the place where they reside.
Each of the **Five Zàng** has its own **domain**,
and their **palaces** (府) are all nearby.
The **Stomach** is near the **Spleen**,
the **Gallbladder** is near the **Liver**,
and the **Bladder** is near the **Kidneys**.
Yet the **Heart** and **Lungs** are distant from the **Large Intestine** and **Small Intestine**.
The **Heart** and **Lungs** are located above the **Lì** (鬲),
while the **Large Intestine** and **Small Intestine** are below.
They are distant and do not lie close to one another.

What is the **Lì** (鬲)?
Why are some **palaces** near and others far?
The **Canon** states that the **Heart** governs **Nourishing** (荣) while the **Lungs** govern **Defensive** (卫),
circulating **Yang Qi** (阳气) throughout.
Thus, they reside above.
The upper region follows the **Celestial** (天),
as **Heaven** is **Yang**,
and the **Heart** and **Lungs** are above the **Lì**.
The **Heart** governs **blood**, which is **Nourishing**,
and the **Lungs** govern **qi**, which is **Defensive**.
**Yang** ascends to the upper body,
and the **Heart** and **Lungs** circulate **Yang Qi**,
so they are located above.
The **Large Intestine** and **Small Intestine** transmit and descend **Yin Qi** (阴气),
so they reside below.
The lower region follows the **Earth** (地),
as **Earth** is **Yin**,
and both the **Large Intestine** and **Small Intestine** are below the **Lì**.
They transmit and channel **Yin Qi** downward,
so they reside in the lower region.
This is why they are distant and not nearby.

The **Heart** and **Lungs** circulate **Yang Qi**,
while the **Large Intestine** and **Small Intestine** transmit **Yin Qi**,
so they are distant and not nearby.

Again, the **Six Palaces** (六府) are all **Yang**,
residing in **clear-cold** (清冷) regions.
The **Six Palaces** are all **Yang**,
as **Yang** is **Qi**,
and the **Three Yang Meridians of the Hands and Feet** are the pathways for **Qi** to circulate.
**Yang** is the most **clear-cold**,
so they are called **clear-cold** regions.

Now, why do the **Large Intestine**, **Small Intestine**, **Stomach**, and **Bladder**
all receive **unrefined** (不争) substances?
What is the meaning of this?
The **Large Intestine** transmits **unrefined** (不冷) **water and grain**,
the **Small Intestine** receives and stores **unrefined** (不冷) **water and grain**,
the **Stomach** contains **unrefined** (不冷) **water and grain**,
and the **Bladder** stores **fluids** (津液) that are **unrefined** (不冷).
Only the **Gallbladder** is **clear-cold**—what is its meaning?

Yet, the **Six Palaces** are those of **right and wrong** (是非).
Speaking of **right and wrong**,
each **Palace** has its distinct function—this is **right**.
The **Small Intestine** is a **Palace**—this is **wrong**.
Why is it so?
Though the **Small Intestine** is paired with the **Heart** as its surface counterpart,
its governance and **Qi** are distinct.
Though its **Qi** is distinct,
its primary function is different,
so though it is called **Heart Qi**,
it does not have a separate **Heart** position.
Thus, it is **wrong**.

The **Canon** states that the **Small Intestine** is the **Palace of Receipt and Storage** (受盛之府).
It receives and stores the **Stomach**’s refined **water and grain**,
accepts **unrefined** (糟粕) substances,
and after further refinement,
transmits them into the **Large Intestine**.
The **Large Intestine** is the **Palace of Transmission and Passage** (传写行道之府).
The **Large Intestine** transmits and channels **unrefined** (不洁) substances,
making them the pathways for their flow.

The **Gallbladder** is the **Palace of Clear-Cold** (清争之府).
It lies beneath the **Liver**’s shorter leaf (短叶闲).
The **Liver**’s **Qi** circulates to the **eyes**,
and when the **eyes** are harmonized,
one discerns **white and black** (白黑).
The **Gallbladder**, as the **Liver**’s **Palace**,
prefers **clear-cold** above all.

The **Stomach** is the **Palace of Water and Grain** (水谷之府).
It functions as the **granary** (仓库),
and is also governed by the **Lungs**.
All **water and grain** gather here.

The **Bladder** is the **Palace of Fluids** (津液之府).
**Fluids** (津) are the perspired essence that exceeds regulation,
while **liquids** (液) are those that seep into empty cavities,
lingering and not flowing freely.
The **Bladder** stores these **fluids**.

Each **Palace** does not have two names,
so it is known to be **wrong**.
Why should there be two names for one **Palace**?
The **Small Intestine** is the **Palace of the Heart**,
as it is the **Heart**’s **Zàng** **Palace**.
The **Large Intestine** is the **Palace of the Lungs**,
as it is the **Lungs**’ **Zàng** **Palace**.
The **Stomach** is the **Palace of the Spleen**,
as it is the **Spleen**’s **Zàng** **Palace**.
The **Gallbladder** is the **Palace of the Liver**,
as it is the **Liver**’s **Zàng** **Palace**.
The **Bladder** is the **Palace of the Kidneys**,
as it is the **Kidneys**’ **Zàng** **Palace**.

The **Small Intestine** is called the **Red Intestine** (赤肠),
as the **Heart**’s color is **red**.
The **Large Intestine** is called the **White Intestine** (白肠),
as the **Lungs**’ color is **white**.
The **Gallbladder** is called the **Green Intestine** (青肠),
as the **Liver**’s color is **green**.
The **Stomach** is called the **Yellow Intestine** (黄肠),
as the **Spleen**’s color is **yellow**.
The **Bladder** is called the **Black Intestine** (黑肠),
as it governs the **lower burner** (下焦).
The **Kidneys**’ color is **black**,
so the **Bladder** is the **Black Intestine**,
residing in the **lower burner**.

Thirty-Sixth Difficulty says: The **Zàng** each have one **ear** (耳).
The **Liver**, **Spleen**, **Lungs**, and **Heart** each have one.
The **Kidneys** alone have two—why is this?
Why do the **Kidneys** have two?

Yet, the two **Kidneys** are not both **Kidneys**.
The two **Kidneys** are not both truly **Kidneys**.
One is the **Kidney**,
and one is the **Gate of Life** (命门).
The left one is the **Kidney**,
and the right one is the **Gate of Life**.

Speaking of males,
the left is the **Kidney**,
and the right is the **Gate of Life**.
The **Gate of Life** is the **portal to one’s vital essence** (生命之门),
and the **right Guǐ** (尺) is the **Minister Fire** (相火),
which commands the **Master Fire** (君火) of the **Gate of Life**.
All **spirit and essence** (神精) reside here.

The **Gate of Life** has a **pore** (穴),
located in the **14th vertebral segment** (十四椎节).
There are also two **Chambers of the Will** (志室),
situated below the **14th vertebral segment**,
three inches on either side.
A **spirit** (神) guards the **Gate of Life**,
preventing evil from entering the **Chambers of the Will**.

**Reside** (舍) means **abode** (宅).
**Spirit and essence** dwell here.
It is where the **Original Qi** (原气) strikes.

**Original** (原) means **source** (元).
**Original Qi** arises at **Child** (子),
which is the **Kàn** (坎) position.
**Kàn** is the **parental essence** (父母之元气),
as **Qián** (乾) is **Heaven** (天) and **Father** (父),
while **Kūn** (坤) is **Earth** (地) and **Mother** (母).
Now, **Kàn**’s **sixth line** (初六) and **third line** (六三)
are **Kūn**’s **sixth line** and **third line**.
**Kàn**’s **second line** (九二)
is **Qián**’s **second line**.
It is said that **Qián** and **Kūn** interact at **sixth line** (六三) and **second line** (九二),
forming the **Kàn** hexagram,
and thus **Original Qi** is anchored to the **Kidneys**.

Hence, males store **essence** (精).
The **Five Zàng** and **Six Palaces** overflow with **essence and Qi** (精气),
which then **percolate** (淫溢) and **irrigate** (掺灌) the **Kidneys**.
The **Kidneys** receive and store this **essence**.
Each of the **Five Zàng** has its own **essence**,
which is directed to the **Kidneys** as needed.
The **Kidneys** serve as the **capital** (都会),
the **gatekeeper** (关司) of the body,
where all **essence** is stored.

Females **strike** (击) the **uterus** (胞),
so it is known that the **Kidneys** have but one.

**Thirty-seventh Difficulty** asks:
The **Qi** of the **Five Zàng**—where does it originate?
From what source does the **Qi** of the **Five Zàng** arise?
Where does it circulate?
And where does it circulate again?
Can this be understood without doubt?
Can this principle be clearly grasped?

**Response:** The **Qi** of the **Five Zàng** is linked to the **Nine Orifices** above the **Upper Pass** (上关).
The **Nine Orifices** are the ears, eyes, mouth, nose, anterior and posterior **Yīn** (阴), totaling seven **Yáng** orifices and two **Yīn** orifices.
The **Five Zàng** govern them.
Thus, **Lung Qi** circulates through the nose.
The nose is the **orifice** of the **Lungs**,
through which **Lung Essence and Qi** flow.
A harmonized nose discerns fragrances and foul odors.
When the **Five Zàng** enter the nose,
they are stored in the **Heart** and **Lungs**.
If the **Heart** or **Lungs** are diseased,
the nose will fail to function properly.
If the **Lungs** are harmonized,
then the nose is harmonized,
and the nose discerns fragrances and foul odors.

**Liver Qi** circulates through the eyes.
The eyes are the **orifice** of the **Liver**,
through which **Liver Essence and Qi** flow.
A harmonized eye discerns white and black.
**Precision and Clarity** (精明) allow one to perceive all things,
distinguish white from black,
and judge length from shortness.
If one mistakes the long for short,
and white for black,
then **Essence** is diminished.
Now, if the **Liver’s Essence and Qi** flow through the eyes,
and the eyes are harmonized,
then one discerns white and black.

**Spleen Qi** circulates through the mouth.
The mouth is the **orifice** of the **Spleen**,
through which **Liver Essence and Qi** also flow.
A harmonized mouth discerns the flavors of grains.
All people drink and eat,
yet few truly know taste.
The **Spleen** and **Stomach** are the **sea of water and grains** (水谷之海).
If the **Spleen** and **Stomach** are harmonized,
then the mouth is harmonized,
and one knows the flavors of grains.

**Heart Qi** circulates through the tongue.
The tongue is the **orifice** of the **Heart**,
through which **Heart Essence and Qi** flow.
A harmonized tongue discerns the **Five Flavors** (五味).
**Pungent, Sweet, Sour, Bitter, and Salty** are the **Five Flavors**.
If the **Heart** is harmonized,
then the tongue is harmonized,
and one can discern the **Five Flavors**.

**Kidney Qi** circulates through the ears.
The ears are the **orifice** of the **Kidneys**,
through which **Kidney Essence and Qi** flow.
A harmonized ear discerns the **Five Tones** (五音).
**Gōng, Shāng, Jiǎo, Zhēng, and Yǔ** are the **Five Tones**.
If the **Kidneys** are harmonized,
then the ears are harmonized,
and one can discern the **Five Tones**.

If the **Five Zàng** are not harmonized,
then the **Nine Orifices** do not circulate.
When the **Five Zàng** are not harmonized,
**Perverse Qi** (邪气) cannot be externally expelled,
and thus the **Nine Orifices** become obstructed and fail to circulate.

If the **Six Palaces** are not harmonized,
then **stagnation** (留结) forms **abscesses** (瘫).
The **Six Palaces** are **Yáng** in nature.
If **Yáng Qi** cannot internally circulate,
it stagnates and accumulates,
forming **abscesses** (瘫疽).

If **Perverse Qi** resides in the **Six Palaces**,
then the **Yáng Meridians** are not harmonized.
The **Six Palaces** belong to **Yáng**,
and the **meridians** beneath the **skin and muscles** are **Yáng Meridians**.
If **Perverse Qi** lingers in the **Six Palaces**,
then the **meridians** beneath the **skin and muscles** are not harmonized.
If the **Yáng Meridians** are not harmonized,
then **Qi** stagnates.
**Yáng** is **Qi**, ruling the exterior—
if the **meridians** beneath the **skin and muscles** are not harmonized,
then **Perverse Qi** lingers in the **skin and pores** and does not disperse.
If **Qi** stagnates,
then the **Yáng Meridians** become excessive.
If **Perverse Qi** lingers,
then the **Yáng Meridians** grow disproportionately strong.

If **Perverse Qi** resides in the **Five Zàng**,
then the **Yīn Meridians** are not harmonized.
The **Five Zàng** belong to **Yīn**,
and the **meridians** beneath the **muscles and flesh** are **Yīn Meridians**.
If **Perverse Qi** lingers in the **Five Zàng**,
then the **meridians** beneath the **muscles and flesh** are not harmonized.
If the **Yīn Meridians** are not harmonized,
then **blood** stagnates.
**Yīn** is **blood**, ruling the interior—
if the **meridians** beneath the **muscles and flesh** are not harmonized,
then **blood-perverse** (血邪) lingers in the **flesh and sinews**.
If **blood** stagnates,
then the **Yīn Meridians** become excessive.
If **blood-perverse** lingers,
then the **Yīn Meridians** grow disproportionately strong.

If **Yīn Qi** is excessively strong,
then **Yáng Qi** cannot nourish the exterior,
and this is called **Obstruction** (隔).
**Obstruction** means **rejection** (拒).
If the interior is blocked,
then the **Yáng Meridians** cannot enter,
and thus when **Yīn** is excessively strong,
**Yáng Qi** cannot nourish the exterior,
and this is called **Obstruction-Rejection** (隔拒).

If **Yáng Qi** is excessively strong,
then **Yīn Qi** cannot nourish the interior,
and this is called **Closure** (关).
**Closure** means **blockage** (闭).
If the exterior is blocked,
then the **Yīn Meridians** cannot exit,
and thus when **Yáng** is excessively strong,
**Yīn Qi** cannot nourish the interior,
and this is called **Closure**.

If both **Yīn** and **Yáng** are excessive,
they cannot mutually nourish each other,
and this is called **Closure-Obstruction** (关隔).
The interior and exterior fail to support each other,
and this is **Closure-Obstruction**.

What a person possesses is **Qi** and **blood**.
**Qi** is **Yáng**, and **blood** is **Yīn**.
Sometimes both are excessive,
sometimes both are deficient,
sometimes one is excessive while the other is deficient,
and all these conditions lead to disease.

The **Canon** states that **Qi** alone circulates through the **Five Zàng**,
yet does not nourish the **Six Palaces**—why is this so?
It asks: How can **Qi** circulate solely through the **Five Zàng**,
and yet not nourish the **Six Palaces**?

**Response:** The circulation of **Qi** is like water flowing,
never ceasing.
**Qi** flows ceaselessly, like water,
never resting day or night.
Thus, the **Yīn Meridians** nourish the **Five Zàng**.
The **Five Zàng** belong to **Yīn**,
so the **Yīn Meridians** reside within them.

The **Yáng Meridians** nourish the **Six Palaces**.
The **Six Palaces** belong to **Yáng**,
so the **Yáng Meridians** reside within them.

Like a ring with no end,
its beginning is unknown.
**Qi** and **blood** circulate endlessly like a ring,
with no discernible start or finish.
It ends and yet begins anew.
In one day and night,
**Qi** and **blood** conclude their cycle at the **Liver**,
and the next day at **Gèn** hour (艮时) they begin anew at the **Lungs**.

They do not **overflow** or **cover**.
If all **Yīn** is insufficient,
**Yáng** will **intrude** (乘),
causing **overflow** (覆).
Again, if **Yáng** intrudes into the **inch** (尺),
this is also **overflow**.
If all **Yáng** is insufficient,
**Yīn** will **extrude** (出),
causing **excess** (溢).
Again, if **Yīn** rises to the **fish** (鱼) position,
this is also **excess**.
Now, if **Yīn** and **Yáng** each remain in their proper measure,
there will be no **overflow** or **excess**.

**Human Qi** internally warms the **Zàng** and **Fǔ**.
If **Yīn** and **Yáng** do not **overflow** or **extrude**,
then **human Qi** can internally harmonize the **Zàng** and **Fǔ**.
Externally, it **percolates** (溢) into the **victorious pores** (胜理).
**Victorious pores** refer to the **hair follicles** and **skin texture** (毛孔及文理).
Externally, it can **moisten and nourish** the **victorious pores**,
making them smooth and supple.

**Chapter Sixty of the *Degree Numbers of the Zàng and Fǔ*** (藏府度数第六十首)
**Thirty-eighth Difficulty** states:

**Chapter Sixty of the *Degree Numbers of the Zàng and Fǔ* (藏府度数第六十首)**

**Thirty-eighth Difficulty (三十八难)** continued:
Why are there five **Zàng** but six **Fǔ**?
Though there are five **Zàng**, how can there be six **Fǔ**?
Yet, the reason there are six **Fǔ** is the **San Jiāo** (三焦).
The **Fǔ** alone have six because the **San Jiāo** is also one **Fǔ**.
There is a distinction in **Yuan Qi** (原气).
**Yuan Qi** was discussed in the previous chapter.
The **San Jiāo** converges **Qi** at the **Shèn** (肾).
The **Shèn** is the **Zheng** (正) of **Yuan Qi**, while the **San Jiāo** is the **Bié** (别) of **Yuan Qi**.
It governs all **Qi**.
It is called **Qi Hǎi** (气海) in the middle, located in the **Upper Jiāo** (上焦).
Again, the **Qi Hǎi** acupuncture point lies two *cùn* below the navel, in the **Lower Jiāo** (下焦).
It maintains the **Qi** of the entire body.
It has a name but no form.
The **San Jiāo** is merely **jīn mò** (筋膜), attached to the upper, middle, and lower openings of the **stomach**.
Though it bears the name **San Jiāo**, it lacks its true form.
Its **jing** (经) belongs to the **Shǒu Shǎo Yáng** (手少阳).
Its **meridian** belongs to the **Shǒu Shǎo Yáng** meridian.
This is an **external Fǔ**.
The **San Jiāo** has no **internal Fǔ**, only a meridian named **Shǒu Shǎo Yáng**, hence it is called an **external Fǔ**.
Thus, it is said there are six **Fǔ**.
Because the **San Jiāo** is also one **Fǔ**, it is said there are six **Fǔ**.

**Thirty-ninth Difficulty (三十九难)** states:
The **Classics** say there are five **Fǔ**: the **Dǎn** (胆), **Wèi** (胃), **Dà Cháng** (大肠), **Xiǎo Cháng** (小肠), and **Páng Guāng** (膀胱).
Yet, there are six **Zàng**: the **Gān** (肝), **Xīn** (心), **Pí** (脾), **Fèi** (肺), **Shèn** (肾), and **Mìng Mén** (命门).
Why?
Yet, the six **Fǔ** are truly five **Fǔ**.
The **San Jiāo** is added to make six, but in reality, there are five **Fǔ**.
Yet, the five **Zàng** can also be six **Zàng** because the **Shèn** has two **Zàng**.
The five **Zàng** are six because the **Shèn** externally has the **Mìng Mén**, making six in total.
The left side is the **Shèn**, the right side is the **Mìng Mén**.
The **Mìng Mén** is where **spirit** (神) resides.
In men, it stores **jīng** (精); in women, it anchors the **fetus** (胞).
This is detailed in the **Thirty-sixth Difficulty** (三十六难).
Its **Qi** communicates with the **Shèn**.
The **Mìng Mén Qi** is connected to the **Shèn Qi**.
Thus, it is said there are six **Zàng**.
Because of the **Mìng Mén** externally, it is said there are six **Zàng**.

Why are there five **Fǔ**?
Though the **Zàng** have six, why are there only five **Fǔ**?
Yet, each of the five **Zàng** corresponds to one **Fǔ**.
One **Zàng** is paired with one **Fǔ**, making five **Fǔ** in total.
The **San Jiāo** is also one **Fǔ**, yet it does not belong to the five **Zàng**.
The **San Jiāo** is one **Fǔ**, paired with the **Xīn Bāo Luò** (心包络) as **Zàng**, but unrelated to the five **Zàng**.
Thus, it is said there are five **Fǔ**.
Because the **San Jiāo** is not a true **Fǔ**, it is said there are five **Fǔ**.

**Fortieth Difficulty (四十难)** states:
The **Classics** say the **Gān** governs **color**.
The **Gān** is **Mù** (木).
The **huā** (华) and **è** (萼) of **Mù** display the five **colors**.
The **Xīn** governs **odor**.
The **Xīn** is **Huǒ** (火).
The transformation of **Huǒ** produces the five **odors**.
Of the five **odors** in humans, the **Xīn** governs them all.
The **Pí** governs **taste**.
The **Pí** governs **sweetness**; sweetness receives **taste**, hence it governs **taste**.
The **Fèi** governs **sound**.
The **Fèi** is **Jīn** (金).
**Jīn** embodies **sound**, so the five **tones** all emerge from the **Fèi**.
The **Shèn** governs **fluids**.
The five **fluids** all derive from **water**, and the **Shèn** governs **water**, hence it governs **fluids**.
The **nose** is the **candidate** (候) of the **Fèi**, yet it discerns **aroma and odor**.
Though the **Fèi** governs **sound**, the **nose** belongs to the **Fèi** and cannot hear **sound**, yet it discerns **aroma and odor**.
The **ear** is the **candidate** of the **Shèn**, yet it hears **sound**.
Though the **Shèn** governs **fluids**, the **ear** belongs to the **Shèn** and does not pertain to **saliva and tears**, yet it can hear **sound**.
What is the reasoning behind this?
Why does the **nose** and **ear** function thus?

Yet, the **Fèi** is the **Xī Fāng Jīn** (西方金).
The **Fèi** belongs to **Xī Fāng** (西方) **Gēng Xīn Jīn** (庚辛金).
**Jīn** is generated in **Sì** (巳).
**Jīn**’s **Cháng Shēng** (长生) phase is in **Sì**.
**Sì** is **Nán Fāng Huǒ** (南方火).
**Sì-Wǔ-Wèi** (巳午未) belong to **Nán Fāng Huǒ**, with **Sì** being the **Zhèng** (正) of **Nán Fāng Huǒ**.
**Huǒ** is the **Xīn**.
**Nán Fāng Huǒ** corresponds to the **Xīn** among the **Zàng**.
The **Xīn** governs **odor**, and its governance lies in the five **odors**.
Thus, the **nose** knows **aroma and odor**.
The **nose** belongs to the **Fèi**, and **Fèi Jīn** is generated in the position of **Xīn Huǒ**, hence it can distinguish the **odors** governed by **Xīn Huǒ**.

The **Shèn** is the **Běi Fāng Shuǐ** (北方水).
The **Shèn** belongs to **Běi Fāng** (北方) **Rén Guǐ Shuǐ** (壬癸水).
**Shuǐ** is generated in **Shēn** (申).
**Shuǐ**’s **Cháng Shēng** phase is in **Shēn**.
**Shēn** is **Xī Fāng Jīn**.
**Shēn-Yǒu-Xū** (申酉戌) belong to **Xī Fāng Jīn**, with **Shēn** being the **Lín Guān** (临官) position.
**Jīn** is the **Fèi**.
**Xī Fāng Jīn** corresponds to the **Fèi** among the **Zàng**.
The **Fèi** governs **sound**, and its governance lies in **sound**.
Thus, the **ear** hears **sound**.
The **ear** belongs to the **Shèn**, and **Shèn Shuǐ** is generated in the position of **Fèi Jīn**, hence the **ear** can hear **sound**.

**Forty-first Difficulty (四十一难)** states:
Why does the **Gān** alone have two **leaves**?

「Why does the **Liver** alone have two **leaves**, and what does this correspond to?

Indeed: the **Liver** is the **束方 Wood** (束方木).
The **Liver** belongs to the **束方 Jia-Yǐ Wood** (束方甲乙木).
**Wood** is the **Spring** (春).
**Spring** is the time when **Wood** begins to flourish (木发生之时).
All things begin to grow, yet they are still young and tender.
The **Liver**’s **roots** and **teeth** are in their initial stage of sprouting (黄牙甲萌动之始), still young and not yet fully formed.
Its **intent** (意) has no close affinity.
When all things first emerge, they stand upright and independent, with nothing to cling to.
It is still near the **Tài Yīn** (太阴).
The twelve **channels** (经) interrelate, with the **Foot Jué Yīn** (足厥阴) near the **Tài Yīn** (太阴), hence the proximity.
It is not far from the **Tài Yáng** (太阳).
The **Foot Jué Yīn** (足厥阴) **Wood** of the **Shao Yáng** (少阳) phase generates the **Hand Tài Yáng** (手太阳) **Shao Yīn** (少阴) **Fire** (火), hence the **Liver** remains close to the **Tài Yáng** (太阳).
It still possesses two **hearts** (心).
When it flows into the **Tài Yīn** (太阴), it bears the **heart** of fearing **Metal** (金), and when it arises from the **Tài Yáng** (太阳), it bears the **heart** of generating **Fire** (火).
Thus, it has two **leaves**.
Because it has two **hearts**, it has two **leaves**.
This also corresponds to the **Wood leaves** (木叶).
The **Liver leaves** (肝叶) are in alignment with the **Wood leaves** (水应木叶).

**Forty-second Difficulty (四十二难)** states:
What are the lengths of the human **stomach** and **intestines** (肠胃), and how much **water and grain** (水谷) do they each receive?

The lengths of the human **stomach** and **intestines** (肠胃), and the amounts of **water and grain** (水谷) they receive, are as follows:

Indeed: the **Stomach** (胃) is vast, measuring **one chi five cun** (一尺五寸).
The **Stomach** (胃) is the **receptacle** (围), receiving **water and grain** (水谷).
Its **circumference** (大) is **one chi five cun** (一尺五寸).
Its **diameter** (径) is **five cun** (五寸).
The **diameter** (径) is **five cun** (计五寸).
Its **length** (长) is **two chi six cun** (二尺六寸).
The **length** (长) is **two chi six cun** (计二尺六寸).
When **bent horizontally** (横屈), it receives **three dou five sheng** (三斗五升) of **water and grain** (水谷).
One **bend** (横) and one **fold** (屈) together receive **three dou five sheng** (三斗五升).
Within it, **two dou** (二斗) of **grain** and **one dou five sheng** (一斗五升) of **water** are always retained.
Its **content** (其中常留) holds **two dou** (谷二斗) of **grain** and **one dou five sheng** (水一斗五升) of **water**, which are conveyed through the **Small Intestine** (小肠).
The **Small Intestine** (小肠) is vast, measuring **two cun three fen** (二寸半).
The **intestine** (肠) is the **passageway** (畅), allowing the **Stomach qi** (胃气) to flow and removing impurities (去滓秽).
The **Small Intestine** (少肠) has a **circumference** (大) of **two cun three fen** (计二寸半).
Its **diameter** (径) is **eight fen** (八分), minus a **small half** (分之少半).
The **diameter** (径) is **eight fen half** (计八分半).
Its **length** (长) is **three zhang two chi** (三丈二尺).
The **length** (长) is **three zhang two chi** (计三丈二尺).
It receives **two dou four sheng** (二斗四升) of **grain** from the **Stomach**.
It receives **two dou four sheng** (胃中谷计二斗四升) of **grain** from the **Stomach**.
It receives **six sheng three he** (水六升三合) of **water**, minus a **large half** (合之太半).
It receives **six sheng three he half** (水计六升三合半) of **water**.

The **Large Intestine** (大肠), also called the **reciprocal intestine** (回肠), is named so because it curves and twists.
The **Large Intestine** (大肠) has a **circumference** (大) of **four cun** (四寸).
Its **diameter** (径) is **one cun half** (一寸半).」

The **diameter** (径) is **one cun half** (计一寸半).
Its **length** (长) is **two zhang one chi** (二丈一尺).
Its **length** (长计) is **two zhang one chi** (计二丈一尺).
It receives **one dou** (一斗) of **grain** and **seven sheng half** (七升半) of **water**.
From the **Stomach**, **water and grain** total **three dou five sheng** (三斗五升), which are transmitted into the **Small Intestine**, where **grain remains four dou** (谷剩四斗) and **water decreases by eight sheng six he half** (水少八升六合合之少半).
When transmitted further into the **Large Intestine**, the **water and grain** are each reduced by **one dou** (各减一斗).
At this point, **water** enters the **bladder** (膀胱), while **grain** is transmitted to the **anus** (肛门).

The **Rectum** (广肠), also called the **terminal intestine** (肢肠) or **anus** (一名肛门), has a **circumference** (大) of **four cun** (四寸).
Its **diameter** (径) is **two cun half** (二寸半).
Its **diameter** (径计) is **two cun half** (径计二寸半).
Its **length** (长) is **two chi eight cun** (二尺八寸).
Its **length** (长) is **two chi eight cun** (计二尺八寸).
It receives **nine sheng three he eight fen half** (九升三合八分合之一) of **grain** from the **Large Intestine** and transmits it out, totaling **nine sheng three he eight fen half** (受大肠之谷而传出，计九升三合八分合之一).

Thus, the total **length** of the **intestines and stomach** is **five zhang eight chi four cun** (五丈八尺四寸).
Combining the **Stomach, Small Intestine, Large Intestine,** and **Rectum**, the total **length** is **five zhang eight chi four cun** (总计胃、小肠、大肠、广肠，共计五丈八尺四寸).
The total **water and grain** they receive is **eight dou seven sheng six he eight fen half** (合受水谷八斗七升六合八分合之).
This is the total **measure of water and grain** in the **intestines and stomach** (总计肠胃水谷之数).
This describes the **length and capacity** of the **intestines and stomach** for **water and grain** (此肠胃长短，受水谷之数也).
Summarizing again, the **length and water-grain totals** are as stated (再总言长短水谷数).

The **Liver** (肝) weighs **four jin four liang** (四斤四两).
The **Liver** is the **"dry"** (干) organ—its structure resembles a **trunk with branches** (於体状有枝干).
Its weight is **four jin four liang** (计四斤四两).
It has **three lobes on the left** (左三叶) and **four on the right** (右四叶).
The **left three lobes** belong to **Jia (甲)** and **Yang** (属阳), while the **right four lobes** belong to **Yi (乙)** and **Yin** (属阴).
In total, there are **seven lobes** (凡七叶).
The **Liver** governs **Jueyin** (足厥阴) and corresponds to **Zhaoyang** (足少阳), whose numerical association is **seven** (少阳之数剧於七), hence the **seven lobes**.
It stores the **Soul** (魂).
*(See Annotation 34 for further explanation.)*

The **Heart** weighs **twelve liang**. The **Heart** is calculated to weigh **twelve liang**. Within it are **seven apertures and three hairs**. For the **wise of superior intelligence**, the **Heart** has **seven orifices**; it is also said to have **nine orifices and three hairs**; for those of **medium intelligence**, the **Heart** has **five orifices and two hairs**; for the **wise of inferior intelligence**, it has **three orifices and one hair**; for the **common person**, the **Heart** has **two orifices and no hairs**; for the **foolish**, it has **one orifice**; and for the **greatly foolish**, the **Heart** has **one orifice, very small**. Moreover, there are those born with **congenital dullness and delusion**, whose **Hearts**, though present, lack any orifices. Thus, the **Spirit** (神) has no passage for entry or exit, and it is difficult for them to attain **fruit of cultivation** (色果). It holds **three measures of vital essence and fluids**. It stores and contains **three measures of vital essence and fluids**. It governs the **Spirit**. *(See Annotation 34 for further explanation.)*

The **Spleen** weighs **two jin three liang**. The **Spleen**, **pī** (俾), is located beneath the **Stomach** and aids the **Stomach-qi** (胃气), governing the reception of **water and grains** (水谷). It is calculated to weigh **three jin three liang**. Its breadth is **three cun**. Its width is measured at **three cun**. Its length is **five cun**. It has **half a jin of scattered fat**. It contains **half a jin of scattered fat**. It governs the **encapsulation of blood** (裹血) and **nourishes the five Zàng** (五藏). It governs the **encapsulation of blood** to **nourish the five Zàng**. It stores the **Mind** (意). *(See Annotation 34 for further explanation.)*

The **Lung** weighs **three jin three liang**, with **eight lobes and two ears**. The **Lung**, **bó** (勃), is where the **qi** (气) **rises and stagnates**. It is calculated to weigh **three jin three liang**, with **six large lobes and two small lobes**. In total, there are **eight lobes**. The **Metal** (金) resides in **Yǒu** (酉), which is the **eighth of the Eight Gates** (八门), corresponding to the **eight lobes**. This is the **law** (法) governing it. It stores the **Po** (魄). *(See Annotation 34 for further explanation.)*

The **Kidney** has **two units**. The **Kidney**, **yǐn** (引), **draws and directs water-qi** (水气) to **irrigate the various Zàng** (诸豚). The **Kidney** has **two units**, located at the **waist and opposite the navel**. It weighs **one jin one liang**. It is calculated to weigh **one jin one liang**.

The **Liver** stores the **will/intent** (志). *(See Annotation 34 for further explanation.)*

The **Gallbladder** is located at the **short lobe of the Liver** *(#17 Question)*. The **Gallbladder**, **dǎn** (胆), is **bold** (敢), meaning it contains **bold-qi** (胆气), which is **resolute and daring**. It is generated in the **two small lobes of the Liver** *(#17 Question)*. It weighs **three liang three zhu** (三两三铢). The **Gallbladder** is calculated to weigh **three liang three zhu**. It holds **three sheng of essence-fluid** (精汁). It stores and holds **three sheng of essence-fluid**.

The **Stomach** weighs **two jin one liang** *(#18 Question)*. The **Stomach** is calculated to weigh **two jin one liang**. It **twists and turns, flexes and extends**. Its **coiled and winding flexure** (纡回) and **bending and straightening** (屈曲) are contained within the **Zàng** (藏). It is **two chi six cun** long, **one chi five cun** wide, and has a **diameter of five cun**. It holds **two dou of grains** (谷) and **one dou five sheng of water**. *(See previous section for further explanation.)*

The **Small Intestine** weighs **two jin fourteen liang**. The **Small Intestine** is calculated to weigh **two jin fourteen liang**. It is **three zhang two chi** long, **two cun and a half** wide, and has a **diameter of eight fen, less than half** *(少半)*. *(See previous section for further explanation.)* It **spirals and folds in sixteen loops** (曲), beginning from the **left flank** and winding back, **overlapping and accumulating in sixteen turns**. It holds **two dou four sheng of grains** and **six sheng three sheng of water, more than half** *(大半)*. *(See previous section for further explanation.)*

The **Large Intestine** weighs **two jin eleven and one-third liang**. The **Large Intestine** is calculated to weigh **two jin eleven and one-third liang**. It is **two zhang one chi** long, **four cun** wide, and has a **diameter of one cun**. *(See previous section for further explanation.)*

The **Bladder** should curve sixteen times to the right of the navel, winding with sixteen bends. It holds **one dou of grains** and **seven sheng and a half of water**. *(See previous section for further explanation.)*

The **Bladder** weighs **nine liang two zhu**. *"Páng"* means horizontal; *"guāng"* means broad. Its body is short yet horizontally broad. It is also called *"pāo"*—*"pāo"* means boot, and a boot is hollow, used to hold fluids. Its weight is **nine liang two zhu**. Its vertical width is **nine cun**—a total of **nine cun**. It stores **nine sheng nine ge of urine**. It holds **nine sheng nine ge of urine**. Its mouth is **two cun and a half** wide—its mouth measures **two cun and a half** in width. From lip to teeth, it is **nine fen** long. The distance from the mouth to the epiglottis is **three cun and a half** deep, with a capacity of **five ge**. The epiglottis is the throat’s barrier; when water and food descend, it presses down with breathing. The depth from mouth to epiglottis measures **three cun and a half**, with a maximum capacity of **five ge**.

The **Tongue** weighs **ten liang**. *"Shé"* means luminous; it spreads and contracts to articulate speech. It weighs **ten liang**. It is **seven cun** long.

The tongue measures **seven cun** in length.

Its width is **two cun and a half**—a total of **two cun and a half** in breadth.

The **throat gate** weighs **ten liang**.
The throat gate, *"yàn"* (咽), is the throat itself, capable of swallowing food.
It is also called the **passage of speech**—where the flow of *qi* is most critical for communication.
The throat gate governs **Earth-qi**, weighing **ten liang** in total.
Its width is **two cun and a half**—a total of **two cun and a half** in breadth.
From the throat gate to the stomach, it is **sixteen cun** long.
The throat gate to the stomach measures **sixteen cun**.

The **larynx** weighs **twelve liang**.
The larynx is hollow and void—its interior is empty, allowing the passage of breath.
The larynx is the **connection of the lungs**, the pathway for inhalation and exhalation, governing **Heaven-qi**.
It weighs **twelve liang**.
Its width is **two cun**—a total of **two cun** in breadth.
Its length is **twelve cun**, with **nine joints**.
It measures **twelve cun** in length, divided into **nine joints**.

The **anus** weighs **twelve liang**.
The anus, *"gāng"* (釭), is so named because its shape resembles a **cart axle**; it is also called the **thoroughfare intestine**, or **great intestine**.
It weighs **twelve liang** in total.
Its width is **eight cun**, with a diameter of **two cun and a half**, and its length is **twenty-eight cun**.
It can receive **nine sheng three ge eight fen** of food.
(See annotation in the previous section.)
The **Forty-Third Difficulty** states:

「Why does a person die within seven days if they neither eat nor drink?
If one does not consume food and water, how can they survive beyond seven days before death?

Yet:
The stomach always retains **two dou** of grain and **one dou five sheng** of water.
Without roots or nourishment, food and drink are essential to life.
The stomach is the **sea of water and grain**, and when it receives water and grain, it retains **three dou five sheng**.
Thus, an average person urinates **twice daily**.
The **凤** (*feng*), meaning **wind** or **urination**, is the place of discharge.
An average person urinates twice daily, removing **two sheng and half** each time.
By midday, they expel **five sheng**.
If they urinate twice more by day’s end, they remove another **two sheng and half**, totaling **five sheng** daily.
Seven days:
Within seven days.
Five times seven:
Seven sets of **five sheng**.
Thus, **three dou five sheng** of water and grain are exhausted.
Each urination removes **five sheng**, so within seven days, **three dou five sheng** of water and grain are depleted.
Therefore, an average person dies within seven days without food or drink because their **water and grain essence** (*jinye*) are all spent, and thus they die.
All cases where an average person dies after seven days without food or drink are due to the exhaustion of **water and grain essence** (*jinye*), hence death.

**The Forty-Fourth Difficulty** asks:
Where are the **Seven Orifices** (*Qichuangmen*) located?
The **orifices** (*chong*) refer to pathways of passage and discharge for the **organs and bowels** (*zangfu*).
Where are the **Seven Orifices** (*Qichuangmen*)?

Yet:
The **lips** are the **Flying Orifice** (*Feimen*).
The lips belong to the **spleen** (*pi*), and they are dynamic in motion.
It is said that the lips receive water and grain, then transform them inward.

The **teeth** are the **Gate Orifice** (*Mumen*).
The mouth and teeth are the pathways for the **heart-qi** (*xinqi*).
In the heart, they manifest as **intent** (*zhi*); when spoken, they become **words** (*yan*), hence the teeth are the **orifices of the heart**.
They also serve to **crush and subdue** the five grains, passing them inward.

The **stomach** is the **Respectful Orifice** (*Binmen*).
**Respectful** (*bin*) refers to the **steamer** (*ge*), where the stomach-qi discharges.
The stomach transmits **grain-qi** (*guqi*) to the lungs, and since the **steamer** (*ge*) is above the stomach, it is called the **Respectful Orifice**.

The **epiglottis** is the **Inhaling Orifice** (*Ximen*).
The epiglottis serves as the **orifice for the sounds of the five organs** (*zang*).

The **lower opening of the great stomach** (*Dacangxiakou*) is the **Dark Orifice** (*Youmen*).
The **great stomach** (*dacang*) refers to the stomach.
Its lower opening lies three **cun** below the **navel** (*qi*), a hidden and obscure location, hence the **Dark Orifice**.

The **junction of the large and small intestines** is the **Barrier Orifice** (*Lanmen*).
**Junction** (*hui*) means **union**.
Where the large and small intestines meet, they **separate water and grain essence from vital blood**, each returning to their proper place—hence the **Barrier Orifice**.

The **lower extreme** is the **Soul Orifice** (*Pomen*).
The **Soul Orifice** (*pomen*) refers to the **anus** (*gāng*).
Lung-qi ascends through the throat and descends through the anus—this is the pathway of lung-qi.
The lungs **house the soul** (*po*), thus it is called the **Soul Orifice**.
Hence, they are called the **Seven Orifices**.

So it is said that the **Seven Orifices** are the seven pathways of passage and discharge for the **organs and bowels**.

**The Forty-Fifth Difficulty** asks:
Why does the Classic speak of the **Eight Convergences** (*Bahui*)?
How do the **organs** (*zang*), **bowels** (*fu*), **tendons** (*jin*), **bones** (*gu*), **marrow** (*sui*), **blood** (*xue*), **vessels** (*mai*), and **qi** converge?

Yet:
The **bowels converge at the great stomach** (*Fu hui dacang*).
The **convergence** (*hui*) of the **foot field** (*zuchang*)—where the **stomach** (*wei*) and **spleen** (*pi*) meet—
the **hand greater yang** (*shoutaiyang*) **small intestine** (*xiao chang*),
the **hand lesser yang** (*shao taiyang*) **triple burner** (*san jiao*),
and the **conception vessel** (*ren mai*) meet—
is originally called the **middle courtyard** (*zhongyuan*), the **great stomach** (*dacang*), and the **suction point** (*zhuo mu*).
The stomach transforms **qi** and nourishes the six bowels, hence it is called the **convergence**.

The **organs converge at the lateral ribs** (*Zang hui jixie*).
The **lateral ribs** (*jixie*) are the **soft ribs** (*ruan xie*).
At their ends lies a **suction point** (*mu*), which belongs to the **spleen**.
The **foot lesser yin** (*zue jinyin*) and **foot lesser yang** (*zue jinyin*) converge here—
hence, it is called the **convergence of the organs at the lateral ribs**.

The **tendons converge at Yanglingquan** (*Jin hui yanglingquan*).
The **Yanglingquan** (*yanglingquan*) **suction point** lies one **cun** above the **knee** (*xiguan*), in the **popliteal fossa** (*kang*).
It is where the **qi of the foot lesser yang** (*zue shaoyang*) **gallbladder** (*dan*) **bowel** discharges.

The **marrow converges at Juegu** (*Sui hui juegu*).
**Juegu** is the name of a **bone**.
Its **suction point** lies four **cun** above the **external ankle** (*waixu*), at the **yangzhen** (*yangzhen*) **suction point**.
It is also where the **qi of the foot lesser yang** (*zue shaoyang*) **bowel** discharges.

The **blood converges at Gegu** (*Xue hui gegu*).
The **gegū** (*gegū*) **suction points** are two points on the back,
below the seventh vertebra, each one and a half **cun** from the sides.
They are where the **qi of the foot greater yang** (*zue taiyang*) **bladder** (*pangguang*) **bowel** discharges.

The **bones converge at Dayu** (*Gu hui dayu*).
The **dayu** (*dayu*) **suction point** lies beside the first vertebra at the back of the neck,
each one and a half **cun** from the sides.
It is where the **qi of the foot greater yang** (*zue taiyang*) **bowel** discharges.

The **vessels converge at Taiyuan** (*Mai hui taiyuan*).
The **Taiyuan** (*tai yuan*) **suction point** lies within one **cun** of the right **wrist** (*zhang*), at the **fish际** (*yujie*).
It is where the **qi of the hand lesser yin** (*shoutaiyin*) **lung** (*fei*) **bowel** discharges.

The **qi converges at the Triple Burner** (*San jiao*), externally along a **tendon** leading to the **two breasts** (*liang ru*).
The **upper burner** (*shang jiao*) lies below the heart, and the **middle burner** (*zhong jiao*) is above the stomach.
The **lower burner** (*xia jiao*) is below the **navel** (*qi*), one and six-tenths **cun** beneath the **玉堂** (*yutang*).
It aligns with the **two breasts’ depression** (*liang ru guan xian*), which is the **膻中** (*dan zhong*) **suction point**.

It is said that **qi** from the **丹中** (*dan zhong*)—the **ministerial messenger’s office** (*chen shi zhi guan*)—
emanates from the **sea of qi** (*qi hai*), then spreads downward to irrigate all the **organs**.
For internal **heat diseases** (*re bing*), one takes the **convergence suction points** (*hui zhi qixue*) externally.
If the **heat disease** (*re bing*) lies within, one externally targets its **convergence suction points** (*hui zhi qixue*)
to dispel the illness.

**The Forty-Sixth Difficulty** asks:
Why do the elderly lie awake while the young sleep soundly?
Why do the elderly remain **unsettled** (*bu an*) and **unable to sleep** (*bu mei*) when lying down,
while the young, though they sleep, do not awaken?
Why do the elderly and young behave oppositely?

Yet:
The Classic says that the young are **abundant in blood and qi**.
The young are vigorous in the flow of **blood and qi**.
Their **flesh and muscles are smooth**.
Their **skin and muscle tissues are supple and moist**.
Their **qi pathways are open**.
The routes for **blood and qi** are unobstructed.
The **circulation of nourishing and defensive** (*rong wei*) **qi** is regular and unerring.
The harmony of **Heaven and Earth** (*tian di*) and the interplay of **sun and moon** (*ri yue*)
—**winter** is the **dark season** of the four seasons,
and **night** is the **winter** of a day—
the **awakening and sleeping** (*mei mei*) of humans align with these cosmic rhythms.
Since the young have not yet diminished their **defensive qi** (*wei qi*), their **awakening and sleeping** match the **yin and yang** of Heaven and Earth.
Thus, they are **spiritually alert** (*jing*) during the day but **sleep soundly** at night.

The elderly have **weakened blood and qi**.
Their **flesh and muscles are dry**.
Their **qi pathways are sluggish**.」

「When the **blood and qi** pathways become **sluggish** (*se*), their **egress and ingress** fail to follow the proper **cosmic timing** (*shí*).
Thus, the elderly cannot be **spiritually alert** (*jing*) during the day and cannot **sleep soundly** at night.

By day, they cannot find **tranquility** (*ān jìng*), and by night, they cannot **rest** (*miàn*).
Hence, it is known that the elderly cannot **sleep soundly**.

When **defensive qi** (*wei qi*) **emerges**, they **awaken**; when it **retires**, they **sleep**.
But since the **defensive qi** of the elderly has **waned** (*shuāi*), they cannot **sleep soundly** at night.

---
*Huangdi Baishiyi Nan Jing Muyu Jujie Jie卷之六* (compiled by Lu Guo, Qin Yue, and Ren Zi from the *Nan Jing* commentary by Lin Chang’s *Xuanzi* and Li Ao’s *Yejie*)」

**Question Forty-Seven:** Why is it that only the human face can endure cold?

The head and face alone can withstand cold—how so?

**Explanation:** The head is the convergence of all the **Yang** pathways. *(See the diagram: "The Head as the Convergence of All Yang Pathways.")*

All the **Yin** vessels ascend only to the neck and chest before descending. The **Hand Minor Yin** (Heart channel) originates from the heart’s pericardial region beside the throat, ascends to the lungs, emerges beneath the armpit, and descends along the inner posterior aspect of the upper arm. The **Foot Minor Yin** (Kidney channel) rises from the kidneys, pierces the liver and diaphragm, enters the lungs, follows the throat, interconnects with the heart, and converges in the chest. The **Hand Major Yin** (Lung channel) originates from the lungs, emerges beneath the armpit, and descends along the inner upper arm. The **Foot Major Yin** (Spleen channel) ascends from the stomach through the diaphragm and converges in the heart. The **Hand Jueyin** (Pericardium channel) courses through the chest, emerges at the ribs, descends three *cun* below the armpit, and follows the inner upper arm. The **Foot Jueyin** (Liver channel) ascends through the diaphragm, spreads across the ribs, and follows the posterior throat.

Only the **Yang** pathways ascend to the head and ears. The **Yin** pathways do not reach the head—only the **Yang** pathways do. Thus, the face can endure cold.

Since **Yang qi** resides in the head and face, it can withstand cold.

*(Section: "Discerning Empty, Full, Evil, and True" #1)*

**Question Forty-Eight:**

There are three kinds of **emptiness** and **fullness** in a person—how so?

There are three kinds of emptiness and three kinds of fullness in a person. Thus:

There is the **emptiness and fullness of the pulse**. The pulse has emptiness and fullness.
There is the **emptiness and fullness of the disease**. Disease has emptiness and fullness.
There is the **emptiness and fullness of diagnosis**. Diagnosis has emptiness and fullness.

The **emptiness and fullness of the pulse**:
A **soft** pulse is **emptiness**; a **tight and firm** pulse is **fullness**.
A soft pulse is **Yin emptiness**, hence called **emptiness**; a tight and firm pulse is **Yang fullness**, hence called **fullness**.

The **emptiness and fullness of disease**:
There are three kinds.
**Outward manifestations** are **emptiness**; **inward manifestations** are **fullness**.
First, **Yin and Yang** govern the **inner and outer**—now if **Yang is insufficient**, **Yin** surges outward to dominate, hence **outward** is **emptiness**. If **Yin is insufficient**, **Yang** invades inward to dominate, and **Yang** prevails externally, hence **inward** is **fullness**.
**Speech** is **emptiness**; **silence** is **fullness**.
Second, the **lungs govern voice**; when **voice enters the heart**, it is **speech**, hence **emptiness**. The **liver governs planning**; when it enters the heart, there is **no speech**, hence **silence** is **fullness**.
**Sluggishness** is **emptiness**; **urgency** is **fullness**.
Third, **Yang governs emptiness**, and **Yang** is **urgent**; **tight and tense skin** is **fullness**. **Yin governs stillness**, and **Yin** is **sluggish**; **loose and relaxed skin** is **emptiness**.

The **emptiness and fullness of diagnosis**:
There are four kinds.
A **soft** pulse is **emptiness**; a **firm** pulse is **fullness**.
That is, **soft and relaxed skin** is **emptiness**; **tight and strong skin** is **fullness**.
**Itching** is **emptiness**; **pain** is **fullness**.
Second, **general itching** is **emptiness**; **localized pain** is **fullness**.
**External pain with internal relief** is **external fullness and internal emptiness**.
Third, if **light pressure causes pain**, it is **external fullness**—the disease is shallow. If **heavy pressure brings relief**, it is **internal emptiness**—the disease is deep.
**Internal pain with external relief** is **internal fullness and external emptiness**.
Fourth, if **heavy pressure causes pain**, it is **internal fullness**—the disease is deep. If **light pressure brings relief**, it is **external emptiness**—the disease is shallow.

Thus it is said: **emptiness and fullness**.

Moreover, in summary:

**The Forty-Nine Difficulties:**

There are diseases arising from the **True Channels** themselves.
When the **True Channels** are **empty**, the **remaining tendons** slacken.
When the **victorious tendons** slacken, the ailment **emanates internally** and does not originate externally.

There are diseases caused by **external pathogens** invading the **True Channels**.
The **Five Phases** counter one another—**evil** enters from without.
How may they be distinguished? How are these two cases differentiated?

It is said:
**Worry, grief, and overthinking** injure the **Heart**.
The **Heart** governs **spirit**—the sovereign of the **Five Viscera**, the source of **cleverness** and **intuitive wisdom**.
Excessive **grief and labor** weaken the **Heart**, and when the **Heart** is injured, the **spirit** grows feeble.

**Cold exposure and excessive drinking** injure the **Lungs**.
The **Lungs** govern **skin and fur**—when the body is cold, the **skin and fur** are chilled.
They also receive **fluids and grains**; excessive drinking harms them, and the **Lungs** detest cold, hence it is said they are injured.

**Anger and rebellious *qi*** injure the **Liver**.
The **Liver** governs **planning and foresight**; the **Gallbladder** governs **courage and decisiveness**.
Though anger stems from **will**, if it is excessive, injury may occur.

**Overindulgence in food and exhaustion** injure the **Spleen**.
Doubling one’s usual intake harms the **intestines and stomach**.
When food is consumed to excess, the **stomach’s *qi*** becomes full, and the **Spleen’s vessels** are often strained.
Or, from **riding swiftly, leaping, or excessive sexual activity**, the **Spleen** may also be injured.

**Prolonged sitting in damp places or excessive exertion in water** injures the **Kidneys**.
The **loins** are the **Kidneys’ domain**; prolonged sitting obstructs the **Kidney *qi*** from flowing freely, hence damage occurs.
The **Kidney’s acupuncture point** lies at the **sole of the foot**, named **Yǒngquán (Surging Spring)**.
Residing in damp places also causes harm.
**Excessive exertion** refers to lifting heavy objects or drawing a bow;
**Entering water** refers to crossing water and falling, or **breathing difficulties** arising from the **Kidneys**, or a woman whose **menstrual flow has not ceased** yet engaging in intercourse.
