These are diseases arising from the **True Channels** themselves.

These five cases are all **self-inflicted by the True Channels**—all classified as **other evils**.
What are called the **Five Evils**?
*(Referring to external pathogens affecting the Five Viscera.)*

There is **Wind Invasion**.
*"Invasion"* means injury.
The **Liver** corresponds to **Wind**; when evil disperses through the **Five Viscera**, it manifests as **five colors**.
If **Wind** injures in **Spring**, lingering evil leads to **chronic diarrhea**.

There is **Summerheat Injury**.
**Summerheat** favors the **Heart**; when evil enters the **Five Viscera**, it produces **five odors**.
If **Summerheat** injures in **Summer**, **Autumn** brings **wasting and intermittent fever**.

There is **Overindulgence in Food and Exhaustion**.
The **Spleen** governs **taste**; when evil enters the **Five Viscera**, it manifests as **five flavors**.
*(Previously, self-inflicted by the True Channels referred to overindulgence and exhaustion.)*
Now, the **Five Evils** also mention **overindulgence and exhaustion**.
Disease from the **True Channels** refers to **emptiness** in the **True Channels**.
Additionally, **Five Evils** injure through **food and drink**, meaning **disease arises from overindulgence damaging the Spleen**.

There is **Cold Injury**.
The **Lungs** govern **dryness**, and their **order is pure and sharp**.
The **Lungs** govern **sound**; when evil disperses into the **Five Viscera**, it produces **five sounds**.
The **Lungs** govern **skin and fur**, detest cold—if **Cold** injures in **Winter**, **Spring** brings **warm diseases**.

There is **Dampness Invasion**.
The **Kidneys** govern **water** and **dampness**; when evil enters the **Five Viscera**, it manifests as **five fluids**.
If **Dampness** injures in **Autumn**, it **rebels upward**, causing **coughing**, and manifests as **wasting and tetany**.

These are the **Five Evils**.
These five diseases originate externally.

Suppose the **Liver** is diseased.
If the **Liver** does not **receive** disease, how do we know it was caused by **Wind Invasion**?
*(Asking about the cause of Wind Invasion.)*

It is said:
Its **color should be green**.
**Wind** corresponds to **Perseverance (Xùn)**, belongs to **Wood**, and its color is **green**.
How is this known?
The **Liver** governs **color**.
The **flower and calyx of Wood** spread the **five colors**, generating the **Five Evils**.
When **Wind** enters internally, it manifests as **green**.
*(Self-inflicted by the True Channels.)*
When it enters the **Heart**, it becomes **red**.
When **Liver evil** enters the **Heart**, its color turns **red**.
When it enters the **Spleen**, it becomes **yellow**.
When **Liver evil** enters the **Spleen**, its color is **yellow**.
When it enters the **Lungs**, it becomes **white**.
When **Liver evil** enters the **Lungs**, its color is **white**.
When it enters the **Kidneys**, it becomes **black**.
When **Liver evil** enters the **Kidneys**, its color is **black**.
Since the **Liver** is the **Heart’s evil**, it is known that the **color should be red**.
The **Liver** governs **Wind**; the **Heart** governs **Summerheat**.
Now, if the **Heart** is diseased by **Wind Invasion**, it is known that **Liver evil** has invaded the **Heart**.
The disease presents as **feverish body** and **distended, painful flanks**.
The **Heart** governs **Summerheat**; when diseased, the body **fevers**.
The **Liver** spreads across the **flanks**, hence **flank distension**.
Its **pulse** is **floating, large, and taut**.
A **floating, large pulse** belongs to the **Heart**; a **taut pulse** belongs to the **Liver**.

How do we know it was caused by **Summerheat Injury**?
*(Asking about the cause of Summerheat Injury.)*

It is said:
It **dislikes foul odors**.
The **Heart** governs **Summerheat** and **odors**.
The **Heart** governs **odors**.
The **Heart** is **Fire**; the **transformations of Fire** produce **odors**.
When **Fire** enters internally, it becomes **scorched odor**.
**Fire’s** nature is to **flame upward**, generating **bitter, scorched** qualities—*(self-inflicted by the True Channels.)*
When it enters the **Spleen**, it becomes **fragrant odor**.
**Fire** transforming **Earth** yields a **fragrant** odor.
When it enters the **Liver**, it becomes **irritating odor**.
**Fire** transforming **Wood** yields an **irritating** odor.
When it enters the **Kidneys**, it becomes **putrid odor**.
**Fire** transforming **Water** yields a **putrid** odor.
When it enters the **Lungs**, it becomes **rancid odor**.
**Fire** transforming **Metal** yields a **rancid** odor.
Thus, when the **Heart** is diseased by **Summerheat Injury**, it **dislikes foul odors**.
Because the **Heart’s** disease stems from **Summerheat Injury**, it **dislikes foul odors**.
The disease presents as **feverish body, restlessness, and heart pain**.
*(See the sixteenth difficulty for annotation.)*
Its **pulse** is **floating, large, and scattered**.
The **Heart’s** self-inflicted pulse is **floating**.

How do we know it was caused by **overindulgence in food and exhaustion**?
*(Asking about the cause of overindulgence and exhaustion.)*

It is said:
It **craves bitter flavors**—when **empty**, it **lacks appetite**; when **full**, it **desires food**.
The **Spleen** governs **taste**, hence it is known.
When the **Spleen Channel** is **empty**, there is **no appetite**; when **full**, there is **desire for food**.
How is this known?
*(Asking about preferred flavors.)*
The **Spleen** governs **taste**.
The **Spleen** governs **sweetness**; **sweetness** receives **flavor**, hence it governs **taste**.
When it enters the **Liver**, it becomes **sour**.
When **Spleen evil** overcomes the **Liver**, it **craves sour**.
When it enters the **Heart**, it becomes **bitter**.
When **Spleen evil** overcomes the **Heart**, it **craves bitter**.
When it enters the **Lungs**, it becomes **spicy**.
When **Spleen evil** enters the **Lungs**, it **craves spicy**.
When it enters the **Kidneys**, it becomes **salty**.
When **Spleen evil** enters the **Kidneys**, it **craves salty**.
When it enters internally, it **craves sweet**.
*(Self-inflicted by the True Channels, hence it craves sweet.)*
Thus, it is known that **Spleen evil** has entered the **Heart**, causing a **craving for bitter flavors**.
The **Heart** governs **heat injury**; the **Spleen** governs **exhaustion**.
Now, if the **Heart’s** disease arises from **overindulgence and exhaustion**, it is known that **Spleen evil** has entered the **Heart**, causing a **craving for bitter flavors**.

The illness manifests as **fever with a heavy body**, aversion to wind, and **limbs failing to withdraw**.
Fever belongs to the **Heart**, heaviness to the **Spleen**, and governs the **limbs’ inability to contract**.
Its pulse is **floating, large, and slack**.
A **floating, large pulse** belongs to the **Heart**; **slackness** belongs to the **Spleen**.
How do we know it arises from **cold damage**?
Asking about **cold damage**.
Indeed: **delirious speech and incoherent words** will occur.
Once injured by cold, speech should become **delirious and incoherent**.
How so?
How does speech become **delirious and incoherent**?
The **Lungs govern voice**. When **Metal** is struck, sound emerges, hence the **Five Tones** arise from the **Lungs**.
When it enters the **Liver**, it becomes **groaning**.
**Wood fears Metal**, hence **groaning**.
When it enters the **Heart**, it becomes **speech**.
**Metal and Fire** correspond; **husband and wife** meet, hence **speech and laughter**.
When it enters the **Spleen**, it becomes **singing**.
**Earth**, as mother to **Metal** and child to **Metal**, when mother and child meet, hence **singing**.
When it enters the **Kidneys**, it becomes **moaning**.
**Metal** is mother to **Water**; when child meets mother, it emits **soft, plaintive sounds**.
When it enters internally, it becomes **weeping**.
The **Lungs govern Autumn**; **Autumn brings sorrow**. Its tone is **Shang**, and **Shang** is **injury**.
Thus, it weeps of its own accord.
Hence, it is known that **Lung evil** has entered the **Heart**, causing **delirious, incoherent speech**.
The **Heart governs heat**; the **Lungs govern cold**.
**Delirious, incoherent speech** all stems from **Lung evil entering the Heart**.

The illness manifests as **fever, aversion to alcohol and wind**, and in severe cases, **wheezing and coughing**.
Fever belongs to the **Heart**; aversion to cold and **wheezing/coughing** belong to the **Lungs**.
Its pulse is **floating, large, and rough**.
A **floating, large pulse** belongs to the **Heart**; **roughness** belongs to the **Lungs**.
How do we know it arises from **damp damage**?
Asking about the cause of **damp damage**.
Indeed: **excessive sweating that cannot be stopped** will occur.
Once injured by dampness, **spontaneous sweating** will not cease.
How so?
How does **spontaneous sweating** occur?
The **Kidneys govern dampness**. The **Kidneys govern water**; water flows as dampness, and the **Five Dampnesses** all arise from the **Kidneys**.
When it enters the **Liver**, it becomes **weeping**.
**Grief and sorrow** move the **mind**, injuring the **spirit**; when the **spirit** is injured, it is moved to **weep**.
It is said the **Lungs govern sorrow**; sorrow causes **Metal excess**, and **Wood fears it**.
**Water** is the **mother of Wood**; when the mother is troubled, the child ceases to thrive.
Thus, the **Liver** enters as **weeping**.
When it enters the **Heart**, it becomes **sweating**.
**Water and Fire** harmonize; their interaction **steams as sweat**.

When it enters the **Spleen**, it becomes **drooling** (salivation).
**Earth** is the **husband** of **Water**, and **Water** follows **Earth** to generate **drooling**.

When it enters the **Lungs**, it becomes **nasal mucus** (rhinorrhea).
The **North** generates **Cold**; **Cold** generates the **Kidneys**.
Now, **Cold** affects the **skin and hair**, and internally converges with the **Lungs**.
When the **Lungs** are **Cold**, **nasal mucus** flows.
When it enters the **Liver** itself, it becomes **spitting** (ptyalism).
The **Kidney’s pulse** ascends to connect with the **tongue**,
so when the **Liver** itself is diseased, it manifests as **spitting**.

Thus, it is known that **Liver evil** entering the **Heart** causes **spontaneous sweating** that cannot cease.
Thus, it is known that **spontaneous sweating** that does not cease is all due to **Liver evil** entering the **Heart**.

When the disease presents with **body heat** but **lower abdomen pain** and **cold-induced leg cramps**,
**body heat** with **Heart** disease—all others are **Kidney** diseases—see the **Sixteen Difficulties** (of the *Yellow Emperor’s Inner Canon*).
Its pulse is **deep and soft** yet **large**.
**Large** indicates **Heart** **fullness** (excess),
**deep and soft** indicates **Kidney** **fullness** (excess).
This is the law of the **Five Evils** (五邪).
The **pulse patterns of the Five Evils** are thus measured.

The **Fifty Difficulties** (of the *Yellow Emperor’s Inner Canon*) say:
There are **Vacuous Evils**, **Solid Evils**,
**Thief Evils**, **Micro Evils**, and **Direct Evils**—how do they differ?
Asking of these **Five Evils**, where do they distinguish?

Now:
**Evil** coming from what follows is **Vacuous Evil**.
When the **Heart** governs its time, the pulse should be **surging and large** yet **long**,
but instead it is **taut and small** yet **urgent**—this is the **Liver** governing **thought**,
**Wood** transmitting to the **Heart**, seizing the **Heart’s governance**.
This is the **Liver** overpowering the **Heart**,
hence the saying "coming from what follows."
The **Liver** is the **mother** of the **Heart**;
when the **mother** overpowers the **child**, this is **Vacuous Evil**.

**Evil** coming from what precedes is **Solid Evil**.
The **Heart**’s governance is taken by **Spleen fullness** (excess),
and when the **Heart**’s governance is complete, it should transmit to the **Spleen**.
Now, the **Heart**’s governance is not yet complete,
so the **Spleen** comes prematurely to seize its governance—
hence the saying "coming from what precedes."
The **Spleen** is the **child** of the **Heart**;
when the **child** overpowers the **mother**, this is **Solid Evil**.

**Evil** coming from what does not conquer is **Thief Evil**.
**Fire** does not conquer **Water**,
but now **Kidney** **fullness** (excess) comes to overpower—
hence it is **Thief Evil**.

**Evil** coming from what does conquer is **Micro Evil**.
**Fire** conquers **Metal** (金),
so when the **Heart** is diseased and **Lung fullness** (excess) comes to overpower,
it is called **Micro Evil**.

**Evil** arising from itself is **Direct Evil**.
When the **Heart** governs its time, **fullness** (excess) should not be overbearing,
nor should **emptiness** (deficiency) be too weak—
this is **Direct Evil**.
And there are no other **evils** overpowering it.

How so?

「Further inquiry: If the **Heart** is afflicted by **heat** (暑), it is **Direct Evil**.
The **Heart** governs **summer heat** (暑), and when it is itself diseased by **heat** (暑), this is **Direct Evil**.

Excessive **diet and exertion** (饮食劳倦) result in **Solid Evil**.
When **Evil** comes from what precedes, the **Spleen** overpowers the **Heart**—hence it is **Solid Evil**.

Affliction by **cold** (寒) results in **Micro Evil**.
When **Evil** comes from what does conquer, the **Lung** overpowers the **Heart**; the **Lung** governs **cold** (寒), and it also fears the **Heart**—hence it is **Micro Evil**.

Excessive **dampness** (湿) results in **Thief Evil**.
When **Evil** comes from what does not fully conquer, the **Kidney** overpowers the **Heart**; the **Kidney** governs **dampness** (湿), and **Water** subdues **Fire**—hence it is **Thief Evil**.

The *Fifty-One Difficulties* (五十一难) states:
There are those who, when sick, **desire warmth** (欲得温).
There are those who, when sick, **desire warm and sheltered places** (欲得温暖之处).
There are those who **desire cold** (欲得寒).
There are those who, when sick, **desire cold and freezing places** (欲得寒冻之处).
There are those who **desire to be seen by others** (欲得见人).
There are those who, when sick, **desire to be seen by others** (欲与人相见).
There are those who **do not desire to be seen by others** (不欲得见人).
There are those who, when sick, **do not desire to be seen by others** (不欲与人相见).
Yet each case varies.
Their desires differ.»

Where does the illness reside—within the **zàng** (藏) or **fǔ** (府)?

The afflicted **zàng** (藏) and **fǔ** (府) are as follows:
Yet: those who, when sick, **desire to be seen by others** (欲得见人), have their illness in the **fǔ** (府). The **fǔ** (府) governs all **illnesses of excess** (府万物病在府), hence they desire to be seen by others.

Those who **desire warmth** (欲得温) but **do not desire to be seen by others** (不欲得见人), have their illness in the **zàng** (藏). The **zàng** (藏) belong to **yin** (阴), and **yin** illnesses desire warmth, hence they seek warmth but avoid being seen by others.

How so? Again inquired: **fǔ** (府) are **yang** (阳). The Six **fǔ** (府) belong to **yang** (阳). **Yang** illnesses desire cold, yet also wish to be seen by others. The **hand’s three yin meridians and three yang meridians** (手三阴三阳) correspond to Heaven, governing **dryness** (暄者燥), hence they seek cold and desire to be seen by others.

The **zàng** (藏) belong to **yin** (阴). The Five **zàng** (藏) belong to **yin** (阴). **Yin** illnesses desire warmth. The **foot’s one yin meridian and three yang meridians** (足一阴三阳) correspond to Earth, governing **illnesses of cold and warmth** (主病寒温), hence they desire warmth. They also wish to **retreat into seclusion alone** (闭户独处), for **yin** governs the interior, hence they seek to **retreat into seclusion alone from the exterior** (独处於外). They **loathe hearing human voices** (恶闻人声), for **yin** governs stillness, hence they loathe hearing human voices.

Thus, one may distinguish between **zàng** (藏) and **fǔ** (府) illnesses by their desires.

Thus, one may **discriminate between zàng (藏) and fǔ (府) illnesses by their desires**.

The *Fifty-Two Difficulties* (五十二难) states:
Are the **fǔ** (府) and **zàng** (藏) illnesses rooted in the same origin? When **fǔ** (府) and **zàng** (藏) illnesses manifest, do they share the same root cause?

Yet: they are not the same. **Final Answer**: They are never the same.

How then do they differ? *How* do they differ?

Yet: **Zàng** illnesses **remain fixed and do not shift**. **Zàng** illnesses belong to **yin**, and **yin** governs stillness, hence they remain fixed and do not move. Their illness does not depart from its place. The illness remains in one place and does not stray.

**Fǔ** illnesses, however, **manifest diffusely and surge onward**. **Fǔ** illnesses belong to **yang**, and **yang** governs motion, hence their manifestations surge and rush upward. They flow upward or downward. Their dwelling is not constant. The illness does not remain fixed in one place but wanders without a fixed abode.

**The Eighth Transmission of Zàng-Fǔ Illnesses** (藏府传病第八)
The *Fifty-Third Difficulty* states:

The classics say: *"Seven transmissions bring death."* (经言七传者死)
See *Discriminating Doubts* (详见辩疑) for details.

**Transmission** refers to the Five Phases generating in sequence—counting to five, then recounting back to two, making seven. The five ascend to transmit to seven, and seven is subdued, hence it is said.

**Transmission to a zàng** means life. (间藏者生)
*Transmission* question: What does it mean for one **zàng** to transmit?

How can transmission be the same, yet life and death differ?

Yet: **Seven transmissions** mean transmitting to what **conquers** it. Transmitting to the **zàng** that subdues it, hence it is called **seven transmissions**.

**Transmission to a zàng** means transmitting to its **child**. Transmitting to the **zàng** it generates, hence it is called **transmission to a zàng**.

Why then is it called **one transmission**? (何以一甭之？) *Another question.*

**Transmission of Organ Diseases**

Suppose the heart is diseased and transmits to the lungs. Water, wood, fire, earth, and metal—fire is the fifth phase—overcomes the sixth phase, earth, and metal is subdued by fire, so death ensues. The rest follow this pattern accordingly.

The lungs transmit to the liver. Metal subdues wood. The liver transmits to the spleen. Wood subdues earth. The spleen transmits to the kidneys. Earth subdues water. The kidneys transmit to the heart. Water subdues fire.

One organ does not repeatedly transmit harm. For instance, if the heart transmits to the lungs, and the lungs die without further transmission, then the transmission halts at one organ, and the first organ does not transmit again. Thus, it is said that seven transmissions lead to death.

**Seven transmissions** mean death. The seventh transmission means death.

**Transmission to an organ** means transmitting to its offspring. The mother can generate the child, and when an organ transmits to its child, it is called *transmission to an organ*.

For example, if the heart’s disease transmits to the spleen: the heart transmits to the spleen, and the spleen receives the *generative qi*, which is the mother-child transmission, hence it is called *generation*.

The heart overcomes the lungs, and the spleen transmits to them. The spleen overcomes the kidneys, and the lungs transmit to them. The lungs overcome the spleen, and the kidneys transmit to them. The kidneys overcome the heart, and the liver transmits to them. The liver overcomes wood, and the heart transmits to it.

Thus, the mother and child mutually transmit and cycle endlessly, like an unbroken ring, hence it is called *generation*.

The mother and child mutually transmit and cycle endlessly, yet there is no final exhaustion, hence there is the principle of *eternal generation*.

**The 54th Question:** Organ diseases are difficult to treat. Why are diseases of the five *zàng* (solid organs) the most difficult to cure?

Diseases of the six *fǔ* (hollow organs) are easier to treat. How so?

Thus: Organ diseases are difficult to treat because they transmit to what overcomes them. The liver overcomes the spleen, the spleen overcomes the kidneys, the kidneys overcome the heart, the heart overcomes the lungs, and the lungs overcome the liver—hence they are difficult to treat.

Diseases of the hollow organs are easier to treat because they transmit to their offspring. Wood transmits to fire, fire transmits to earth, earth transmits to metal, metal transmits to water, water transmits to wood, and wood *inversely* generates wood—hence they are easier to treat. This follows the same principle as *seven transmissions* and *transmission to an organ*.

This aligns with the methods of the previous chapter.

**The 92nd Question on Organ Accumulation:** The 55th Question asks: How do we distinguish between *accumulation* and *conglomeration* diseases?

There are diseases of accumulation and diseases of conglomeration—how do we differentiate them?

Thus: *Accumulation* refers to *yin qi*. When *yin qi* congeals, it forms accumulation.

*Conglomeration* refers to *yang qi*. When *yang qi* gathers, it forms conglomeration.

Thus, *yin* is deep and latent. *Yin* by nature is still, often submerged and hidden within.

*Yang* is superficial and dynamic. *Yang* by nature is active, floating and manifesting above.

Where *qi* accumulates, it is called *accumulation*. Where *qi* gathers, it is called *conglomeration*.

Thus, *accumulation* arises from the five *zàng* (solid organs). *Accumulation* is *yin qi* from the five *zàng*—when they transmit to what overcomes them, and the *Way* does not gather them, they congeal and form *accumulation*.

They have a fixed starting point. The liver accumulates in the left flank, the lungs in the right flank, the heart above the navel, the kidneys below the navel, and the spleen in the middle of the bladder region—each has its fixed location.

Their pain does not stray from its region. The ache remains localized to its respective area.

They have defined upper and lower limits. The heart’s region extends upward to the navel, the kidneys downward to the navel—each has its defined limits.

They have defined left and right boundaries. The left flank and right flank each have their own defined regions.

*Conglomeration* refers to *yang qi*. The six *fǔ* (hollow organs) belong to *yang*, and the five conglomerations are formed by *yang qi*.

Their onset has no root. When the illness begins, there is no fixed origin.

They do not linger above or below. The *qi* that gathers shifts unpredictably, never remaining fixed above or below the navel.

Their pain has no fixed location—this is called *conglomeration*. The ache is not confined to a single region, hence it is called *conglomeration*.

Thus, we distinguish between *accumulation* and *conglomeration* based on whether they originate from the *zàng* or *fǔ*.

**The 56th Question:** Do the five *zàng* (solid organs) each have their own named *accumulations*?

*Accumulation* means *stagnation*. When blood and *qi* do not flow freely and stagnate, it forms disease. Do they each have their own names?

By which month and day are they acquired?

「By which month and day does one acquire this *accumulation*?

Now, the *accumulation* of the liver is called *féiqì* (肥气).
*Féiqì* is like the appearance of fat and abundant flesh—this is the liver’s *accumulation*, often manifesting as a slight distension with frequent occurrences.
It appears in the left lateral costal region, like a cup inverted over it, with a distinct head and foot.
It originates in the left lateral costal area, then **#35** spreads outward like an inverted cup, protruding with a clear head and foot.
If left untreated for long, it does not heal.
If it persists for long without proper relief, it fails to resolve.
It causes one to develop coughing, reverse breathing, **lump** (瘤), and **ague** (疟), year after year without cessation.
It leads to **#34** wheezing, reverse breathing, **emaciation** (瘠), and **ague**, enduring through successive years without abating.
It is acquired in the **late summer** (季夏) on days *wù-jǐ* (戊己日).

Late summer is the sixth month, when the spleen—**#36** the sovereign of earth—holds its peak.
*Wù-jǐ* are earth days, and since the spleen’s **#36** *qi* is sovereign in late summer, it does not receive evil.
Thus, the liver’s *accumulation* is obtained on *wù-jǐ* days.
Why is this so?
The liver receives *féiqì* on *wù-jǐ* days.
When lung illness spreads to the liver,
The five *zàng* transmit illness to their respective **#37** dominants; initially, the lung’s illness affects the liver, as lung metal **#37** overcomes liver wood, so the lung transmits its evil to the liver.
The liver should then transmit it to the spleen.
Since liver wood **#38** overcomes spleen earth, the liver does not receive the lung’s evil, and so transmits it to the spleen.
The spleen, in its sovereign state during late summer, does not receive evil.
Late summer is the time when the spleen—**#36** the sovereign of earth—holds its peak, and thus does not receive the evil transmitted by the liver.
The liver, having failed to transmit the evil, **#39** seeks to return it to the lung.
The liver, unable to transmit the evil due to the spleen’s sovereignty, thus **#39** refuses to align with the pulse’s natural flow.
The lung refuses to receive it, so the evil remains and congeals into an *accumulation*.
The lung also refuses to receive it, so the evil lingers and congeals, forming the *féiqì* *accumulation*, which grows and solidifies, and the illness thus develops.
Hence, one knows that *féiqì* is acquired in late summer on *wù-jǐ* days.
Hence, one knows that in the sixth month on *wù-jǐ* days, the *féiqì* *accumulation* is obtained.

The *accumulation* of the heart is called *fúlìang* (伏梁).
*Fúlìang* is like the beams and rafters of an old house—this is the heart’s *accumulation*.
It rises from the **#32** *qí* (齐) above, swelling as large as an arm, then ascends to the heart’s lower region.
It **#32** begins at *qí* above, swelling to the size of an arm, and then **#33** ascends in reverse to the heart’s lower region.
If left untreated for long, it does not heal.
If it persists for long without proper relief, it fails to resolve.**»**

*«It vexes the mind. It causes the heart’s lower region to be vexed by *fúlìang* **#40**.
Hence, one knows that *fúlìang* is acquired in autumn on *gēng-xīn* **#41** days.
In autumn, the month of *xuán* **#41** (弦) and *tún* (豚), when *jīn* **#41** (金) is in power and *wáng* **#41** (王) rules, *gēng-xīn* are the days of *jīn*—thus, *fúlìang* is obtained on *gēng-xīn* days.
How does one know this? *Fúlìang* is acquired on *gēng-xīn* days.

When kidney illness transmits to the heart, it first subtly affects the kidney.
Kidney water overcomes heart fire, so *fúlìang* is obtained.
The heart should then transmit to the lungs.
If the heart does not receive the kidney’s evil, heart fire overcomes lung *jīn* **#41**, and it should transmit to the lungs.
In autumn, the lungs rule, and *wáng* **#41** (王) does not receive evil.
In the month when lung *jīn* **#41** rules, it does not receive the evil transmitted by the heart.
The heart then desires to return to the kidney.
With the lungs ruling in autumn and not receiving evil, the heart again seeks to transmit back to the kidney.
If the kidney cannot **#42** receive it, it congeals and forms an *accumulation*.
If the kidney does not receive it, then *fúlìang* **#40** thus congeals and forms an *accumulation*.
Hence, one knows that *fúlìang* is acquired in autumn on *gēng-xīn* days.
Hence, one knows that in autumn on *gēng-xīn* days, the *fúlìang* *accumulation* is obtained.

The *accumulation* of the spleen is called *pǐqì* (痞气).
*Pǐ* (痞) means stagnation.
Stagnation congeals into an *accumulation*—this is the spleen’s *accumulation*.
It lodges in the stomach’s precincts, swelling as large as a plate.
*Pǐqì* congeals over the stomach’s precincts, swelling like a plate.
If left untreated for long, it does not heal.
If it persists for long without proper relief, it fails to resolve.**»**

*«It causes the four limbs to fail in contraction.
The spleen governs the four limbs, which cannot be gathered or controlled.
It induces jaundice.
Jaundice—when the body and hands and feet all turn yellow.
Food and drink do not nourish the flesh.
The spleen and stomach are the repositories and granaries of sustenance, excelling in intake and reception, known as *shí* (食#43). [It is acquired] on winter *rén-guì* days.
Winter is the month when the kidney’s water reigns supreme; *rén-guì* are the water days, and *pǐqì* (痞气) is obtained on these days.
How so?
On *rén-guì* days, one acquires the *pǐqì* (痞气) *accumulation*.

Liver illness transmits to the spleen.
Initially, it first affects the liver; when liver wood overcomes spleen earth, it is transmitted to the liver.
The spleen should transmit to the kidney.
The spleen, not receiving the liver’s evil, with spleen earth overcoming kidney water, should transmit to the kidney.
The kidney, in winter, is the reigning organ, and the sovereign does not receive evil.
In winter, the month when kidney water reigns, it does not receive the evil transmitted by the spleen.
The spleen then desires to return to the liver.
The spleen, having balanced the kidney but not receiving the evil, again seeks to return to the liver.
The liver refuses to receive it, so it congeals and forms an *accumulation*.
The liver further refuses to receive it, so it thus congeals and forms the *accumulation* of *pǐqì* (痞气).
Hence, one knows that *pǐqì* (痞气) is acquired on *rén-guì* (壬癸) days.
Hence, one knows that on *rén-guì* days, the *pǐqì* (痞气) *accumulation* is obtained.

The lung’s *accumulation*, named *xī-bēn* (息贲).
*Xī* (息) means surface#46.
*Bēn* (贲), is *lì* (鬲). [It signifies] the lung’s *qi* stagnating above the *lì* (鬲), gradually growing and pressing against it—this is the name for lung *accumulation*.
It lodges beneath the right flank, swelling as large as a cup.
The *accumulation* covers the right flank, swelling like a cup.
If left untreated for long, it does not cease.
If it persists for long without proper relief, it does not subside.*»*

「It causes the patient to shiver with cold and heat.
When the lung is *empty* (虚), the shivering with cold is *xī* (洒淅).
When the lung is *full* (实), there is heat and oppression.
Shortness of breath and coughing indicate lung *obstruction* (壅).
If the lung is cold, the *Dao/Way* of its *qi* becomes constricted,
changing the shortness of breath and coughing into lung *obstruction*.
Hence, one knows it is acquired on *spring rén-jǐ* (甲乙) days.
In spring, the month ruled by liver *wood* (木), *rén-jǐ* (甲乙) is the *wood* (木) phase—
the *xī-bēn* (息贲) *accumulation* is obtained on *rén-jǐ* (甲乙) days.
Why so? Because *xī-bēn* (息贲) is acquired on *rén-jǐ* (甲乙) days.

When the heart is diseased, it transmits to the lung.
If one initially suffers from heart disease,
the heart *fire* (火) overpowers the lung *metal* (金),
so the disease transmits to the lung#47.
The lung should then transmit to the liver.
If the lung refuses to receive the heart’s *evil* (邪),
the *metal* (金) overpowers the *liver wood* (肝木),
and the disease should transmit to the liver#48.
The liver, in spring, is the sovereign *wood* (木) phase—
a sovereign does not receive *evil* (邪).
In spring, the month ruled by liver *wood*,
it does not receive the *evil* (邪) transmitted by the lung.
The lung then seeks to return to the heart.
Since the lung is ruled by the liver *wood* and refuses *evil*,
it again seeks to transmit back to the heart.
The heart does not accept it,
so it congeals and forms an *accumulation*.
The heart further refuses to receive it,
so it thus congeals and forms the *accumulation* of *xī-bēn* (息贲).
Hence, one knows that *xī-bēn* (息贲) is acquired on *spring rén-jǐ* (甲乙) days.
Hence, one knows that on *spring rén-jǐ* days, the *xī-bēn* (息贲) *accumulation* is obtained.

The kidney’s *accumulation*, named *bēn-tún* (贲豚).
*Bēn* (贲) means *congealed mass* (聚).
*Tún* (豚) resembles the shape of a *piglet* (豚状),
thus the name for kidney *accumulation*.
It manifests in the lower abdomen,
rising as far as beneath the heart—
its shape is like a *piglet*,
sometimes rising, sometimes descending without fixed timing.
Beginning from the lower abdomen,
it surges upward toward the heart—
its form is like a *piglet*,
now ascending, now descending at no set hour.
If left untreated for long, it does not cease.
If it persists for long without proper relief, it does not subside.»

「It causes *shortness of breath with wheezing* (喘逆).
It causes *sticky breath and wheezing* (沾息喘逆).
It leads to *weakened bones and diminished qi* (骨痿少气).
The kidney governs the bones; when bones are withered and marrow is exhausted,
it manifests as *bone weakness* (骨痿).
*Weakness* (痿) means *lack of strength* (无力).
It is acquired on *summer bǐng-dīng* (丙丁) days.

In the months when the heart’s fire reigns supreme in summer,
*bǐng-dīng* (丙丁) are the fire days—
on these days, the *bēn-tún* (贲豚) *accumulation* is obtained.
How does one know this?
One knows that on *summer bǐng-dīng* days,
the *bēn-tún* (贲豚) *accumulation* is obtained.

When the spleen is diseased, it transmits to the kidney.
Initially, the spleen’s disease is *sticky breath* (沾息),
whereby earth of the spleen overpowers water of the kidney,
thus transmitting to the kidney.
The kidney should then transmit to the heart.
The kidney does not receive the spleen’s *pathogenic influence* (邪),
as kidney water overpowers heart fire,
thus transmitting to the heart.
In summer, the heart reigns supreme—
the sovereign does not receive *pathogenic influence*.
In the months when the heart’s fire reigns supreme in summer,
it does not receive the *pathogenic influence* transmitted by the kidney.
The kidney then wishes to return to the spleen.
The kidney, though the heart reigns supreme and it does not receive *pathogenic influence*,
still wishes to transmit back to the spleen.
The spleen refuses to receive it,
thus leaving it congealed as *accumulation*—
the spleen again refuses to receive it,
so it remains congealed as the *bēn-tún* (贲豚) *accumulation*.
Hence, one knows that the *bēn-tún* (贲豚) *accumulation*
is acquired on *summer bǐng-dīng* (丙丁) days.
Hence, one knows that on *bǐng-dīng* (丙丁) days,
the *bēn-tún* (贲豚) *accumulation* is obtained.

This is the essential principle of the *Five Accumulations* (五积).
This is the essential principle of the *Five Accumulations* diseases.

---
*[End of the 7th chapter of the *Yellow Emperor’s Eighty-One Difficulties Canon (Huángdì Bāshíyī Nàn Jīng)*—
*Explanatory Illustrations and Commentary on the Text]
The title of the text: *Yellow Emperor’s Eighty-One Difficulties Canon—Explanatory Illustrations and Commentary on the Text.*」

---

**Chapter 7 of the *Yellow Emperor’s Eighty-One Difficulties Canon—Illustrated and Sentence-by-Sentence Exegesis by Lu Guo and Qin Yue Ren**, compiled by **Lian Zi of Linchuan** (宋李駉撰)

**Title of the Text:** *Yellow Emperor’s Eighty-One Difficulties Canon—Illustrated and Sentence-by-Sentence Exegesis* (黄帝八十一难经慕图句解)

**The Fourteenth Poem on the *Five Discharges* (五泄) and Cold Damage (伤寒):**

**Difficulty 57:** How many kinds of *discharge* (泄) are there?
*Discharge* (泄) means diarrhea (利), but how many varieties exist?
Do they all have names?
Do they each have distinct designations?

Indeed: there are five kinds of *discharge* (泄), each with a different name.
The five varieties of diarrhea (泄利) differ in their *patterns* (令).
There is **stomach discharge** (胃泄), where the stomach meridian (胃经) causes diarrhea.
There is **spleen discharge** (脾泄), where the spleen meridian (脾经) causes diarrhea.
There is **large intestine discharge** (大肠泄), where the large intestine causes diarrhea.
There is **small intestine discharge** (小肠泄), where the small intestine causes diarrhea.
There is **large syndrome discharge** (大症泄), also called **posterior heaviness** (后重).
*痕* (痕) means *congestion* (结). When the lower abdomen (小腹) is congested (有结) but still desires diarrhea (而欲下利), this is **posterior heaviness** (后重), which is why it is named **kidney discharge** (肾泄).

**Stomach discharge** (胃泄) manifests as undigested food passing (饮食不化).
Wind enters the intestines (风入於肠), and earth *qi* (土#1) is obstructed in the stomach (土#1需於胃).
Thus, the stomach expels unprocessed food (泄所食之物), which exits prematurely and remains undigested (先出而不消化).
The stool is yellow in color (色黄).
The spleen governs earth (土), so diarrhea from spleen *qi* imbalance manifests as yellow stool (脾属土，泄利色黄).

**Spleen discharge** (脾泄) causes abdominal distension and fullness (腹胀满).
Excessive rain and dampness (雨淫腹疾) harm the spleen, and the spleen’s earth *qi* (脾土) is adversely affected by dampness (恶湿).
When dampness and spleen *qi* overpower each other (二气之胜), the abdomen swells and distends (故腹肚膨胀充满).
This results in **unregulated diarrhea** (泄注#2).
*注* (注) means without control (无节度).
Excess dampness causes **leaky diarrhea** (濡泻), as damp *qi* attacks the spleen internally (湿气内攻於脾).
This disrupts the separation of fluids and grains (水谷不分), leading to uncontrolled diarrhea (故泄利无度).
Eating triggers vomiting and belching (食即呕吐瞇).
The spleen and stomach are the *zàng-fǔ* (藏府) for digestion.
When the spleen is diseased, it cannot transform fluids and grains (化水谷), so eating causes vomiting and belching (故食则呕吐).

**Large intestine discharge** (大肠泄) manifests as **urgent postprandial diarrhea** (食已窘迫).
*窘迫* (窘迫) means **urgency** (急).
When the large intestine’s *qi* is deficient (大肠气虚), eating completes (食讫) but triggers **urgent wind-like expulsion** (昏凤#3), causing uncontrollable bowel movements (迫急不可止).
The stool is white in color (大便色白), derived from the lung’s white hue (白从肺色).
The intestines rumble with sharp pain (肠呜切痛), as if cut by a knife (肠内虚呜，如刀切其肠之痛).

**Small intestine discharge** (小肠泄) presents as **rumbling followed by purulent bloody stool** (搜而便脓血).
The small intestine is the *fǔ* (府) of the heart (心之府).
The heart generates blood (心生血), so this discharge involves bloody stool (故便血).
Pain is felt in the lower abdomen (小腹痛), where urination occurs (小便处).

**Large syndrome discharge** (大底泄) manifests as **abdominal wrapping pain and posterior heaviness** (裹急后重).
*裹急* (裹急) means **abdominal pain** (腹中痛).
*后重* (后重) means **pain at defecation** (大便处疼痛).
The patient frequently urges to defecate (数至图) but cannot (而不能便).
*囿* (囿) means **toilet** (厕).
The kidney opens into the **two yin orifices** (二阴), but when *qi* is deficient (惟气虚), the urge arises (故数思凤).
Upon reaching the toilet (及至厕), posterior heaviness prevents defecation (后重不能便).
Pain extends into the penis (茎中痛), drawing into the anterior yin orifice (前阴).

This is the **law of the Five Discharges** (五泄之法).
This is the **pathomechanism of the Five Discharges diseases** (五泄受病法度).

**Difficulty 58:** How many kinds of cold damage (伤寒) are there?
When one is struck by cold damage (中伤寒邪), how many varieties exist?
Does the pulse change?
Does the pulse (脉息) exhibit variations?

Indeed: there are five kinds of cold damage (伤寒).
There are five varieties of cold-related illnesses (五般伤病#4).
There is **wind damage** (中风).
When struck by wind *qi* (风邪), it is called **wind damage** (中风).

There is **cold damage** (伤寒).
In winter (冬), it is called **cold damage** (伤寒).
During times of severe cold (诸严寒之时), if one is exposed to cold (为寒所冒) and falls ill (其之时而病),
they experience headache, body ache, and aversion to cold on the skin (头痛身疼，肌肤而恶),
thus earning the name **Five Cold Damages** (五#5伤寒).

There is **damp-warm damage** (湿温#6).
If a person has previously suffered dampness (其人尝伤於湿),
and then contracts wind (因而中风),
with heat and cold alternating (寒热相传#7),
they develop **damp-warm damage** (湿温#8),
marked by **two meridians in opposition** (苦两经逆),
such as excessive sweating in the chest (胸多汗),
head and eye pain (头目痛),
and incoherent speech (妄言).

There is **heat illness** (热病).
In winter, if one is struck by cold (冬伤於寒),
and their *qi* later transforms into heat (因而气发为热病),
they present with fever, aversion to cold, headache, and body ache (发热恶寒，头痛身疼).

There is **warm illness** (温病).
In spring and summer (春夏月#9), when cold is clear,
and in autumn and winter (秋冬), during times of warmth and heat (有暄暑时),
if one contracts epidemic *qi* (疫疠之气),
then throughout the year (一岁之中),
the illness neither wanes nor grows (病无长少),
and its symptoms persist similarly (卒相似者).
This is the **epidemic *qi*** (时行之气),
commonly called **Great Epidemic** (大#10行).
Each variety causes distinct suffering (其所苦各不同).
The symptoms and afflictions differ accordingly (其证候所苦害不同).

The pulse of **stroke** (*zhòngfēng*) is characterized by a floating and slippery *yang* pulse, while the *yin* pulse is damp and weak. On the surface of the muscles, where the *yang* pulse travels, a light touch reveals a pulse that seems excessive. Beneath the muscles, where the *yin* pulse travels, pressing yields a deficiency—this is called weakness. Thus, when pressing yields deficiency but lifting reveals excess, one knows it is **stroke**.

The pulse of **damp-warm illness** (*shīwēn*) is damp and weak in *yang*, while the *yin* pulse is small and tense. On the surface of the muscles, where the *yang* pulse travels, dampness and weakness indicate that damp *qi* overcomes fire. Beneath the muscles, where the *yin* pulse travels, smallness and tension signify that earthly dampness cannot overcome wood.

The pulse of **cold damage** (*shānghán*) is marked by both *yin* and *yang* pulses being excessively full and tense. The *cùn* and *chǐ* pulses are both at their peak, tense and rough.

The pulse of **heat illness** (*rèbìng*) is floating in both *yin* and *yang*. The pulse of heat illness, whether at the *cùn* or *chǐ*, is floating. When floating and slippery—light pressure reveals slipperiness—this indicates that the heart is injured by heat *qi*. When pressed deeply and dispersed with roughness—heavy pressure reveals dispersion and roughness—this indicates depletion of body fluids.

The pulse of **warm illness** (*wēnbìng*) travels through all the channels, and it is unclear which channel is affected. One must follow each channel’s symptoms to diagnose it. Warm illness arises from **epidemic *qi*** (*guǐlì zhīqì*) dispersing through the channels unpredictably; it cannot be foreseen which channel is affected. Only by observing the symptoms and diagnosing accordingly can one determine the appropriate treatment.

In **cold damage**, there are cases where sweating leads to recovery, while purgation leads to death, and cases where sweating leads to death, while purgation leads to recovery. Why is this so?

When *yang* is deficient and *yin* is excessive, sweating leads to recovery, but purgation leads to death. *Yang* deficiency causes external cold, while *yin* excess also causes external cold. Cold toxins contend within the *róngwèi*, causing alternating fever and aversion to cold. The *cùn* and *chǐ* pulses are both large and floating, yet internally, there is no restlessness. If there is slight agitation, the patient prefers warm food but dislikes cold. This indicates *yang* deficiency and *yin* excess—sweating will heal, but mistaken purgation will kill.

When *yang* is excessive and *yin* is deficient, sweating leads to death, but purgation leads to recovery. *Yang* excess causes internal heat, while *yin* deficiency also causes internal heat. Cold toxins clash within the *róngwèi*, with *yang* excess and *yin* decline. Extreme *yin* transforms into *yang*, cold begets heat, and the heat *qi* surges inward, poisoning the stomach. Body fluids dry up, and dry feces accumulate. The patient does not dislike cold externally but instead feels a steaming, dry fever, and in severe cases, delirium. Purgation will heal, but mistaken sweating will kill.

How does one diagnose **cold-heat illnesses**?

If the **skin** is affected by cold and heat: The lungs govern the skin and hair, and the large intestine is its paired organ. If the *zàng* (solid organ) is diseased, it manifests as cold; if the *fǔ* (hollow organ) is diseased, it manifests as heat. The skin cannot tolerate proximity to warmth. The hands’ three *yin* and three *yang* channels follow the movements of heaven—when heaven is active, disease arises, and the patient avoids warmth. If the hair is scorched and the nose is withered, this indicates internal heart fire causing dry heat. **Do not induce sweating**—if you do, death will follow. Purgation will heal.

If the **muscles** are affected by cold and heat: The spleen governs the muscles, and the stomach is its paired organ. Disease in the *zàng* manifests as cold, while disease in the *fǔ* manifests as heat. The skin and muscles ache. The spleen governs earth, which governs dampness, and dampness flows into the joints, causing pain. The lips and tongue wither. The spleen’s essence resides in the four whites of the lips; when its fluids leak outward, they wither. There is no sweating. This disease arises from dry dampness—**do not induce sweating**, as it will obstruct the intestines and stomach. Purgation will cause diarrhea; instead, warm the center and regulate the *qi*.

If the **bones** are affected by cold and heat: The kidneys govern the bones, and the bladder is its paired organ. If the disease is in *yang*, the body is hot, with heavy aversion to cold; if in *yin*, it is cold. The patient finds no comfort in any position.

---

Sweating ceaselessly and pouring forth. The kidneys generate fluids, which enter the heart and become sweat. *Zhù* (注) means without restraint—sweat flows endlessly without pause. The teeth wither and ache. The teeth are the remnants of bone, and when the kidneys’ fluids leak outward, the roots of the teeth wither and dry, causing pain.

In the *Five Thousand Nine Difficulties* (五十九难), it is asked: How does one distinguish between *madness* (狂) and *epilepsy* (癫)?

Madness and epilepsy are inherently two distinct afflictions—how does one differentiate them? Yet:

At the onset of madness (狂之始发), it is marked by excess *yang* (重阳). Madness arises when *yang* is excessive, manifesting when the disease is in the three *yang* channels and sweat is induced inappropriately—*yang* overabundance gives rise to madness. The patient does not hunger externally (少外不饥)—they have no desire for food or nourishment from the outside world. They do not wish to sleep externally (不欲眠外)—they remain unaware of hunger or fatigue. Yet they claim their own virtue and wisdom (而自高贤也, 自辩智也)—delusively boasting of their greatness and intelligence, though their actions betray no true clarity. They act haughty and disdainful (自贵倨也)—arrogantly asserting their superiority, indulging in pride, and resenting others. They laugh wildly (妄笑), delighting in music and song (好歌乐也)—unprovoked mirth and an obsession with singing, as if it brings them joy. Their unrestrained behavior continues endlessly (妄行不休是也)—climbing heights, scaling walls, and venturing into places far beyond their ordinary capacity, with no pause in their erratic movements.

At the onset of epilepsy (癫疾始发), *癫* (epilepsy) is a form of *preten* (颠, vertigo). It begins when *yin* is excessive (重阴者). The patient’s mind is discontented (意不乐)—their spirit finds no peace.

「Directly gazing like a stiff corpse—when the eyes stare fixedly, yet the patient cannot walk or stand and instead collapses. This occurs when the pulses of all three *yin-yang* regions are both vigorous. The *cùnkǒu* pulse (寸口) belongs to the *yang* region, the *chǐ* pulse (尺) to the *yin* region, and all three pulses are strong.

The *Sixiù Nán* (六十难) states: *"Head and heart diseases"*—there are *jué* headaches (厥痛), true headaches (真痛), *jué* heart pains (厥心痛), and true heart pains (真心痛). What does this mean?

*Jué* (厥) is *líng* (玲, a sudden onset of disorder). When the three *yang* pulses of the hand (手三阳之脉) are affected by wind and cold, they stagnate and fail to flow, this is called *jué* headache. The disruptive *líng* (playing, disorderly) *qi* of wind invades the three *yang* channels, stagnates, and fails to flow, causing obstruction and a sudden surge into the head—thus it is called *jué* headache.

If the disorder follows the *lián* (连) channel into the brain (脑), it is called true headache. The brain is the *suǐhǎi* (髓海, marrow sea), and when wind and *líng* *qi* enter the *níjiǔ guān* (泥九官, muddy nine orifices), it means the *xie* (邪, pathogenic) *qi* follows the *fēngfǔ* (风府) channel into the brain, causing severe pain. When *líng* (playing) reaches the elbows and knees of the hands and feet (手足玲至肘膝), this also results in true headache.

When the *qi* of the five *zàng* (藏, viscera) interacts with one another, it is called *jué* heart pain. All channels belong to the heart (心), and if one channel is diseased, its *qi* flows in reverse, and when it reverses, it assaults the heart, causing heart pain—hence it is called *jué* heart pain. This is when the *qi* of the five *zàng* surges in reverse, causing pain, not the heart itself generating pain. When the pain is severe and the hands and feet turn blue (手足青), it is called true heart pain.

True heart pain is said to strike once in the morning and kill by evening, or strike in the evening and kill by morning. See further in the *Shíliù Nán* (十六难).

**Divine Insight and Skillful Mastery (神圣工巧, Shénshèng Gōngqiǎo) – Difficulty #61**

The *Sixiùyī Nán* (六十一难) states: *"The Classics say 'to know by looking' is called divine insight."* To diagnose a disease by merely observing the patient is called divine insight.

*"To know by hearing" is called sagacity.* To diagnose a disease by listening to the patient is called sagacity.

*"To know by questioning" is called skilled mastery.* Before diagnosis, to ask questions first is the most reliable method; to diagnose a disease through questioning is called skilled mastery.

*"To know by pulse-taking" is called artful precision.* To diagnose by observing the patient’s breathing (息) during pulse examination is called artful precision.

What does this mean? How do the four methods of knowing work?»

---

**Divine Insight and Skillful Mastery (神圣工巧, Shénshèng Gōngqiǎo) – Difficulty #16**

Now, *"to know by looking"* means observing the patient’s five colors (五色, *wǔsè*) to discern their illness. If one sees green (青色, *qīngsè*), it indicates a liver disorder (肝病, *gānbìng*); if one sees red (赤色, *chìsè*), it suggests the heart (心, *xīn*) has overpowered the liver, yet the liver is still afflicted. Thus, by observing the five colors, one may know the disease.

*"To know by hearing"* means listening to the patient’s five tones (五音, *wǔyīn*)—*guān, shāng, jiǎo, zhǐng, yǔ*—to distinguish their ailment. These five tones correspond to the five *Zàng* (藏, visceral organs). If a patient frequently weeps (好哭, *hàokū*), it is a sign of lung disease (肺病, *fèibìng*); if they enjoy singing (好歌, *hàogē*), it suggests spleen disorder (脾病, *píbìng*).

*"To know by questioning"* means inquiring about the patient’s cravings for the five flavors (五味, *wǔwèi*) to determine the source and location of their illness. When asked, a patient who prefers spicy (辛, *xīn*) flavors likely suffers from lung disease; one who favors sour (酸, *suān*) flavors, liver disease; one who craves sweet (甜, *tián*), spleen disease; one who prefers bitter (苦, *kǔ*), heart disease; and one who favors salty (咸, *xián*), kidney disease (肾病, *shènbìng*). Those who consume cold, raw foods (玲物, *língwù*) often have internal heat (内热, *nèirè*).

*"To know by pulse-taking"* means diagnosing by examining the *cùnkǒu* (寸口, radial pulse) and observing its *xūshí* (虚实, emptiness/fullness) to determine the illness and which *Zàng* (藏) or *Fǔ* (腑, hollow organs) it affects. If the pulse is *túnxī* (豚息, slack and faint) or *xián* (弦, taut), it indicates liver disorder (肝, *gān*). If a light touch reveals a slack or taut pulse, the illness lies in the hollow organs (府, *fǔ*). If a firm touch makes the pulse taut and the patient’s voice sounds hollow, the illness is in the visceral organs (藏, *zàng*). This is the meaning of *"to know by pulse-taking."*

The Classics say *"to know from outward signs"* is called *sagacity* (圣, *Shèng*). Observing the pulse (视脉, *shìmài*), initial pulse (初脉, *chūmài*), and pulse-taking (切脉, *qiēmài*) all involve knowing the illness from outward signs. *"To know from inward signs"* is called *divine insight* (神, *Shén*). This is its meaning: to perceive the illness in the mind (於心, *yúxīn*) is inward knowledge, hence called *divine insight*.

**Difficulty #61 (六十一难, *Liùshíyī Nán*) – The Five and Six (藏井荣有五，腑独有六, *Zàng Jǐng Róng Yǒu Wǔ, Fǔ Dú Yǒu Liù*)**
The *Sixiùyī Nán* (六十一难) asks: *"The visceral organs have five *jǐng* (井, wells) and five *róng* (荣, branches), while the hollow organs have six—why is this?"*

The five *Zàng* (藏) share five categories: *jǐng* (井, wells), *yù* (俞, streams), *róng* (荣, branches), *jīng* (经, channels), and *hé* (合, junctions). The six hollow organs (六府, *Liùfǔ*), however, have these five categories in addition to another point called *yuán* (原, source). How is this?

Now, the hollow organs (府, *Fǔ*) are of *Yáng* (阳, Yang) nature. The six hollow organs belong to *Yáng*. The *Sānjiāo* (三焦, Triple Burner) traverses all *Yáng* pathways, hence it is assigned a *yù* (俞) point named *yuán* (原). The *Sānjiāo* (三焦) is a ministerial organ (臣使之官, *chénshǐzhīguān*) aligned with the *Xiānghuǒ* (相火, Ministerial Fire), which carries out the commands of the *Jūnhuǒ* (君火, Sovereign Fire) across all *Yáng* channels (经, *jīng*). Thus, it is assigned a *yù* point named *yuán*.

*Yuán* (原) means *origin* (元, *yuán*). The six hollow organs share one *qi* (气) with the *Sānjiāo*, all being *Yáng* in nature, hence they are said to share one *qi*.

**Difficulty #62 (六十二难, *Liùshíèr Nán*) – The Ten Transformations (十变, *Shí Biàn*)**
The *Shí Biàn* (十变) states: *"The visceral organs, hollow organs, branches, and junctions all begin with the wells (井, *jǐng*)—why is this?"*

The five *Zàng* (藏) and six hollow organs (六府, *Liùfǔ*), with their wells (井, *jǐng*), branches (荣, *róng*), streams (俞, *yù*), channels (经, *jīng*), and junctions (合, *hé*), all trace their origins to the wells. Why is this?

Now, the wells (井, *jǐng*) represent the *east* (东方, *Dōngfāng*) and *spring* (春, *chūn*). Spring is *rén* (仁, benevolence), the greatest of the five *Cáng* (常, constants). In the yearly cycle, spring is the beginning (首, *shǒu*); in the monthly cycle, the *jiǎ* (甲) stem is the beginning. In the channel pathways, the wells are the beginning. All things are born in spring: plants sprout, insects stir, and creatures awaken. Thus, the *qíchóng* (岐虫, forked insects) move, and the *chuǎnchóng* (喘虫, panting insects) breathe. The *miánchóng* (绢虫, silkworm-like insects) and *rúchóng* (蠕虫, wriggling insects) also begin their activity in spring.

All living things that should be born arise from spring *qi* (气). Hence, the yearly count begins with spring: the first month (正月, *Zhèngyuè*) is the start of the year, so spring holds the beginning of the year. The monthly count begins with *jiǎ* (甲). The east belongs to *jiǎ* and *yǐ* (乙), both wood stems (木, *mù*), and the first month is associated with *jiǎ* and *yǐ*, both belonging to spring. Thus, the start of the twelve months lies in *jiǎ*. Therefore, the wells are the origin.

Only because of the year and month does one know that the wells are the beginning.

**Difficulty #63 (六十三难, *Liùshísān Nán*) – Yin and Yang Wells (阴井木，阳井金, *Yīn Jǐng Mù, Yáng Jǐng Jīn*)**
The *Shí Biàn* (十变) states: *"Yin wells are wood (木), Yang wells are metal (金)."*

The wells (井, *jǐng*) are like mountain valleys (谷井, *gǔjǐng*), not man-made pits. They are the places where water first emerges from the earth in mountain valleys. The same wells (井) are wood (木) in *Yáng* (阳) and metal (金) in *Yīn* (阴).

Yin branches (荣, *róng*) are fire (火), Yang branches are water (水). After water emerges from the wells, it lingers near the surface, and before it forms a great flow (大流, *dàliú*), it is called branches (荣). The branches (荣) are the state of small waters (小水, *xiǎoshuǐ*). The same branches (荣) are water (水) in *Yáng* (阳) and fire (火) in *Yīn* (阴).

Yin streams (俞, *yù*) are earth (土), Yang streams are wood (木). When the water lingers deeply, it reaches points where it can be directed to nourish the body, and these are called streams (俞). The same streams (俞) are earth (土) in *Yīn* (阴) and wood (木) in *Yáng* (阳).

Yin channels (经, *jīng*) are metal (金), Yang channels are fire (火). The channels (经) are pathways (径, *jìng*), the meaning of *jīngyíng* (经营, circulation and nourishment). As water accumulates and flows, it forms these channels. The same channels (经) are metal (金) in *Yīn* (阴) and fire (火) in *Yáng* (阳).

Yin junctions (合, *hé*) are water (水), Yang junctions are earth (土). The junctions (合) are convergences (会, *huì*), where lingering water reaches points that direct it to nourish the body. The same junctions (合) are water (水) in *Yīn* (阴) and earth (土) in *Yáng* (阳).

Why do *Yīn* (阴) and *Yáng* (阳) differ so greatly? The meaning is as follows:

This concerns the principles of *jiāng* (刚, firmness) and *róu* (柔, softness). The five *Zàng* (藏) are *Yīn* (阴) and *róu* (柔), while the six hollow organs (六府, *Liùfǔ*) are *Yáng* (阳) and *tiáo* (调, harmonious). *Yáng* alone cannot generate, nor can *Yīn* alone flourish; only when *Yáng* and *Yīn* interact as husband and wife (夫妇, *fūfù*) do they complete each other.

Yin wells are *yǐ* (乙) wood, Yang wells are *gēng* (庚) metal. *Yǐ* is *Yīn* (阴), belonging to wood (木); *gēng* is *Yáng* (阳), belonging to metal (金). Whatever is conquered (克, *kè*) is considered the wife (妻), so Yin wells of *yǐ* (乙) wood are paired with Yang wells of *gēng* (gēng) metal. *Yǐ* and *gēng* metal uphold the principle of husband and wife.

Yang wells of *gēng* (庚) are the firm (刚) aspect of *yǐ* (乙): *gēng* is firm, the husband, and *Yáng* (阳), hence *gēng* is the husband of *yǐ*. Yin wells of *yǐ* (乙) are the soft (柔) aspect of *gēng* (庚): *yǐ* is soft, the wife, and *Yīn* (阴), hence *yǐ* is the wife of *gēng*.

*Yǐ* (乙) is wood (木), hence it is called Yin wells of wood (阴井木). The east (东方, *Dōngfāng*) belongs to *jiǎ* (甲) and *yǐ* (乙), both small in the *Cáng* (常, constants). *Yǐ* is *Yīn* (阴), hence it is wood (木).

「*Gēng* (庚) is metal (金), hence it is called Yang wells of metal (阳井金).
The west (西方) belongs to *gēng* (庚) and *xīn* (辛), both metal (金), with *gēng* being Yang (阳). Metal conquers wood (金克木), hence Yang wells of metal (阳井金).
The same principle applies here.

Yin *róng* (荣) belongs to fire (火), Yang *róng* (荣) belongs to water (水). *Rén* (壬) water is firm (刚), *dīng* (丁) fire is soft (柔), and *dīng* (丁) and *rén* (壬) are paired.

Yang *yù* (俞) belongs to wood (木), Yin *yù* (俞) belongs to earth (土). *Jiǎ* (甲) wood is firm (刚), *jǐ* (己) wood is soft (柔), and *jiǎ* (甲) and *jǐ* (己) are paired. Yin *jīng* (经) belongs to metal (金), Yang *jīng* (经) belongs to fire (火). *Bǐng* (丙) fire is firm (刚), *xīn* (辛) metal is soft (柔), and *bǐng* (丙) and *xīn* (辛) are paired.

Yin *hé* (合) belongs to water (水), Yang *hé* (合) belongs to earth (土). *Wù* (戊) earth is firm (刚), *guǐ* (癸) water is soft (柔), and *wù* (戊) and *guǐ* (癸) are paired.

The sixty-fifth difficulty states:
The *jīng* (经) speaks of what is emitted as *jǐng* (井), and what is received as *hé* (合). How does this principle work?
The *jīng* (经) of the *tún* (豚) speaks of what is emitted as *jǐng* (井), and what is received as *hé* (合). How does this principle proceed?
Thus: what is emitted is *jǐng* (井).
Human Yang *qi* (阳气) follows the four seasons in its ebb and flow, and *jǐng* (井) is the time when Yang *qi* (阳气) first emerges.
*Jǐng* (井) is the east (东方) and spring (春). The east is the beginning of the four directions, spring is the beginning of the four seasons, and *jǐng* (井) is the beginning of the *jīng* (经) waters. Hence, *jǐng* (井) is the east and spring.
All things begin to grow, hence it is said that what is emitted is *jǐng* (井).
All things begin to grow in spring, hence it is said that the *jīng* (经) waters are emitted as *jǐng* (井).

What is received is *hé* (合).
*Hé* (合) is where the *jīng* (经) waters converge, and Yang *qi* (阳气) is hidden and stored similarly.
*Hé* (合) is the north (北方) and winter (冬). The north is the end of the four directions, winter is the end of the four seasons, and *hé* (合) is the end of the *jīng* (经) waters. Hence, *hé* (合) is the north and winter.
Yang *qi* (阳气) is hidden and stored. When Yang *qi* (阳气) reaches the winter solstice and retreats to the north, it enters and is concealed.
Hence, it is said that what is received is *hé* (合).
Hence, it is said that the *jīng* (经) waters are received as *hé* (合).

The sixty-sixth difficulty states:
The *jīng* (经) speaks of the origin (原) of the lung (肺) as *Tàiyuān* (太渊).
*Tàiyuān* (太渊) is located below the *yújì* (鱼际) of the right hand, where the pulse converges greatly, and the *tài yīn* (太阴) *tún* (豚) moves.

The origin (原) of the heart (心) is *Tàilíng* (太陵).
It is located in the depression between the two tendons behind the palm, which is the origin of the heart’s pericardial sac (心包络).

The origin (原) of the liver (肝) is *Tàichōng* (太冲).
It is located two *cùn* (寸) behind the base joint of the big toe on the foot.

The origin (原) of the spleen (脾) is *Tàibái* (太白).
It is located below the *húgǔ* (核骨) on the inner side of the foot.

The origin (原) of the kidney (肾) is *Tàixī* (太溪).
It is located behind the root bone of the inner ankle of the foot.

The origin (原) of the lesser Yin (少阴) is *Duìgǔ* (兑骨).
The five *zàng* (藏) all have *shùyù* (俞) as their origins (原).
Lesser Yin (少阴), the *zhēntún* (真豚) of the heart (心), also has an origin (原), located in the depression at the end of the *duìgǔ* (兑骨) behind the palm, also named *Shéndōng* (神门) and *Zhōngdū* (中都).
Earlier, it was said that the origin of the heart (心) is *Tàilíng* (太陵), which refers to the *tún* (豚) of the pericardial sac (心包络).
When it is said that heart diseases (心病) occur, they all pertain to the *tún* (豚) of the pericardial sac (心包络). The true heart (真心) does not fall ill, hence there is no *shùyù* (俞) for it.
Now, there is an origin (原) for lesser Yin (少阴), which is an external *jīng* (经) disease, not treating the internal *zàng* (藏).

The origin (原) of the gallbladder (胆) is *Qiūxū* (丘虚).
It is located slightly anterior to the outer ankle of the foot.

The origin (原) of the stomach (胃) is *Chōngyáng* (冲阳).
It is located five *cùn* (寸) above the ankle on the foot, between the bones where the pulse moves.

The origin (原) of the triple burner (三焦) is *Yángchí* (阳池).
It is located in the depression behind the base joint of the ring finger on the hand.

The origin (原) of the bladder (膀胱) is *Jīnggǔ* (京骨).
It is located below the large bone on the outer side of the foot, at the junction of red and white flesh.

The origin (原) of the large intestine (大肠) is *Hégǔ* (合谷).
It is located between the thumb and index finger, where the *hú* (虎) pulse moves.

The origin (原) of the small intestine (小肠) is *Wàngǔ* (腕骨).
It is located on the inner side of the wrist bone of the little finger.

Why are the origins (原) of the twelve *jīng* (经) all referred to as *shùyù* (俞)?
*Shùyù* (俞) refers to the *jǐng* (井), *róng* (荣), *shù* (俞), *jīng* (经), and *hé* (合) points, not other *shùyù* (俞) points.
How are the origins (原) of the twelve *jīng* (经) all referred to as *shùyù* (俞)?
Thus: the *shùyù* (俞) points of the five *zàng* (藏) are the pathways (所行) of the triple burner (三焦) and where the *qi* (气) flows and stops.
The triple burner (三焦) communicates through these *shùyù* (俞) points and also flows and stops here.
Thus, the *qi* (气) that moves between the navel and kidney (脐下肾问动气) is also involved.
See annotation in the eighth difficulty.

The kidney (肾) is the foundation of human life (人之生命) and vitality (性命).
The kidney (肾) is the root foundation of the twelve *jīng* (经).
See annotation in the eighth difficulty.»

Thus, it is called *yuán* (原).
The triple burner (三焦) converges its *qi* (气) at the kidney (肾), hence it is called *yuán* (原).
The triple burner (三焦) is the separate envoy of the *yuánqi* (原气).
See annotation in the thirty-eighth difficulty.
It governs the circulation of the three *qi* (三气).
The human triple burner (二#35焦) follows the three *yuán* (元) *qi* of Heaven and Earth, thus circulating the three *qi* (三气).
It traverses the five *zàng* (藏) and six *fǔ* (府).
The upper burner (上焦) lies between the heart and lungs, the middle burner (中焦) between the spleen and stomach, and the lower burner (下焦) between the liver and kidneys, perpetually circulating.
*Yuán* (原) is the exalted title of the triple burner (三焦).
*Yuán* (原) means *yuán* (元).
*Yuánqi* (元气) is the *qi* of the triple burner (三焦).
Its *qi* is exalted and great, hence *yuán* (原) is its exalted title.
Thus, wherever it stops, it is called *yuán* (原).
Wherever the *qi* lingers and halts, that is called *yuán* (原).
When the five *zàng* (藏) and six *fǔ* (府) fall ill, one must treat their *yuán* (原).
When the *zàng* (藏) and *fǔ* (府) are diseased, one must treat their *yuán* (原), hence the cure.
The sixty-seventh difficulty states:
The *mù* (募) points of the five *zàng* (藏) are all on the *yīn* (阴).
The abdomen is *yīn* (阴), and the *mù* (募) points of the five *zàng* (藏) are all within it.
The *mù* (募) of the lungs (肺) are the two *Zhōngfǔ* (中府) points, located one *cùn* (寸) below *Yúnmén* (云门) and three ribs above the nipple.
The *mù* (募) of the heart (心) are the two *Jùquè* (巨阙) points, located one *cùn* (寸) below *Jiūwěi* (鸠尾).
The *mù* (募) of the spleen (脾) are the two *Zhāngmén* (章门) points, directly below the lower ribs, aligned with the navel.
The *mù* (募) of the liver (肝) are the two *Qīmén* (期门) points, one *cùn* and five *fēn* (分) from either side of *Bùróng* (不容).
The *mù* (募) of the kidneys (肾) are the two *Jīngmén* (京门) points, located in the lumbar region, below the lower ribs.
Yet the *shù* (俞) points are all on the *yáng* (阳).
The back is *yáng* (阳), and the *shù* (俞) points of the five *zàng* (藏) are all on it.
The *Fèishù* (肺俞) point is located below the third vertebra, one *cùn* and five *fēn* (分) from either side.
The *Xīnshù* (心俞) point is located below the fifth vertebra, one *cùn* and five *fēn* (分) from the midline.
The *Gānshù* (肝俞) point is located below the ninth vertebra, one *cùn* and five *fēn* (分) from either side.
The *Píshù* (脾俞) point is located below the eleventh vertebra, one *cùn* and five *fēn* (分) from either side.
The *Shènshù* (肾#38俞) point is located below the fourteenth vertebra, one *cùn* and five *fēn* (分) from either side.
How is this so?
Why are the *mù* (募) on *yīn* (阴) and the *shù* (俞) on *yáng* (阳)?

Thus:

The *yīn* (阴) diseases manifest in the *yáng* (阳), while the *yáng* (阳) diseases manifest in the *yīn* (阴).
When the internal *zàng* (藏) are diseased, the ailment manifests externally in the *yáng* (阳)—for the *shù* (俞) points lie on the back.
When the external body is diseased, the ailment descends into the *yīn* (阴)—for the *mù* (募) points lie in the abdomen.
Hence, the *mù* (募) points are on the *yīn* (阴), and the *shù* (俞) points are on the *yáng* (阳).

The *Lùshíbāshì Nán* (六十八难) states:
Each of the five *zàng* (藏) and six *fǔ* (府) has its own *jǐng* (井), *róng* (荣), *shù* (俞), *jīng* (经), and *hé* (合) points.

For the **Liver**:
- *Jǐng* (井): *Dàdūn* (大敦)
- *Róng* (荣): *Xíngwèn* (行间)
- *Shù* (俞): *Dàchōng* (大冲)
- *Jīng* (经): *Zhōngfēng* (中封)
- *Hé* (合): *Qūquán* (曲泉)

For the **Lung**:
- *Jǐng* (井): *Shàoshāng* (少商)
- *Róng* (荣): *Yújì* (鱼际)
- *Shù* (俞): *Dàyuān* (大渊)
- *Jīng* (经): *Jīngqú* (经渠)
- *Hé* (合): *Chǐzé* (尺泽)

For the **Heart**:
- *Jǐng* (井): *Shàochōng* (少冲)
- *Róng* (荣): *Shàofǔ* (少府)
- *Shù* (俞): *Shénd mén* (神门)
- *Jīng* (经): *Língdào* (灵道)
- *Hé* (合): *Shàohǎi* (少海)

For the **Kidney**:
- *Jǐng* (井): *Yǒngquán* (涌泉)
- *Róng* (荣): *Lìgǔ* (丽谷)
- *Shù* (俞): *Dàxī* (大溪)
- *Jīng* (经): *Fùliú* (复溜)
- *Hé* (合): *Liúgǔ* (溜谷)

For the **Spleen**:
- *Jǐng* (井): *Yǐnbái* (隐白)
- *Róng* (荣): *Dàtū* (大都)
- *Shù* (俞): *Dàbái* (大白)
- *Jīng* (经): *Shāngqiū* (商丘)
- *Hé* (合): *Língyīnquán* (陵阴泉)

For the **Pericardium**:
- *Jǐng* (井): *Zhōngchōng* (中冲)
- *Róng* (荣): *Láoguān* (劳官)
- *Shù* (俞): *Dàlíng* (大陵)
- *Jīng* (经): *Wènshǐ* (问使)
- *Hé* (合): *Qūzé* (曲泽)

These are the *jǐng* (井), *róng* (荣), *shù* (俞), *jīng* (经), and *hé* (合) points of the five *zàng* (藏).

For the **Gallbladder**:
- *Jǐng* (井): *Qiàoyīn* (窍阴)
- *Róng* (荣): *Xiákē* (侠溪)
- *Shù* (俞): *Línqì* (临泣)
- *Jīng* (经): *Yángfǔ* (阳辅)
- *Hé* (合): *Yánglíngquán* (阳陵泉), *Yuán* (原): *Guānqiūxū* (原丘虚)

For the **Large Intestine**:
- *Jǐng* (井): *Shāngyáng* (商阳)
- *Róng* (荣): *Èrwèn* (二间)
- *Shù* (俞): *Sānwèn* (三间)
- *Jīng* (经): *Yángxī* (阳溪)
- *Hé* (合): *Qūchí* (曲池)
- *Yuán* (原): *Hégǔ* (合谷)

For the **Small Intestine**:
- *Jǐng* (井): *Shàozé* (少泽)
- *Róng* (荣): *Qiángǔ* (前谷)
- *Shù* (俞): *Hòuxī* (后溪)
- *Jīng* (经): *Yánggǔ* (阳谷)
- *Hé* (合): *Xiǎohǎi* (小海)
- *Yuán* (原): *Wàngǔ* (腕骨)

For the **Stomach**:
- *Jǐng* (井): *Lìduì* (厉兑)
- *Róng* (荣): *Nèitíng* (内庭)
- *Shù* (俞): *Xiàngǔ* (陷谷)
- *Jīng* (经): *Jiěxī* (解溪)
- *Hé* (合): *Sānlǐ* (三里)
- *Yuán* (原): *Chōngyáng* (冲阳)

For the **Bladder**:
- *Jǐng* (井): *Zhìyīn* (至阴)
- *Róng* (荣): *Tōnggǔ* (通谷)
- *Shù* (俞): *Shùgǔ* (束骨)
- *Jīng* (经): *Kūnlún* (昆仑)
- *Hé* (合): *Wěizhōng* (委中)
- *Yuán* (原): *Jīnggǔ* (京骨)

For the **Triple Burner**:
- *Jǐng* (井): *Guānchōng* (关冲)
- *Róng* (荣): *Yèmén* (液门)
- *Shù* (俞): *Zhōngdū* (中都)
- *Jīng* (经): *Zhīgōu* (支沟)
- *Hé* (合): *Tiānjǐng* (天井)
- *Yuán* (原): *Yángchí* (阳池)

These are the *jǐng* (井), *róng* (荣), *shù* (俞), *jīng* (经), *hé* (合), and *yuán* (原) points of the six *fǔ* (府).

What do they govern?

The *jǐng* (井), *róng* (荣), *shù* (俞), *jīng* (经), and *hé* (合) each govern specific ailments.

Thus:
The *jǐng* (井) is where the meridian originates—like a spring welling up in a valley.
The *róng* (荣) is where it flows—like a stream that has yet to become a great river.
The *shù* (俞) is where it converges—where the water gathers deeply, allowing for drainage.
The *jīng* (经) is where it courses—forming channels through which it flows.
The *hé* (合) is where it enters—where the channels converge into the sea.

The *jǐng* (井) governs **distension beneath the heart**.
The *jǐng* (井) follows the principle of Wood (肝), corresponding to the Liver.
When the Liver is diseased, it oppresses the Spleen (Earth), causing distension beneath the heart.
Treating the *jǐng* (井) prevents Wood from overacting upon Earth.

The *róng* (荣) governs **febrile body**.
The *róng* (荣) follows the principle of Fire (心), corresponding to the Heart.
The Lung belongs to Metal, externally governing skin and fur.
When Heart-Fire scorches the Lung-Metal, the body becomes febrile—indicating that the disease lies in the Heart.
Treating the *róng* (荣) prevents Fire from overacting upon Metal, thus alleviating the fever.

The *shù* (俞) governs **heaviness in the body and joint pain**.
The *shù* (俞) follows the principle of Earth (脾), corresponding to the Spleen.
When the Spleen is diseased, it necessarily oppresses Water (肾), and Water governs the bones.
Thus, the disease manifests as joint pain.
When the Spleen itself is diseased, the body feels heavy—hence, treatment should target the *shù* (俞) points.

The *jīng* (经) governs **wheezing, coughing, and alternating cold and heat**.
The *jīng* (经) follows the principle of Metal (肺), corresponding to the Lung.
When the disease lies in the *jīng* (经), the Lung’s ailments manifest—cold induces coughing, heat induces wheezing.
When the disease lies in Metal, it oppresses Wood (肝), and Wood in the emotions is anger.
Anger causes *qi* reversal, hence the wheezing.
Why so?
The Liver’s branch separates and pierces the diaphragm—Metal does not oppress Wood.

The *hé* (合) governs **inverted *qi* and diarrhea**.
The *hé* (合) follows the principle of Water (肾), corresponding to the Kidney.
When Kidney-*qi* is deficient, it injures the *Chōngmài* (冲脉), causing *qi* inversion and diarrhea.
The Kidney governs the orifices of the two *yīn* (阴) regions.
When Kidney-*qi* is unchecked, diarrhea ensues.
When the disease lies in Water, Water must support Fire (心).
The Heart, by principle, should not receive evil.
The Liver-Wood is the mother of Heart-Fire and the child of Kidney-Water.
There are two concerns: first, that the mother (Liver) receives evil, and second, that the child (Liver) is oppressed.
The Liver in the emotions is anger, and worry induces anger.
Anger causes *qi* inversion.
Treating the *hé* (合) prevents Water from overacting upon Fire, thus stopping *qi* inversion.
If the disease is not in the Kidney, there will be no diarrhea.

These are the diseases governed by the *jǐng* (井), *róng* (荣), *shù* (俞), *jīng* (经), and *hé* (合) points of the five *zàng* (藏) and six *fǔ* (府).

The generation of diseases through the *jǐng* (井), *róng* (荣), *shù* (俞), *jīng* (经), and *hé* (合) points should be adjusted according to the four seasons.
This means that the seasonal evils lodge and remain in the *róng* (荣) and *shù* (俞) points.

**On Needling Techniques: Supplementation and Drainage (第十二#52)**

The *Lùshíliùshíjiǔ Nán* (六十九难) states:
The Classics say: *"Supplement the deficient."* For deficient or weak *zàng* (藏) and *fǔ* (府), supplementation is used.
*"Drain the replete."* For overfilled or excessive *zàng* (藏) and *fǔ* (府), drainage is applied.
*"If neither deficient nor replete, follow the channel."* When none of the *zàng* (藏) are overacting and there is no imbalance of deficiency or excess, one should merely follow the channel’s sequence in applying supplementation or drainage.

Why so? How does this work?

Thus:
For the deficient, supplement the **mother**.
In spring, if the Kidney pulse is deficient, it indicates Kidney deficiency unable to transmit *qi* to the Liver.
Thus, the Kidney is supplemented—since the Kidney is the mother of the Liver, it is said, *"Supplement the mother."*

For the replete, drain the **child**.
In spring, if the Heart pulse is replete, it indicates excessive Heart-*qi* overacting upon the Liver.
Thus, the Heart is drained—since the Heart is the child of the Liver, it is said, *"Drain the child."*

First, supplementation should be applied, then drainage.

The **mother** can make the **child** replete—thus, supplementing the **mother** must come first.
The **child** can make the **mother** deficient—thus, draining the **child** must come later.

When neither replete nor deficient, one takes from the **channel itself**—this means the **channel’s own pathology** arises, not an external evil. Thus, one must take from the **channel itself**, hence the saying, *"take from the channel."*

When neither replete nor deficient, the **zàng** do not overact upon one another.

In spring, if the pulse is **tight** or merely **tight**, this is the **Liver zàng’s** own disease, and supplementation or drainage is applied to the **Foot Jueyin-Less Yang channels**.

The **channel’s own** Wood, Fire, Water, Metal, and Earth are taken according to the season.

The **70th Difficulty** says:
The **Classic** states: *"In spring and summer, puncture shallowly. In spring and summer, puncture at the **jing-well** and **ying-spring** [points], from the thin, shallow muscles. In autumn and winter, puncture deeply. In autumn and winter, puncture at the **jing-river** and **he-sea** [points], from the thick, deep muscles."*

What does this mean? How does shallow and deep work?

Thus:
In spring and summer, **yang-qi** is above, and human **qi** is also above—thus, one must take shallowly.
Spring-**qi** resides in the **hair follicles**, summer-**qi** in the **skin**—both **yang-qi** and human **qi** are above, so the needle is used to puncture the shallow **hair follicles** and **skin**.

In autumn and winter, **yang-qi** is below, and human **qi** is also below—thus, one must take deeply.
Autumn-**qi** is in the **flesh**, winter-**qi** in the **sinews and bones**—both **yang-qi** and human **qi** are below, so the needle is used to puncture the deep **flesh**, **sinews**, and **bones**.

In spring and summer, **one must cultivate one yin**.
Spring and summer are **yang**—*cultivate* means *reach* or *attain*. In spring and summer, one cultivates **yang** by attaining one **yin** to nourish it, lest it become **lonely yang**.

In autumn and winter, **one must cultivate one yang**.
Autumn and winter are **yin**—in autumn and winter, one cultivates **yin** by attaining one **yang** to nourish it, lest it become **lonely yin**.

What does this mean? How do the four seasons’ cultivations differ?

Thus:
In spring and summer, warmth requires attaining one **yin**—
Spring and summer diseases manifest in **yang**, so **yin** is drawn to harmonize **yang**.
When first inserting the needle, it is sunk to the **Kidney-Liver** region to obtain **qi**, drawing in **yin** to harmonize it.
Human skin has thick and thin regions—beneath the skin lies the **Liver-Kidney** region, where **yin-qi** flows. Thus, the needle is sunk inward, entering the skin five *cun*, reaching the **Liver-Kidney** region to obtain **qi**, drawing in **yin-qi** to harmonize the **yang**.

In autumn and winter, cold requires attaining one **yang**—
Autumn and winter diseases manifest in **yin**, so **yang** is drawn inward to harmonize **yin**.
When first inserting the needle, it is kept shallow and superficial, reaching the **Heart-Lung** region to obtain **qi**, pushing in **yang** to harmonize it.
Above the skin lies the **Heart-Lung** region—thus, the needle is kept superficial, entering the skin three *cun*, reaching the **Heart-Lung** region to obtain **qi**, pushing the needle inward to draw in **yang-qi** to harmonize the **yin**.

This is called: *"In spring and summer, one must cultivate one yin; in autumn and winter, one must cultivate one yang."*

Returning to the previous text:
The **71st Difficulty** says:

The **Classical** states:
*"Needle the **Nourishing** (*róng*) without harming the **Defensive** (*wèi*).*
When the needle pierces **Nourishing-qi** and **blood**, it must not harm **Defensive-qi**.
When needling the **Defensive**, it must not harm **Nourishing-qi** and **blood**.
What does this mean?
How do these two differ?
Now:
Needling **yang-qi** (*Defensive*) is done externally—thus, the **Defensive** (as **yang**) is needled by entering the skin two *cun*.
If the disease lies in the **Defensive**, the needle is kept shallow, so one lowers the needle to pierce it, lest its depth harm **Nourishing-qi**.

Needling **yin-qi** (*Nourishing*) requires first using the left hand to grasp and press the **acupuncture point** where the **Nourishing** and **Defensive** converge. Once the **qi** disperses, the needle is inserted.
The **Nourishing** is **yin**, so when needling, entering the skin five *cun* reaches **Nourishing-qi**—thus, one first presses the point to await the **qi**’s dispersal before inserting the needle, lest its depth harm **Defensive-qi**.
This is how one needles the **Nourishing** without harming the **Defensive**, and the **Defensive** without harming the **Nourishing**.

The **72nd Difficulty** says:
*"The **Classical** states: ‘If one can know the **qi** of **迎** (*welcoming*) and **随** (*following*), one may order it adjusted.’
**迎** (*welcoming*) is **逆** (*contrary/against*), **取** (*drawing out*).
**随** (*following*) is **顺** (*with/along*), **补** (*supplementing*).
